Calculating Compensation for a Defect (Al-Ruju' bi al-Nuqsan)
Explains the method of calculating arsh (compensation for defect) with examples and procedural rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation for a defect (arsh) using the Hanafi proportional method and explain why it is based on market value rather than purchase price.

Core Idea
Compensation for a defect is proportional to value loss, not price difference.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item but cannot return it because a new defect has occurred, the Hanafi school provides a precise method for compensation. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the calculation: the buyer recovers a fraction of the price equal to the fraction of value lost due to the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario requiring compensation
The buyer discovers an old defect but cannot return the item because they caused a new defect. In this case, the buyer has recourse to compensation (al-rujūʿ bi al-nuqṣān) instead of return.
2
Determine the two valuations
The item must be evaluated (taqwīm) twice: once without the old defect (sound value) and once with the defect (defective value). The difference is the loss in value caused by the defect.
Evaluation requires two expert witnesses (muqawwimān) testifying in the presence of both parties.
3
Calculate the proportional loss
Divide the loss in value by the sound value to get the fraction (e.g., if sound value = 100 and loss = 10, fraction = 1/10).
4
Apply the fraction to the price
Multiply the actual purchase price by the same fraction to determine the compensation amount. The price may differ from the value, but the fraction remains constant.
Example: If price = 10 dirhams and fraction = 1/10, compensation = 1 dirham.
5
Note the distinction from damage evaluation
For defect evaluation, two witnesses are required; for damages (e.g., compensation for loss of use), one evaluator suffices. This distinction is noted in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات اطلع على عيب بالكفن لا يرده ولا يرجع بنقصان العيب إلا إذا أحدث به عيباً فله الرجوع بالنقصان وصورة الرجوع بالنقصان أن يقوم المبيع وليس به عيب قديم ويقوم وبه ذلك فينظر إلى ما نقص من قيمته لأجل النقصان وينسب إلى القيمة السليمة فإن كانت النسبة العشر رجع بعشر الثمن وإن كانت النصف فبنصف الثمن بيانه إذا اشترى ثوباً بعشرة دراهم وقيمته مائة درهم واطلع على عيب ينقصه عشرة دراهم وقد حدث به عيب عنده فإنه يرجع بعشرة الثمن وهو درهم ولو اشتراه بمائتين وقيمته مائة ونقصه العيب عشرة فإنه يرجع بعشر الثمن وذلك عشرون وإن نقصه عشرين رجع بخمس الثمن وهو أربعون وإن اشتراه بمائة وهو يساوي مائة ونقصه عشرة رجع بعشر الثمن وهو عشرة كذا في السراج الوهاج معزياً إلى الينابيع وفي البزازية وفي المقايضة إن النقصان عشر القيمة رجل بعشر ما جعل ثمناً والمقوم لا بد أن يكون اثنين يخبران بلفظ الشهادة بحضرة البائع والمشتري والمقوم الأهل في كل حرفة اهـ ويحتاج إلى الفرق بين التقويم هنا وفي كل موضع فإنهم اكتفوا في تقويم التلفات بتقويم واحد كما في شرح المنظومة
And in al-Waqi'at: He came across a defect in the shroud; he does not return it nor claim compensation for the defect unless he causes a defect in it, then he has recourse to compensation. And the form of recourse to compensation is that the sold item is evaluated without the old defect and evaluated with it. Then one looks at what diminished from its value due to the defect and attributes it to the sound value. If the proportion is one-tenth, he recovers one-tenth of the price; if half, then half of the price. Explanation: If he bought a cloth for ten dirhams while its value is one hundred dirhams, and he discovers a defect that reduces it by ten dirhams, and a defect occurred with him, then he recovers one-tenth of the price, which is one dirham. If he bought it for two hundred while its value is one hundred and the defect reduces it by ten, he recovers one-tenth of the price, which is twenty. And if it reduces it by twenty, he recovers one-fifth of the price, which is forty. And if he bought it for one hundred while it equals one hundred and the defect reduces it by ten, he recovers one-tenth of the price, which is ten. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj attributed to al-Yanabi'. And in al-Bazzaziyya: In muqayada, if the diminution is one-tenth of the value, a man [recovers] one-tenth of what he made the price. And the evaluators must be two, informing with the wording of testimony in the presence of the seller and buyer, and the evaluator is an expert in every craft. End. And there is need for distinction between evaluation here and in every place, for they sufficed in evaluation of damages with one evaluation, as in Sharh al-Manẓuma.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
Ruling: This passage provides the method for calculating compensation (الرجوع بالنقصان) when a new defect has occurred and the buyer cannot return the item. The compensation is based on the proportional loss in market value, not on the purchase price. First evaluate the item without the old defect and with it. The difference in value is the loss. Then calculate that loss as a fraction of the sound value. That same fraction is applied to the actual price paid to determine the compensation amount. Examples illustrate that even if the price differs from the value, the fraction remains constant. The passage also notes that evaluation requires two expert witnesses testifying in the presence of the parties, as opposed to damage evaluation where one evaluator suffices.

Key Terms
الرجوع بالنقصان (al-rujūʿ bi al-nuqṣān): The buyer's claim for compensation equal to the proportion of value loss caused by the defect.
التقويم (al-taqwīm): Assessment of market value by expert witnesses.
النقصان (al-nuqṣān): The reduction in value caused by the defect, measured as the difference between sound value and defective value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a book for 5 dirhams; its sound market value is 10 dirhams. A defect reduces its value by 2 dirhams. What is the correct compensation?
A) 2 dirhams
B) 1 dirham
C) 5 dirhams
D) 0.5 dirham

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for defect compensation applies the proportion of value loss to the actual price, ensuring fairness regardless of the purchase price.

When a New Defect Prevents Return: The Hanafi Dispute on Revival of Khiyar al-Ayb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on revival of the right to return (khiyar al-ayb) after a new defect caused by the buyer disappears.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on a buyer's right to return an item after a new defect disappears and apply the preponderant view to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
A new defect suspends the right to return; once it disappears, the right revives — according to the strongest Hanafi opinion.

When a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after purchase, he generally has the right to return the item. But what if the buyer himself causes a new defect (e.g., cutting the cloth, testing iron in fire)? Does that permanently bar return? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents three Hanafi views on whether the right to return revives after the new defect is removed. It also lists examples of buyer actions that create a new defect and the rulings on compensation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the seller's acceptance scenario
The passage clarifies that when the seller agrees to a return, the buyer has only two options: keep without compensation or return. He does not have a third option to keep and claim compensation.
This corrects a possible misunderstanding from the matn.
2
Identify the three opinions on return after new defect removal
The text quotes three opinions from al-Qunyah: (1) Return is allowed after the new defect disappears; (2) No return ever; (3) Return allowed only if the compensation (taken for the deficiency) is still in hand.
The first opinion is preponderant (mufta bihi).
3
Analyze the proofs for each view
The first opinion argues that the new defect was merely a temporary impediment (man'), so once it goes, the original right returns. The opposing view says the right has 'fallen' (saqit) and does not revive, analogizing to khiyar al-ru'ya.
The author prefers the first due to stronger reasoning.
4
Learn the examples of actions that constitute new defects
The text lists: testing iron in fire, tanning leather, weaving silk, soaking leather, cutting cloth, putting an adze in fire. These actions alter the item or diminish it, barring return.
The buyer may only claim compensation (naqsan) in these cases.
5
Distinguish between actions that permit or prevent return
Sharpening a knife with a stone does not bar return, but using a file does because it removes material. Similarly, gold and silver tested in fire: if melting occurs, it bars return; if only heated, perhaps not.
Key principle: if the action diminishes the item or changes its nature, return is prevented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهر الكتاب أن البائع إذا رضي برده فالخيار للمشتري بين الرد والإمساك والرجوع بالنقصان وليس كذلك بل إذا رضي البائع فإنه يخير إن شاء أمسكه ولا رجوع له بالنقصان وإن شاء رده كما في المعراج وغيره وإذا رجع بالنقصان ثم زال العيب الجديد فله رد المعيب مع النقصان ونقل في القنية فيها أقوالاً ثلاثة الأول ما ذكرناه وقواه بكتاب آخر ثم رقم للثاني بأنه ليس له الرد ثم رقم لثالث بانه مال إلى أنه يرده إن كان بدل النقصان قائماً وإلا فلا اهـ. والذي يظهر ترجيح الأول لأن العيب الحادث كان مانعاً . من بالقديم وقد زال فيعود الرد والقائل بعدمه يقول أن الرد سقط والساقط لا يعود ويشهد له قولهم في خيار الرؤية لو باعه ثم رد عليه بقضاء فإنه لا خيار له لأنه قد سقط فلا يعود ومن العيب الحادث المانع من الرد ما إذا اشترى حديداً ليتخذ منه آلات النجارين وجعله في الكور ليجربه بالنار فوجد به عيباً ولا يصلح لتلك الآلات فإنه يرجع بالنقصان ولا يرده كما في القنية ومنه أيضاً بل الجلود عيب حادث يمنع الرد بقديم وكذا بل الإبريسم منه أيضاً وفي جامع الفصولين بل إبريسما فرأى عيبه يرجع بنقصه وكذا الأديم لو أنقع في الماء فرأى عيبه بائعه ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه فوجد به عيباً رجع بالعيب وإن قبله البائع كذلك فله ذلك وإن لم يرده وإن رضي بائعه وهذا شكل ولو أدخل في النار قدوماً فرأى عيبه لم يرده إذا الحديد ينقص بالنار بخلاف الذهب والفضة كحديد أقول الذهب ينتقص في النار إذا ذاب إيضاً اللهم إلا أن يكون قبل الذوب لو حدد سكيناً فرأى عيبه فإن حدده بحجر فله الرد لا لو حدده بمبرد لأنه ينتقص منه .اهـ.
And apparently the book [says] that if the seller agrees to its return, the buyer has the choice between return, retention, and recourse to compensation. But it is not so; rather, if the seller agrees, he [i.e., the buyer] is given the choice: if he wishes, he retains it and has no recourse to compensation, and if he wishes, he returns it, as in al-Mi'raj and elsewhere. And if he has recourse to compensation and then the new defect disappears, he may return the defective item with the compensation. And it is transmitted in al-Qunyah that there are three opinions: the first is what we mentioned and he supported it with another book; then he numbered the second: he has no return; then he numbered the third: he inclined that he returns it if the substitute for the deficiency remains, otherwise not. End. And apparently the first is preponderant because the new defect was preventing from [return] due to the old, and it has ceased, so the right of return returns. And those who say it does not return say that the right of return has fallen and what falls does not return. And it is attested by their statement in khiyar al-ru'ya: if he sells it and then it is returned to him by a judgment, he has no khiyar because it has fallen and does not return. And of the new defects that prevent return: if he bought iron to make carpenter's tools and put it in the forge to test it with fire and found a defect in it and it is not suitable for those tools, then he has recourse to compensation and does not return it, as in al-Qunyah. And likewise, leather [tanning] is a new defect that prevents return with the old; similarly, silk [weaving] is also from that. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he silk [weaves] and sees its defect, he has recourse to its deficiency. Similarly, if he soaks leather in water and sees its defect. [Page 80] His seller? And whoever buys a cloth and cuts it and finds a defect in it, he has recourse to the defect [compensation]. And if the seller accepts it similarly, he has that [right] even if he does not return it, and if his seller accepts, this is a form. And if he puts an adze in fire and sees its defect, he does not return it because iron diminishes with fire, unlike gold and silver like iron? I say: gold diminishes in fire if it melts as well, unless before melting. If he sharpens a knife and sees its defect, then if he sharpened it with a stone, he has the right to return; not if he sharpened it with a file because it diminishes from it. End.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
Ruling and Dispute: This passage first corrects a misconception from the apparent meaning of the matn (possibly al-Kanz) regarding the seller's acceptance. When the seller accepts the return of a defective item, the buyer does not have three options as might be understood; rather, the buyer can either keep the item with no compensation or return it. If the buyer initially took compensation and later the new defect disappears, the stronger opinion (the first) allows him to return the item with the compensation. This is because the new defect was merely preventing return, and once it is gone, the original right of return revives. The opposing view holds that the right to return is lost forever. The passage then lists examples of buyer actions that create a new defect preventing return: testing iron in a forge, tanning leather, weaving silk, soaking leather, cutting cloth, and putting an adze in fire. These actions change the item's nature or diminish it, so the buyer cannot return the item for the old defect but only claims compensation. Exception: sharpening a knife with a stone does not create a new defect for return purposes, but using a file does because it removes material.

Key Terms
العيب الحادث (al-ʿayb al-ḥādith): A defect occurring after the buyer took possession, caused by his action or a natural event.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ruʾya): The buyer's option to cancel a sale after seeing the item if it does not match description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant Hanafi opinion, if a buyer takes compensation for an old defect and later the new defect (which prevented return) disappears, what can the buyer do?
A) He can only keep the compensation and has no further claim.
B) He may return the item for the old defect along with the compensation already taken.
C) He must return the compensation and then return the item.
D) He cannot return the item under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The preponderant Hanafi view allows the buyer to return an item for an old defect once a new defect caused by him disappears, as the new defect only suspends—not extinguishes—the right of return.

Tree Cutting and Seller's Right After Return
Rulings on buyer's cutting and seller's right after return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer cuts a defective tree or cloth and explain the seller's right to reverse a return upon discovering a new defect.

Core Idea
A new defect caused by the buyer blocks return; the seller may reverse a return if a new defect is found.

This lesson examines two related scenarios from the Hanafi fiqh text. First, a buyer who purchases a tree to make a door, cuts it, and then finds it defective—what are his rights? Second, after a returned item, the seller discovers a defect that occurred while with the buyer—can he reverse the return? Both cases hinge on the principle that a new defect alters the legal options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for the tree-cutting scenario
The buyer cut the tree and found it unsuitable. The ruling: he can claim compensation for the old defect but cannot return the tree unless the seller agrees.
Cutting creates a new defect that prevents return.
2
Analyze the seller's right after return
If the buyer returns an item due to a defect (by court, agreement, or mutual cancellation), and later the seller finds a defect that occurred with the buyer, the seller has the right to return it back to the buyer.
The seller did not consent to the new defect.
3
Apply the same principle to the cloth example
A buyer who cuts a cloth and then finds a defect can only claim compensation for the old defect; the cut is a new defect barring return. If the seller accepts the return, that is allowed because the seller waives his right.
The text says this is repetitive but reinforces the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر قبله شرى شجرة ليتخذ منها باباً أو نحوه فقطعها فوجدها لا تصلح لذلك فله الرجوع بنقص العيب لا الرد إلا برضا بائعه اهـ. وأشار المصنف باشتراط رضا البائع إلى فرع في القنية لو رد المبيع بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء أو تقايلا ثم ظفر البائع بعيب حدث عند المشتري فله الرد .اهـ. ثم اعلم أنا كتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أنه يستثنى من قولهم لو حدث به عیب و به عيب قديم رجع بنقصه أو برضا بائعه مسألتان إحداهما بيع التولية لو باع شيئاً تولية ثم حدث به عيب عند المشتري وبه عيب قديم لا رجوع ولا رد لأنه لو رجع صار الثمن الثاني أنقص من الأول وقضية التولية أن يكون مثل الأول ذكره الشارح في بابها الثانية في السلم لو قبض المسلم فيه فوجد به عيباً كان عند المسلم إليه وحدث به عيب عند رب السلم قال أبو حنيفة خير المسلم إليه إن شاء قبله معيباً بالعيب الحادث وإن شاء لم يقبل ولا شيء عليه لا من رأس المال ولا . من العيب كذا في الخانية من باب السلم وذكره الولوالجي هنا وعلله بأنه لو غرم نقصان العيب من رأس المال كما قال محمد كان اعتياضاً عن الجودة وهو ربا .اهـ. قوله : (ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه فوجد به عيباً فقطعه رجع بالعيب أي بنقصان العيب القديم لأن القطع عيب قوله : (وإن قبله البائع كذلك فله ذلك لأن الامتناع لحقه وقد رضي به وهو تكرار لأن نقصان حادث
And it was mentioned before him: He bought a tree to make a door or the like from it and cut it, and found it not suitable for that, so he has recourse to compensation for the defect, not return, unless with the consent of his seller. End. And the author pointed out by stipulating the seller's consent a branch in al-Qunyah: if he returns the sold item due to a defect by judgment or without judgment or by mutual rescission, then the seller comes across a defect that occurred with the buyer, he has the right to return it. End. Then know that we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya that there are two exceptions from their statement: 'If a defect occurs and there is an old defect, he claims compensation or with his seller's consent.' One is sale by tawliya: if he sells something by tawliya and then a defect occurs with the buyer and there is an old defect, no recourse nor return, because if he had recourse the second price would be less than the first, and the principle of tawliya is that it be like the first; the commentator mentioned it in its chapter. The second is in salam: if he receives the salam goods and finds a defect that was with the seller, and a defect occurs with the buyer (the rabb al-salam), Abu Hanifa said: the seller (muslam ilayh) has the choice: if he wishes, he accepts it defective with the new defect, and if he wishes, he does not accept, and nothing is due from him, neither from the capital nor from the defect. Thus in al-Khaniyya from the chapter of salam, and al-Walwaliji mentioned it here and explained it: because if he paid the deficiency of the defect from the capital as Muhammad said, it would be substitution for quality, and that is riba. End. His statement: 'And whoever buys a cloth and cuts it and finds a defect in it, he has recourse to the defect' i.e., to the deficiency of the old defect because cutting is a defect. His statement: 'And if the seller accepts it similarly, he has that' because the prohibition is due to his right and he has agreed to it, and it is repetition because a new deficiency.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
Ruling: This passage contains several rulings. First, if someone buys a tree with the intention of making a door, and cuts it, then discovers a defect, he cannot return the tree because cutting it is a new defect; he only gets compensation unless the seller accepts the return. Second, a note from al-Qunyah: if a buyer returns a defective item via court or mutual cancellation, and later the seller discovers a defect that occurred while the item was with the buyer, the seller has the right to return it (i.e., to reverse the return) because he did not accept the item with that new defect. Third, there are two exceptions to the general rule that when a new defect occurs with an old one, the buyer claims compensation or returns with seller's consent: (a) Tawliya sale (sale at cost): if a new defect occurs, the buyer cannot claim compensation because that would reduce the price below cost, contradicting the nature of tawliya which requires equality; (b) Salam sale (advance purchase): if the received goods have an old defect and a new defect occurs with the buyer, the seller (not the buyer) has the choice to accept the defective goods or reject them, and no compensation is paid because that would involve riba. The passage ends with a repetitive comment on cutting a cloth, confirming that cutting is a new defect preventing return.

Key Terms
عيب قديم (al-ʿayb al-qadīm): A defect that existed at the time of sale.
عيب حادث (al-ʿayb al-ḥādith): A defect that occurs after the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the tree-cutting scenario, what must the seller consent to for the buyer to return the tree?
A) The return of the tree despite the cut.
B) Compensation for the cut.
C) The buyer's claim for full refund.
D) Buyer's compensation for the old defect.

Key Takeaway
A new defect caused by the buyer prevents return unless the seller consents; the seller can reverse a return if a new defect appears.

Exceptions to the Defect Rule: Tawliya and Salam
Two exceptions to the general defect rule in specific contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions to the general defect rule in tawliya and salam sales, and explain why compensation is prohibited in each.

Core Idea
In tawliya, compensation breaks cost equality; in salam, compensation would be riba.

This lesson examines two specific contracts where the normal right to compensation for defects is suspended: sale at cost (tawliya) and advance payment sale (salam). The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the reasoning: in tawliya, compensating would make the price differ from the original cost, violating the contract's nature; in salam, compensating would be an exchange for quality, amounting to riba.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule about defects
Normally, if a sold item has an old defect and a new defect occurs, the buyer can claim compensation or return with seller's consent. This lesson gives exceptions.
The general rule is mentioned in the text as 'their statement'.
2
Identify the first exception: tawliya sale
In a tawliya sale (selling at cost without profit), if a new defect occurs, the buyer cannot claim compensation or return because compensation would make the second price less than the first, contradicting the nature of tawliya.
The price must remain equal to the original cost.
3
Identify the second exception: salam contract
In salam (advance payment for future delivery), if the goods have an old defect and a new defect occurs with the buyer, Abu Hanifa gives the seller the choice to accept the defective goods or reject them, with no compensation. Compensation from the capital would be riba.
Imam Muhammad differed, but the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad reportedly allowed compensating for the old defect from the capital in salam, but Abu Hanifa prohibits it because it would be returning something extra for quality, i.e., riba.
The text cites al-Khaniyya and al-Walwaliji for Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر قبله شرى شجرة ليتخذ منها باباً أو نحوه فقطعها فوجدها لا تصلح لذلك فله الرجوع بنقص العيب لا الرد إلا برضا بائعه اهـ. وأشار المصنف باشتراط رضا البائع إلى فرع في القنية لو رد المبيع بعيب بقضاء أو بغير قضاء أو تقايلا ثم ظفر البائع بعيب حدث عند المشتري فله الرد .اهـ. ثم اعلم أنا كتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أنه يستثنى من قولهم لو حدث به عیب و به عيب قديم رجع بنقصه أو برضا بائعه مسألتان إحداهما بيع التولية لو باع شيئاً تولية ثم حدث به عيب عند المشتري وبه عيب قديم لا رجوع ولا رد لأنه لو رجع صار الثمن الثاني أنقص من الأول وقضية التولية أن يكون مثل الأول ذكره الشارح في بابها الثانية في السلم لو قبض المسلم فيه فوجد به عيباً كان عند المسلم إليه وحدث به عيب عند رب السلم قال أبو حنيفة خير المسلم إليه إن شاء قبله معيباً بالعيب الحادث وإن شاء لم يقبل ولا شيء عليه لا من رأس المال ولا . من العيب كذا في الخانية من باب السلم وذكره الولوالجي هنا وعلله بأنه لو غرم نقصان العيب من رأس المال كما قال محمد كان اعتياضاً عن الجودة وهو ربا .اهـ. قوله : (ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه فوجد به عيباً فقطعه رجع بالعيب أي بنقصان العيب القديم لأن القطع عيب قوله : (وإن قبله البائع كذلك فله ذلك لأن الامتناع لحقه وقد رضي به وهو تكرار لأن نقصان حادث
And it was mentioned before him: He bought a tree to make a door or the like from it and cut it, and found it not suitable for that, so he has recourse to compensation for the defect, not return, unless with the consent of his seller. End. And the author pointed out by stipulating the seller's consent a branch in al-Qunyah: if he returns the sold item due to a defect by judgment or without judgment or by mutual rescission, then the seller comes across a defect that occurred with the buyer, he has the right to return it. End. Then know that we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya that there are two exceptions from their statement: 'If a defect occurs and there is an old defect, he claims compensation or with his seller's consent.' One is sale by tawliya: if he sells something by tawliya and then a defect occurs with the buyer and there is an old defect, no recourse nor return, because if he had recourse the second price would be less than the first, and the principle of tawliya is that it be like the first; the commentator mentioned it in its chapter. The second is in salam: if he receives the salam goods and finds a defect that was with the seller, and a defect occurs with the buyer (the rabb al-salam), Abu Hanifa said: the seller (muslam ilayh) has the choice: if he wishes, he accepts it defective with the new defect, and if he wishes, he does not accept, and nothing is due from him, neither from the capital nor from the defect. Thus in al-Khaniyya from the chapter of salam, and al-Walwaliji mentioned it here and explained it: because if he paid the deficiency of the defect from the capital as Muhammad said, it would be substitution for quality, and that is riba. End. His statement: 'And whoever buys a cloth and cuts it and finds a defect in it, he has recourse to the defect' i.e., to the deficiency of the old defect because cutting is a defect. His statement: 'And if the seller accepts it similarly, he has that' because the prohibition is due to his right and he has agreed to it, and it is repetition because a new deficiency.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
Ruling: This passage contains several rulings. First, if someone buys a tree with the intention of making a door, and cuts it, then discovers a defect, he cannot return the tree because cutting it is a new defect; he only gets compensation unless the seller accepts the return. Second, a note from al-Qunyah: if a buyer returns a defective item via court or mutual cancellation, and later the seller discovers a defect that occurred while the item was with the buyer, the seller has the right to return it (i.e., to reverse the return) because he did not accept the item with that new defect. Third, there are two exceptions to the general rule that when a new defect occurs with an old one, the buyer claims compensation or returns with seller's consent: (a) Tawliya sale (sale at cost): if a new defect occurs, the buyer cannot claim compensation because that would reduce the price below cost, contradicting the nature of tawliya which requires equality; (b) Salam sale (advance purchase): if the received goods have an old defect and a new defect occurs with the buyer, the seller (not the buyer) has the choice to accept the defective goods or reject them, and no compensation is paid because that would involve riba. The passage ends with a repetitive comment on cutting a cloth, confirming that cutting is a new defect preventing return.

Key Terms
بيع التولية (bayʿ al-tawliya): Sale at the original purchase price (cost) without profit.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment for future delivery of goods (salam contract).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, if the goods have an old defect and a new defect occurs with the buyer, what does Abu Hanifa allow the seller to do?
A) Claim compensation for the old defect from the capital.
B) Either accept the defective goods or reject them, with no compensation.
C) Return the goods and get a refund of the capital plus compensation.
D) Force the buyer to accept the goods with a price reduction.

Key Takeaway
In tawliya and salam, the normal defect rules are suspended to preserve the contract's legal nature and avoid riba.

Intercourse with a Female Slave Prevents Return
Ruling on returning a female slave after intimate contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intercourse (or intimate contact with desire) with a female slave after purchase prevents return for defects and distinguish it from mere usage which does not prevent return.

Core Idea
Intercourse creates a new defect preventing return; only compensation is allowed.

In Hanafi sales law, certain actions by the buyer after purchase can alter their rights regarding defects. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that intimate contact with a female slave—intercourse or kissing with desire—is treated as a new defect that prevents the buyer from returning her for pre-existing defects. Instead, the buyer can only claim compensation. This rule is distinguished from mere usage, such as employing the slave for work, which does not prevent return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what action (intercourse, kissing with desire) prevents return and state the legal consequence: no return, only compensation.
The act must be with desire (shahwa); without desire, it does not prevent return.
2
Distinguish from mere usage
Compare with mere usage (istikhdam) such as using the slave for work. Usage does not prevent return because it does not create a new defect.
The illa (effective cause) is the creation of a new defect in the slave.
3
Examine the virginity scenario and intra-Hanafi difference
Consider the case where the buyer bought the slave on representation that she was a virgin. If he has intercourse and thereby knows she was not a virgin, the slave becomes binding (no return). Abu Yusuf allowed return with women's testimony.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is the majority opinion preventing return.
4
Apply the exceptions
The seller may agree to take back the slave without compensation. If so, return is allowed by mutual consent.
This exception shows the ruling is not absolute; it protects the seller from forced return after alteration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وفي الظهيرية ووطؤها يمنع الرد الخ مثله في الخانية حيث قال : اشترى جارية وقبضها فوطئها أو قبلها بشهوة ثم وجد بها عيباً لا يردها ولكن يرجع بنقصان العيب إلا إذا رضي البائع أن يأخذها ولا يدفع النقصان .اهـ. وقال في الخلاصة : وفي الأصل رجل اشترى جارية ولم يبرأ . من عيوبها فوطئها ثم وجد بها عيباً لا يملك ردها، سواء كانت بكراً أو ثيباً، نقصها الوطء أو لا، بخلاف الاستخدام. وكذا لو قبلها أو لمسها بشهوة ويرجع بالنقصان إلا أن يقول البائع أن أقبلها اهـ . لكن ذكر في الخانية في أول فصل العيوب : ولو اشترى جارية على أنها بكر ثم قال فإن القاضي يريها النساء إن قلن هي بكر كان القول للبائع ولا يمين عليه وإن قلن هي ثيب كان القول للبائع مع يمينه. وإن وطئها المشتري فعلم بالوطء فإن زايلها كما علم أنها ليست بكراً بلا لبث وإلا لزمته الجارية ؛ هكذا ذكر الشيخ أبو القاسم رحمه الله تعالى. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يردها بشهادة هي ٹیب
His statement: (And in al-Zahiriyya: Intercourse with her prevents return, etc.) Similar in al-Khaniyya where he said: He bought a female slave and took possession and had intercourse with her or kissed her with desire, then found a defect in her; he does not return her but claims compensation for the defect, unless the seller agrees to take her and does not pay the deficiency. End. And he said in al-Khulasa: And in al-Asl: A man bought a female slave and was not free from her defects, then had intercourse with her, then found a defect; he does not have the right to return her, whether she was a virgin or non-virgin, whether intercourse diminished her or not, unlike using her [for work]. Similarly, if he kissed her or touched her with desire, he claims compensation unless the seller says 'I accept her'. End. But it is mentioned in al-Khaniyya in the first chapter of defects: If he bought a female slave on condition that she was a virgin, then he said: The judge should show her to women; if they say she is a virgin, the seller's word is taken and no oath; if they say she is deflowered, the seller's word is taken with his oath. And if the buyer had intercourse with her and knows by intercourse that she was not a virgin without doubt, then the female slave becomes binding on him; thus mentioned by Shaykh Abu al-Qasim, may Allah have mercy on him. And from Abu Yusuf that he returns her with the testimony of women? And she is deflowered?
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
Ruling: This passage deals with the female slave (jariya) special case. When a buyer has intercourse with or kisses with desire (shahwa) a female slave, he cannot return her for any pre-existing defect because his action (intercourse) is a new defect that prevents return. He can only claim compensation for the old defect, unless the seller agrees to take her back without compensation. This applies whether the slave is virgin or not, and whether intercourse caused a decrease in value or not, unlike mere usage (e.g., using her for work) which does not prevent return. The passage also mentions a specific case: if the buyer bought her on the representation that she was a virgin and then had intercourse, if he thereby becomes certain she was not a virgin, he cannot return her and must keep her. However, Abu Yusuf is reported to allow return if there is evidence from women testifying that she was deflowered before purchase.

Key Terms
الجارية (al-jāriya): A female slave; in this context, subject to special rules due to potential offspring and moral considerations.
الوطء (al-waṭʾ): Sexual intercourse; considered a major new defect that prevents return.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire; a condition for the action to be considered a new defect preventing return.
الرد (ar-radd): Return of the sold item due to defect.
نقصان العيب (nuqsān al-ʿayb): Compensation for the decrease in value caused by the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He can return the slave but must pay for the kiss.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse or intimate contact with desire after purchase creates a new defect that prevents the right of return; only compensation is available.

Khiyar al-Ayb: Buyer's Alteration Bars Return
Ruling on khiyar al-ayb with buyer's alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a buyer who makes a material change to a defective item cannot return it, but may claim compensation, and apply the rule to scenarios including cutting cloth, heating iron, sharpening, and cutting a tree.

Core Idea
A buyer who alters the goods cannot return them for a defect, but can still claim compensation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains a key limitation on khiyar al-ayb: if the buyer's own action creates a new defect in the item, the right to return (radd) is lost, but compensation (naqs al-ayb) remains. We will walk through the examples to understand the principle and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main principle
Read the opening line: if a buyer cuts a cloth and finds a defect, he has recourse for the defect (compensation) but cannot return it unless the seller agrees. Why? Because the cutting itself is a new defect.
The principle: any change that diminishes the item's value (new defect) bars return.
2
Examine the fire example
If a buyer puts iron into fire and sees a defect, he cannot return it because iron diminishes by fire. This is contrasted with gold and silver, which only diminish when they melt (before melting, return allowed).
The test: does the action cause a reduction in the item's value? If yes, return barred.
3
Analyze the knife sharpening
Sharpening with a stone does not diminish the knife, so return allowed. Sharpening with a file does diminish it, so return barred.
Distinguish actions that preserve value vs. those that reduce it.
4
Apply to the tree scenario
Buying a tree to make a door, cutting it, then finding it unsuitable: return barred (cutting is a change), but compensation for the original defect remains. Return only if seller agrees.
Same principle applies to any object that is cut or altered.
5
Summarize the remedy
When return is barred, the buyer's only remedy is compensation (naqs al-ayb), calculated as the difference between the price of a sound item and the defective item at the time of sale.
Compensation is always available, even if return is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار العيب بائعه ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه فوجد به عيباً رجع بالعيب وإن قبله البائع كذلك فله ذلك وإن لم يرده وإن رضي بائعه وهذا شكل ولو أدخل في النار قدوماً فرأى عيبه لم يرده إذا الحديد ينقص بالنار بخلاف الذهب والفضة كحديد أقول الذهب ينتقص في النار إذا ذاب إيضاً اللهم إلا أن يكون قبل الذوب لو حدد سكيناً فرأى عيبه فإن حدده بحجر فله الرد لا لو حدده بمبرد لأنه ينتقص منه .اهـ. وذكر قبله شرى شجرة ليتخذ منها باباً أو نحوه فقطعها فوجدها لا تصلح لذلك فله الرجوع بنقص العيب لا الرد إلا برضا بائعه اهـ.
Chapter of the Option of Defect. And whoever buys a cloth and cuts it, then finds a defect in it, he has recourse for the defect (i.e., compensation for the original defect). And if the seller accepts it likewise, then he has that (i.e., the seller may accept the return). [But] if he does not return it, and the seller is satisfied, this is a form [of acceptance]. And if he puts it into fire and sees its defect, he does not return it, because iron diminishes by fire, unlike gold and silver. [As for] iron, I say: gold is diminished by fire when it melts, except before melting. If he sharpens a knife and sees its defect, then if he sharpened it with a stone, he has the right of return; not if he sharpened it with a file, because it diminishes from it. End. And before it was mentioned: He bought a tree to make a door or the like from it, and cut it, and found it not suitable for that, so he has recourse for the deficiency of the defect, not the return, except with the consent of the seller. End.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn on khiyar al-ayb. The author states that if a buyer cuts a cloth and then discovers a defect, he may claim the deficiency of the original defect (i.e., compensation) but cannot return the cloth because the cutting is itself a new defect. However, if the seller agrees to accept the return, it is permissible. The text then discusses a sub-case: if a buyer puts iron into fire and sees a defect, he cannot return it because the iron is diminished by the flame, and the fire itself caused a change. This is contrasted with gold and silver, which are not diminished by fire until they melt. Similarly, if one sharpens a knife with a stone, that does not diminish it, so the buyer may return it; but sharpening with a file does diminish it, so return is not allowed. Another branch is mentioned: if a person buys a tree with the intention of making a door, cuts it, and then finds it unsuitable, he may claim compensation for the defect but cannot return the tree unless the seller agrees. The overarching principle is that any change made by the buyer that is itself a defect prevents return, but compensation for the original defect remains.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option for the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation.
الرد (radd): Return of the sold item to the seller due to a defect.
نقصان العيب (naqs al-ayb): Compensation for the reduction in value caused by the original defect, measured by the difference between the price of a sound item and the defective one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a buyer cuts a cloth and then discovers a defect, what is his recourse?
A) He may return the cloth and demand a full refund.
B) He may only claim compensation for the original defect, not return the cloth unless the seller agrees.
C) He has no recourse because cutting invalidates the sale.
D) He may return the cloth only if he cut it with scissors, not with a knife.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who alters a defective item loses the right to return it, but retains the right to compensation for the original defect.

Exception to Defect Compensation: Bai' al-Tawliya
Exception for tawliya sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that in a tawliya sale, the buyer cannot claim compensation for an old defect after a new defect occurs, and explain the reason based on price parity.

Core Idea
In tawliya, compensation for an old defect is barred to keep the sale price equal to the seller's cost.

We previously learned the general rule: if a buyer finds an old defect after a new defect has occurred, he can claim compensation or return with the seller's consent. However, this lesson presents a key exception for bai' al-tawliya, a trust sale at cost. The rationale hinges on the nature of tawliya, where the price must mirror the original cost exactly. Any compensation would reduce the price below cost, violating the contract's basis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
State the general rule from the previous lesson: when an old defect and a new defect coexist, the buyer has the option to claim compensation for the old defect or return the item with the seller's consent.
This rule applies to most sales.
2
Identify the exception
Recognize that bai' al-tawliya is an exception: if a seller sells at his cost price, and the buyer later discovers an old defect after a new defect has occurred, the buyer loses the right to compensation or return.
The exception is specific to tawliya, not other trust sales like murabaha.
3
Apply the exception to a scenario
Imagine a seller sells an item at exactly his purchase price (tawliya). The buyer later finds a hidden defect and also accidentally damages the item. According to the exception, the buyer cannot claim compensation for the hidden defect.
The buyer's only option is to keep the item with the new defect.
4
Explain the rationale
Understand why: compensation would make the second price lower than the first, contradicting the requirement that tawliya must be equal to the original cost. This maintains contractual consistency.
The commentator in the source (al-Sharih) refers to the chapter on tawliya for further details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنا كتبنا رد في الفوائد الفقهية أنه يستثنى من قولهم لو حدث به عیب و به عيب قديم رجع بنقصه أو برضا بائعه مسألتان إحداهما بيع التولية لو باع شيئاً تولية ثم حدث به عيب عند المشتري وبه عيب قديم لا رجوع ولا رد لأنه لو رجع صار الثمن الثاني أنقص من الأول وقضية التولية أن يكون مثل الأول ذكره الشارح في بابها الثانية في السلم لو قبض المسلم فيه فوجد به عيباً كان عند المسلم إليه وحدث به عيب عند رب السلم قال أبو حنيفة خير المسلم إليه إن شاء قبله معيباً بالعيب الحادث وإن شاء لم يقبل ولا شيء عليه لا من رأس المال ولا من العيب كذا في الخانية من باب السلم وذكره الولوالجي هنا وعلله بأنه لو غرم نقصان العيب من رأس المال كما قال محمد كان اعتياضاً عن الجودة وهو ربا .اهـ.
Then know that we have written a response in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya that two matters are excepted from their statement: 'If a defect occurs with an old defect, he has recourse for the deficiency or with the consent of the seller.' One of them is bai' al-tawliya: if he sells something as tawliya, then a defect occurs with the buyer, and there is an old defect, there is no recourse and no return, because if he had recourse, the second price would be less than the first, and the requirement of tawliya is that it be like the first. The commentator mentioned this in its chapter. The second is in salam: if he takes delivery of the salam commodity and finds it defective with a defect that existed with the seller, and a new defect occurs with the buyer, Abu Hanifa said: the seller [muslam ilayh] has the choice: if he wishes, he may accept it defective with the new defect; if he wishes, he may not accept it, and nothing is due from him, neither from the capital nor from the defect. This is in al-Khaniyya in the chapter of salam, and al-Walwaliji mentioned it here and explained it: because if he were to pay the compensation for the defect from the capital, as Muhammad said, it would be an exchange for quality and that is riba. End.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage brings two exceptions to the general rule that when a buyer has an item with both an old defect and a new defect, he can claim compensation for the old defect or return with the seller's consent. The first exception is in bai' al-tawliya (a sale at cost price). Here, if the buyer discovers an old defect after a new defect has occurred, he cannot claim compensation because that would reduce the price below the original cost, contradicting the nature of tawliya. The second exception is in salam (advance purchase). According to Abu Hanifa, if the seller delivers a defective commodity and then a new defect arises with the buyer, the seller may choose to accept the defective item with the new defect or reject it, and the buyer cannot force compensation. Muhammad al-Shaybani held that compensation could be paid, but Abu Hanifa objected that this would be riba because it exchanges a lower payment for quality. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
بيع التولية (bai' al-tawliya): A sale where the seller transfers the item at exactly his cost price, without profit.
رد العيب (radd al-ayb): Return of the item due to defect, or claim for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a bai' al-tawliya sale, why does the buyer lose the right to compensation for an old defect when a new defect occurs?
A) Because the buyer caused the new defect.
B) Because compensating would make the price lower than the seller's cost, violating tawliya.
C) Because the seller can choose not to compensate.
D) Because the old defect is considered part of the sale.

Key Takeaway
In a tawliya sale, price parity prevents compensation for any pre-existing defect when a new defect arises.

Exception to Defect Compensation: Salam and the Riba Concern
Exception for salam sale with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa denied compensation for an old defect in a salam contract and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
In salam, compensating for an old defect from the paid capital is riba, as it exchanges a fixed sum for better quality.

The second exception to the defect compensation rule occurs in salam (forward sale). Abu Hanifa ruled that if the delivered commodity has an old defect and a new defect arises with the buyer, the seller has a choice: either accept the defective item with the new defect or reject it, but the buyer cannot force compensation. This lesson explores the reasoning and the disagreement with Muhammad al-Shaybani, who allowed compensation from the capital. Abu Hanifa's view is ultimately preferred due to the prohibition of riba.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
The general rule: when an old defect and a new defect coexist, the buyer can claim compensation for the old defect or return with consent.
This applies to most sales.
2
Identify the salam exception
In salam, if the seller delivers a defective commodity (old defect) and a new defect occurs with the buyer, Abu Hanifa says the buyer has no recourse. The seller may accept the item with the new defect or reject it, but owes no compensation.
The exception is specific to salam due to its advance payment nature.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Muhammad al-Shaybani disagreed: he held that the buyer could claim compensation for the old defect from the capital (the paid price).
Both are Hanafi views; Abu Hanifa's is considered more sound.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa's reason: compensating from the capital would be an exchange for quality, which constitutes riba (interest). Since the capital is fixed, any reduction for defect is a prohibited increase in value received.
Al-Walwaliji provided this explanation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: no compensation, due to the risk of riba.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنا كتبنا رد في الفوائد الفقهية أنه يستثنى من قولهم لو حدث به عیب و به عيب قديم رجع بنقصه أو برضا بائعه مسألتان إحداهما بيع التولية لو باع شيئاً تولية ثم حدث به عيب عند المشتري وبه عيب قديم لا رجوع ولا رد لأنه لو رجع صار الثمن الثاني أنقص من الأول وقضية التولية أن يكون مثل الأول ذكره الشارح في بابها الثانية في السلم لو قبض المسلم فيه فوجد به عيباً كان عند المسلم إليه وحدث به عيب عند رب السلم قال أبو حنيفة خير المسلم إليه إن شاء قبله معيباً بالعيب الحادث وإن شاء لم يقبل ولا شيء عليه لا من رأس المال ولا من العيب كذا في الخانية من باب السلم وذكره الولوالجي هنا وعلله بأنه لو غرم نقصان العيب من رأس المال كما قال محمد كان اعتياضاً عن الجودة وهو ربا .اهـ.
Then know that we have written a response in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya that two matters are excepted from their statement: 'If a defect occurs with an old defect, he has recourse for the deficiency or with the consent of the seller.' One of them is bai' al-tawliya: if he sells something as tawliya, then a defect occurs with the buyer, and there is an old defect, there is no recourse and no return, because if he had recourse, the second price would be less than the first, and the requirement of tawliya is that it be like the first. The commentator mentioned this in its chapter. The second is in salam: if he takes delivery of the salam commodity and finds it defective with a defect that existed with the seller, and a new defect occurs with the buyer, Abu Hanifa said: the seller [muslam ilayh] has the choice: if he wishes, he may accept it defective with the new defect; if he wishes, he may not accept it, and nothing is due from him, neither from the capital nor from the defect. This is in al-Khaniyya in the chapter of salam, and al-Walwaliji mentioned it here and explained it: because if he were to pay the compensation for the defect from the capital, as Muhammad said, it would be an exchange for quality and that is riba. End.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage brings two exceptions to the general rule that when a buyer has an item with both an old defect and a new defect, he can claim compensation for the old defect or return with the seller's consent. The first exception is in bai' al-tawliya (a sale at cost price). Here, if the buyer discovers an old defect after a new defect has occurred, he cannot claim compensation because that would reduce the price below the original cost, contradicting the nature of tawliya. The second exception is in salam (advance purchase). According to Abu Hanifa, if the seller delivers a defective commodity and then a new defect arises with the buyer, the seller may choose to accept the defective item with the new defect or reject it, and the buyer cannot force compensation. Muhammad al-Shaybani held that compensation could be paid, but Abu Hanifa objected that this would be riba because it exchanges a lower payment for quality. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
السلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for future delivery of specified goods.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; in this context, any unjustified increase in exchange.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or principal amount paid in advance in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is compensating for an old defect from the capital in a salam contract prohibited?
A) Because the seller must pay from his own pocket.
B) Because it would be an exchange of quality for the fixed price, which is riba.
C) Because the buyer already accepted the goods.
D) Because the defect is considered minor.

Key Takeaway
In salam, compensating for a pre-existing defect from the paid capital is disallowed because it constitutes riba.

Physical Changes and Intercourse Prevent Return of a Defective Item
Detailed discussion on how actions like intercourse, castration, and selling affect the buyer's right to return a defective item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that physical changes (such as cutting, sewing, dyeing, or intercourse) to a purchased item prevent the buyer from returning it due to a defect and instead limit the buyer to claiming compensation for the defect.

Core Idea
Any physical change to a purchased item that constitutes a new defect prevents return; the buyer may only claim compensation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the effect of various post-purchase actions on the buyer's khyar al-ayb (option of defect). The central principle is that if the buyer introduces a new change to the item, even a permissible one like intercourse with a slave-girl or cutting cloth, the right to return the item for an old defect is lost. Instead, the buyer may seek monetary compensation for the defect. The commentary nuances this rule with several scenarios, including actions by third parties and the interaction with other legal considerations such as shubha (uncertainty) and the prohibition of wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and its legal effect
Review the text and list the specific actions mentioned (cutting, sewing, dyeing, intercourse, castration) and for each, determine whether it prevents return or not.
Note that the default is that return is prevented unless specified otherwise.
2
Recognize the analogy
The text explicitly states that intercourse with a slave-girl is like cutting cloth. Both are considered changes that prevent return. Understand why the Hanafi school treats these as analogous.
This analogy is based on the shared 'illa of a change that itself could be a new defect.
3
Determine the mufta bihi position
Identify which Hanafi position is relied upon (from Zahir al-Riwayah) and note that it overrides views that might allow return.
The commentary states that Abu Qasim's view is weak; the madhhab is what is in al-Asl.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., buyer grinds wheat into flour then finds defect), apply the rule: return is prevented, compensation allowed.
Consider whether the action diminishes value or changes the item's identity.

If the buyer sells it, he cannot claim anything. Even if he cuts it and sews it, or dyes it, or mixes sawiq with ghee, then he discovers [defect] and his recourse and permissibility of return with the consent of the seller in cloth are among what he presented. No benefit appears for singling out cloth except to base on it the issue of if he sews it, then the return is prevented even with his consent. And he could have said initially: or return with the consent of the seller except when an increase occurs. And intercourse with a slave-girl is like cutting cloth. And in al-Zahiriyya: 'Intercourse with her prevents return due to defect, whether she was a virgin or not, and he may have recourse for the deficiency unless the seller says: I accept her likewise.' And intercourse by someone other than the buyer also prevents return due to defect, whether it is based on shubha or not. Except that if the intercourse is based on shubha, the buyer may have recourse for the deficiency, even if the seller says: I accept her likewise, because of the obligatory dower from intercourse based on shubha. And if the slave-girl had a husband with the seller and her husband had intercourse with her at the buyer's place, if the slave-girl was a virgin, then the buyer may not return her; if she was non-virgin, if the intercourse diminished her, then the answer is the same; if it did not diminish her, then the buyer may return her. This is if the husband had intercourse once in the seller's hand and then had intercourse at the buyer's place. But if he had not ever had intercourse with her at the seller's place and only had intercourse at the buyer's place, Muhammad did not mention this section in al-Asl. The mashayikh differed, and the correct view is that she is returned with the defect. And if he bought a mule and castrated it, then discovered a defect after castration, he has the right of return if the castration did not diminish it. This is in the fatawa of the people of Samarkand, but Imam Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani used to give fatwa contrary to it. End. Statement (if the buyer sells it, he does not return to anything) because he is holding it by sale and it is possible to return with the seller's consent, so he has forfeited the return. He made it absolute, so it includes whether he sold it after seeing the defect or before, as in Fath al-Qadir, and whether out of necessity or not. According to al-Qunyah: He bought a fish and found it defective, and the seller was absent, and if he waited for him it would spoil, so he fried it and sold it. He does not have recourse for the deficiency of the defect, and there is no way for him to pay this harm. He was asked about a similar case with apricots, and he said: He does not have recourse according to Abu Hanifa's saying. End. And in al-Muhit, with reference to al-Jami': He bought juice and took possession, then it became wine, then he found a defect. He does not return it even if the seller agrees, because in the return there is acquisition of wine intentionally, because return by mutual consent is a new sale concerning the owner, and prohibition of wine acquisition is a right of the Shari'a, so it is considered a new sale regarding him. If it becomes vinegar, he does not return it unless the seller agrees, because it became defective with another defect, because he took it sweet and returns it sour. And he has recourse for the deficiency of the defect in both cases. Similarly, two Christians who bought wine and took possession, then became Muslims, then the buyer found a defect in the wine... women. End. And it is distinguished between when he finds a defect after intercourse and when he knows the defect by intercourse, so consider what its face is. Then I saw in al-Qunyah mention of Abu Qasim's statement, and he symbolized and said: 'Intercourse prevents return, and that is the madhhab.' End. Its implication is that what Abu Qasim said is contrary to the madhhab because it contradicts what was passed from al-Asl, which is from the books of Zahir al-Riwayah. And the expression of al-Khaniyya with 'thus mentioned etc.' suggests its weakness. It is established that intercourse and its incentives prevent return due to defect. And by this, the answer to the question of the fatwa emerges: He bought a Roman slave-girl for concubinage, and had intercourse with her, then found her to be retracted, and two women also informed about that. So if the seller takes a definitive oath, nothing is due from him, as will come.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This extensive passage from the commentary addresses various scenarios where a physical change or action affects the buyer's right to return a defective item. The overarching principle is that any change that is itself a defect prevents return, but compensation for the original defect remains. The commentary begins by discussing the sale of cloth: sewing or dyeing prevents return even with seller consent. It then focuses on intercourse with a slave-girl. The relied-upon Hanafi view is that intercourse (or even passionate kissing/touching) prevents the buyer from returning the slave-girl for a defect; he may only claim compensation. This applies whether the buyer or a third party (even the seller's husband) performs the intercourse, with some nuances: if the husband's intercourse does not diminish her, return is allowed. If the intercourse is based on shubha (uncertainty), the buyer may still claim compensation. There is a dispute among scholars regarding whether the buyer can return the slave-girl if he knows of the defect through the act of intercourse itself; the correct view is that intercourse prevents return. The passage also covers castration of an animal: if the castration does not diminish the animal's value, return is allowed, but there is a difference of opinion. If the buyer sells the defective item after discovering the defect, he forfeits all rights, even if he sold out of necessity (e.g., fish that would spoil). A special case is juice that turns into wine: the buyer cannot return it because that would involve handing over wine, which is illicit. If it turns into vinegar, return requires seller consent. The passage concludes with a fatwa about a Roman slave-girl found to be retracted after intercourse, stating that if the seller swears, no compensation is due.

Key Terms
وطء (wati'): Sexual intercourse with a slave-girl.
جارية (jariyah): A female slave; in this context, purchased for concubinage.
رتقاء (ratqa'): A woman with a vaginal obstruction preventing intercourse; a defect in a slave-girl.
شبهة (shubha): Legal uncertainty that may affect the ruling of intercourse (e.g., believing the slave-girl is his wife).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He may return her and demand a full refund.
B) He cannot return her but may claim compensation for the defect.
C) He must keep her with no compensation.
D) He may return her only if she is a virgin.

Key Takeaway
Any physical change to a purchased item, including intercourse, cuts off the buyer's right to return a defective item, though compensation remains available.

Actions That Prevent Return of a Defective Item
Rulings on acts by the buyer that forfeit the right to return a defective item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE which actions by the buyer bar the return of a defective item and EXPLAIN the distinction between temporary and permanent alterations.

Core Idea
Only permanent changes like stitching bar return completely; cutting, dyeing, or toasting still allow return with the seller's consent.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on khiyār al-ʿayb—the buyer's right to return a defective item. The central question: does the buyer's act of physically altering or disposing of the item forfeit that right? The Hanafi school makes careful distinctions between selling the item, which absolutely bars return, and other physical acts like cutting, dyeing, or toasting, which only bar return if they amount to a permanent alteration. Understanding these nuances is crucial for determining the buyer's remedies in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its consequence
For each act mentioned (selling, cutting, stitching, dyeing, toasting, intercourse), note whether the return right is lost or preserved.
2
Distinguish between temporary and permanent alterations
Cutting is reversible (if not stitched), while stitching is irreversible. The text says stitching prevents return even with consent.
Permanent changes create a new status that cannot be undone.
3
Understand the role of seller's consent
For cutting, dyeing, toasting, return is permitted only with the seller's agreement, treated as a new sale.
4
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
Intercourse with a slave-girl is like cutting cloth; it does not bar return if seller consents.
Analogy is based on the nature of the alteration.
5
Note the exception for increases
The text hints at a later discussion: 'except when an increase occurs' – a potential bar.
Increases may also prevent return; this is mentioned but not detailed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باعه المشتري لم يرجع بشيء ولو قطعه وحاطه أو صبغه أو لت السويق بسمن فاطلع على رجوعه وجواز رده برضا بائعه في الثوب من أفراد ما قدمه ولم تظهر فائدة لإفراد الثوب إلا ليترتب عليه مسألة ما إذا خاطه فإنه يمتنع الرد ولو برضاه وكان يمكنه أن يقول أولاً أو رد برضا بائعه إلا عند حدوث زيادة ووطء الجارية كقطع الثوب
If the buyer sold [the defective item], he does not recover anything. And if he cut it, or stitched it, or dyed it, or toasted the sawīq with ghee, then discovered [the defect], he may return it with the seller's consent. In the case of cloth, it is an instance of what was presented; no benefit appears in specifying cloth except to establish the issue that if he stitched it, return is prevented even with his consent. And he could have said initially: 'or return with the seller's consent except when an increase occurs.' And intercourse with the slave-girl is like cutting the cloth.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about actions of the buyer that affect the right to return a defective item (khiyār al-ʿayb). The general rule: if the buyer sells the defective item, he forfeits all claim to compensation. If he cuts, stitches, dyes, or toasts the item, he may still return it with the seller's voluntary acceptance. However, stitching is an exception: once stitched, return is absolutely prohibited, even if the seller agrees, because stitching is a permanent change. The comparison of intercourse with a slave-girl to cutting indicates that intercourse does not absolutely bar return if the seller consents. The phrase 'except when an increase occurs' refers to a later discussion about increases preventing return.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyār al-ʿayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation for the defect
الرد برضا بائعه (al-radd bi-riḍā bāʿihi): Return of the defective item by the buyer with the voluntary agreement of the seller, treated as a new sale
السويق (al-sawīq): A type of toasted grain or porridge

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Yes, unconditionally.
D) No, even with seller's agreement.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the buyer may return a defective item after cutting, dyeing, or toasting only with the seller's consent; stitching absolutely bars return even with consent.
