When the Buyer's Alteration Prevents Return of a Defective Item
Ruling with rational proof on buyer's alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer makes an inseparable alteration to a defective item and explain why only the deficiency (nuqsan) can be claimed.

Core Idea
An inseparable increase by the buyer blocks return; only the difference in value can be claimed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq clarifies a key principle in sale of defective goods: when the buyer alters the item in a way that adds an inseparable increase (like cutting, sewing, or dyeing), the right to return (radd) is lost. The Shariʿa prevents return because rescission cannot apply to part nor to the whole with the increase. The seller also cannot compel return. Instead, the buyer recovers the deficiency in value (nuqsān). Understanding this ruling helps you distinguish between valid return and compensation-only claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The buyer cuts, sews, dyes the item, or mixes sawiq with ghee, then discovers a defect. The ruling: he cannot return the item but claims the deficiency (nuqsān).
2
Locate the basis of the ruling
The text states that return is prevented because of the increase (ziyāda). The increase is inseparable (muttasila), so rescission cannot be partial (without the increase) nor total (with the increase, which is not part of the sale).
Rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī).
3
Understand the limit on the seller
The seller cannot take the item back either, because the prevention is a Shariʿa ruling, not a right of the seller.
The seller's consent is irrelevant.
4
Compare with analogous cases
The text analogizes to the buyer selling the item after seeing the defect: in both cases, the right of return is forfeited, and only the deficiency can be claimed.
Analogy (qiyās) within the same legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنقصانه كما لو باعه بعد رؤية العيب أو مات العبد أو أعتقه وإن أعتقه على مال أو لأن البائع الأول اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : وإذا طعن المشتري بعيب فصالحه على شيء أخذه أو حط من ثمنه شيئاً فإن كان يقدر على رد المبيع والمطالبة بأرش العيب فالصلح جائز، وإن لم يقدر فالصلح باطل نحو أن يكون المشتري باع المعيب لكونه أبطل حقه في الرد متى باعه اه قوله : (ولو قطعه وخاطه أو صبغه أو لت السويق بسمن فاطلع على عيب رجع بنقصانه كما لو باعه بعد رؤية العيب) لامتناع الرد بسبب الزيادة لأن لا وجه للفسخ في الأصل دونها لأنها لا تنفك عنه ولا وجه إليه معها لأن الزيادة ليست بمبيعة فامتنع أصلاً، وليس للبائع أن يأخذه لأن الامتناع لحق الشرع لا لحقه فإن باعه المشتري بعد ما رأى العيب رجع بالنقصان لأن الرد أصلاً قبله فلا يكون بالبيع حابساً للمبيع.
Due to the deficiency, as if he sold it after seeing the defect, or the slave died, or he manumitted him, even if he manumitted him for a consideration, or because the first seller... etc. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: If the buyer claims a defect and then settles for something taken or a reduction in price, if he is able to return the sold item and claim the compensation for the defect, the settlement is valid; if he is not able, the settlement is void, such as when the buyer sold the defective item, because he forfeited his right to return when he sold it. His statement: (And if he cuts it, sews it, dyes it, or mixes the sawiq with ghee and then discovers a defect, he recourse for the deficiency, just as if he sold it after seeing the defect) due to the impossibility of return because of the increase, because there is no way to rescind the original without it (the increase) since it is inseparable, and no way to rescind with it because the increase is not part of the sale, so it is absolutely prevented. And the seller may not take it because the prevention is due to a right of the Shari'a, not a right of his. If the buyer sells it after seeing the defect, he recourses for the deficiency because the right of return was already forfeited before, so by selling he does not become a detainor of the sold item.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It clarifies the effect of a buyer's alteration on the right of return (radd) for a defect. The general principle is that if the buyer makes a change to the item that adds an inseparable increase (ziyada muttasila), such as cutting, sewing, dyeing, or mixing, then the right to return the item is lost. Instead, the buyer can only claim the difference in value (nuqsan) between the defective and sound item. The reasoning is that rescission (faskh) cannot apply to part of the item (the increase is not sold) nor to the whole (since the increase is inseparable). The seller also cannot take the item back because the prohibition on return is a Shari'a ruling, not a seller's right. The passage compares this to selling after seeing the defect: if the buyer sells the item after discovering the defect, he also forfeits return and claims only the deficiency.

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): The right to return the defective item to the seller and demand a full refund.
النقصان (al-nuqṣān): The difference between the value of the item in its defective state and its sound state, claimed when return is not possible.
الزيادة (al-ziyāda): An addition to the sold item that is not part of the original sale, either separable or inseparable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer dyes a garment (inseparable increase) and then discovers a defect, what is his recourse according to the Hanafi school?
A) He can return the garment and get a full refund.
B) He can only claim the deficiency in value and cannot return the garment.
C) He can claim the full price from the seller.
D) He can return the garment but must pay for the dye.

Key Takeaway
An inseparable increase by the buyer forfeits the right to return the defective item, but the buyer retains the right to claim the deficiency in value.

Altering a Defective Garment: Minor Son vs Adult Son
Ruling with proof: distinction based on ownership transfer timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings when a buyer cuts and sews a garment for his minor son versus his adult son, and state the ownership principle that determines the right to recourse for a defect.

Core Idea
Ownership transfer timing determines whether alteration forfeits the right to claim a deficiency.

After learning the general ruling that a buyer who alters a purchased item loses the right to return it for a defect (due to returning only with decrease), this passage refines the rule by considering who the alteration is for. The key factor is when ownership of the garment transfers to the recipient. For a minor son, ownership transfers at cutting; for an adult son, only upon delivery. This distinction determines whether the buyer can still claim the deficiency after discovering the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The buyer buys a garment, cuts and sews it for his son, then discovers a defect. There are two cases: the son is a minor, or the son is an adult.
The ruling differs based on the son's age.
2
Determine when ownership transfers
For a minor son, ownership transfers by cutting (the father's act of making a garment for him). For an adult son, ownership transfers only upon physical delivery (taslim).
This is from the Hanafi rules of hibah.
3
Assess the right to recourse
If ownership transferred before sewing (minor), the father no longer owns the garment when he sews it, so he cannot claim deficiency. If ownership transferred after sewing (adult), the father sewed while still owner, making return impossible, so he can claim deficiency.
The principle: return is forfeit if buyer removes item from his ownership while he can return; return is preserved if he removes it when return is impossible.
4
Apply the principle to a declared loan
If the father explicitly states at the time of cutting that the garment is a loan (ariyah), then ownership does not transfer to the minor son, so the father retains ownership and can claim deficiency.
The explicit declaration overrides the default gift (hibah) ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قلنا إن من اشترى ثوباً فقطعه لباساً ممتنع لولده الصغير وخاطه ثم اطلع على عيب لا يرجع بالنقصان، ولو كان الولد كبيراً يرجع التمليك حصل في الأول قبل الخياطة، وفي الثاني بعدها بالتسليم إليه، وهذا معنى ما في الفوائد الظهيرية من أن الأصل أن كل موضع يكون المبيع قائماً على ملك المشتري ويمكنه الرد برضا البائع فأخرجه عن ملكه لا يرجع بالنقصان، وكل موضع يكون المبيع قائماً على ملكه ولا يمكنه الرد، وإن قبله البائع فأخرجه عن ملكه يرجع بالنقصان اهـ . لكن وقع التقييد بالخياطة في الثوب الموهوب للولد في الهداية وهو احترازي في الكبير اتفاقي في الصغير فإنه بمجرد القطع له صار ملكاً له فلا رجوع. وفي الكبير القطع والخياطة على ملك نفسه فلما دفعه إليه بعدها أخرجه عن ملكه بعد امتناع رده شرعاً فرجع؛ كذا في المعراج وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في الهبة أنه لو اتخذ لولده الصغير ثياباً يملكه وفي الكبير بالتسليم وليس كالطعام يأكله على ملك أبيه لأن الأمر إذا توجه إلى وجوه فأولاها بالحكم أغلبها تعارفاً وإلا غلب البر والصلة إلا إذا علم بالدليل كونه إعارة كالإشهاد عند الاتخاذ لعدم الاعتبار بالدلالة عند التعارض ؛ كذا في هبة البزازية وقبلها اتخذ لولده ثياباً ليس له أن يدفعها إلى غيره إلا إذا بين وقت الاتخاذ أنها عارية .اهـ. فعلى هذا لو صرح بأنها عارية لا يسقط حقه في الرجوع بنقصان العيب إذا خاطه لولده الصغير.
And upon this we say: If someone buys a garment and cuts it as a ready-made dress for his minor son and sews it, then discovers a defect, he does not recourse for the deficiency. But if the son is an adult, he recourses. The transfer of ownership occurred in the first case before sewing, and in the second after sewing by delivery to him. This is the meaning of what is in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah: The principle is that whenever the sold item remains in the ownership of the buyer and he can return it with the seller's consent, but then he removes it from his ownership, he does not recourse for the deficiency. And whenever the sold item remains in his ownership but he cannot return it, even if the seller accepts it, and he removes it from his ownership, he recourses for the deficiency. But the restriction regarding sewing in the gifted garment was mentioned in al-Hidayah: it is precautionary in the case of an adult son, and agreed upon in the case of a minor, for by merely cutting it becomes his property, so there is no recourse. In the case of an adult, cutting and sewing are on his own ownership; when he gives it to him after that, he removes it from his ownership after the Shari'a prevented its return, so he recourses. Thus in al-Mi'raj and it will come, Allah willing, in hibah that if he makes garments for his minor son, he owns them; for the adult, he owns them upon delivery. It is not like food that the son eats while still in his father's ownership, because when a matter is directed to multiple aspects, the one most appropriate for the ruling is the one most common customarily, otherwise piety and kindness prevail, unless evidence indicates it is a loan, such as having witnesses at the time of making, because conflicting indications do not override each other. Thus in Hibah al-Bazzaziyyah: If he makes garments for his son, he may not give them to others unless he specifies at the time of making that they are a loan. So here, if he explicitly states it is a loan, his right to recourse for the deficiency due to the defect is not forfeited if he sews it for his minor son.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the previous ruling by applying it to a specific scenario: a buyer who cuts and sews a garment for his son. The key distinction is ownership. For a minor son, ownership transfers at the moment of cutting (assuming the father intends it as a gift), which happens before sewing. Thus, when the father later sews it, it is no longer in his ownership, so he cannot claim the deficiency after discovering a defect. For an adult son, ownership transfers only upon delivery (taslim), so the cutting and sewing occur while the garment is still in the father's ownership. Since the father has already made return impossible due to sewing, he can then give it to the adult son and afterwards claim the deficiency. The passage also discusses cases where the father explicitly declares it a loan (ariyah), which preserves his right to recourse.

Key Terms
التمليك (al-tamlik): The transfer of ownership, which for a minor son occurs by act of cutting (gift), for an adult by delivery.
التسليم (al-taslīm): Physical delivery of the item, which transfers ownership to an adult donee.
الإعارة (al-i'ārah): Loan; the owner lends the item without transferring ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A father buys a piece of cloth, cuts and sews it into a shirt for his 15-year-old son (Hanafi minor) and gives it to him. He then discovers a defect. What is the ruling?
A) He can claim a reduction because he altered it before giving.
B) He cannot claim a reduction because the son owned it at cutting.
C) He can return the shirt to the seller for a full refund.
D) He can claim half the price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between minor and adult children in gift ownership, which directly affects whether a father can claim a price reduction after altering and gifting a defective item.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
15 min
Types of Increases and Their Effect on Return for Defect
Explains classification of increases and effect on return for defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an increase as muttasila/munfasila and mutawallida/ghayr mutawallida, and determine whether it prevents the return of a defective sold item according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Generated connected increases do not prevent return; other types do—due to risk of riba.

In sales, the buyer's right to return a defective item is affected by increases (ziyada) that occur after purchase. The Hanafi school classifies increases along two axes: whether they are connected (muttasila) or separate (munfasila), and whether they are generated from the sold item itself (mutawallida) or not (ghayr mutawallida). This classification determines if the buyer may return the item and how compensation is handled. This lesson walks through the categories and their rulings based on the text of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the increase type: connected or separate?
Determine if the increase is physically attached to the sold item (e.g., planting, building) or separate from it (e.g., offspring, fruit).
2
Check if the increase is generated from the sold item
Ask whether the increase originates from the item itself (e.g., fatness from the animal, milk, fruit) or from an external source (e.g., earnings from labor, black dye).
3
Apply the Hanafi ruling on whether the increase prevents return
Use the following table: Connected non-generated → prevents return; Connected generated → does not prevent return (zahir al-riwayah); Separate generated → prevents return; Separate non-generated → does not prevent return, but there is ikhtilaf on claiming compensation for the increase.
4
Consider the practical effect on the buyer's remedy
If the increase prevents return, the buyer cannot return the item but can claim a reduction in price (arsh) for the defect. If it does not prevent, the buyer may return the item and compensate the seller for the increase if it is connected and non-generated, or keep the increase if separate and non-generated.

He made dyeing absolute, so it includes every color. But in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'Or he dyed it' meaning red; if he dyed it black, likewise according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because black is an increase according to them, while according to Abu Hanifa black is a deficiency, so the seller may take it. In al-Misbah: 'The man mixed the sawiq, mixing from the category of qatala, moistening it with some water, which is lighter than the bas.' The author indicated that the increase connected to the sold item that is not generated from the origin prevents return, such as planting, building, grinding wheat, etc. Unlike if he ate some of it, then he recourses for the deficiency and returns what remains. The difference, as in al-Walwaljiyyah, is that by eating the contract becomes confirmed and its rulings become confirmed; by selling ownership is severed and its rulings are severed. He said: So it becomes like the case where someone buys two slaves, takes possession of them, sells one, then finds a defect in both: he returns the remaining one and does not recourse for the deficiency of what he sold by consensus. So likewise here regarding meat and bread of flour. In Fath al-Qadir: 'And regarding the grinding and such being among the connected increase, there is consideration.' He qualified it because the connected increase that is generated, such as fatness, beauty, and clearing of the white of the eye, does not prevent return for defect in the apparent narration because it is purely incidental to the original, being generated from it without separation, so rescission does not apply to an increase at all. He did not discuss the separate increase in its two types, generated and non-generated. So the generated type, such as offspring, milk, fruit in the sale of trees, and the arsh (compensation) and 'aqr (dower), prevents return like non-generated connected increase, because rescission is not possible on it. In Fath al-Qadir: 'So the buyer has an option before possession: if he wishes, he returns both; if he wishes, he is satisfied with both for the full price. After possession, he returns only the sold item but with a portion of the price by dividing the price over its value at the time of contract and the value of the increase at the time of possession. If its value is one thousand and the value of the increase is one hundred and the price is one thousand, one-tenth of the price falls upon return and he takes nine hundred.' This is inconsistent with his earlier statement that it prevents return, for how can he say that before possession he can return both, and after possession he returns only the sold item? In any case, return is not prevented. This report would only be suitable if we said it does not prevent return. In al-Bazzaziyyah: If the increase occurs after possession and he discovers a defect with the seller, if it is a separate increase generated from the origin, it prevents return and he recourses for the share of the defect unless they mutually agree on return, which would be like a new sale. As for what is in Fath al-Qadir, he mentioned it in al-Bazzaziyyah regarding when the increase occurs before possession and then he discovers a defect. If the discovery is before possession, he has the option as mentioned; if after possession, he returns only the sold item with its share of the price. In al-Sughra: The separate increase prevents return by consensus. Does it prevent the claim for recovery? There is a difference: according to Muhammad, he can claim recovery; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'anduhuma refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? But here it says 'indahu yastariddu wa 'indahuma la', so 'anduhuma likely means the two companions other than Muhammad? Actually standard: Muhammad says he can recover, Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa say no. But the text is ambiguous. In al-Walwaljiyyah: The interpretation of 'aqr is her dower like her, according to some; some say one-tenth of her value if a virgin, half one-tenth if a non-virgin. Before that it was mentioned that the separate increase prevents return for defect after possession and all other causes of rescission like iqalah and return by option of sight etc. In al-Qunyah: 'The increase: Muhammad.' His statement: (It is a mistake because it is not appropriate) etc. He said in al-Nahr: 'And I say: Rather he is the one mistaken, for its meaning is that it prevents the return of the origin alone, unlike the non-generated. This is clarified in al-'Inayah where he said: 'Non-generated like earnings does not prevent, but the way is to rescind the contract regarding the origin without the increase and the increase is given to the buyer for free, unlike the offspring. The difference is that earnings are not sold at all because they are generated from benefits, and benefits are not substances, while offspring is generated from the sold item so it has the ruling of the sold item, so it is not permissible to give it to him for free because of usury. The details between before possession and after are mentioned in al-Bazzaziyyah and others.' Al-Ramli said: 'I say: His statement 'rather he is the one mistaken' is the mistake, for there is no clarification in al-'Inayah about it. Rather the difference between generated and non-generated is that since the generated has the ruling of the sold item, return is prevented because if return were allowed with it, the origin would be returned without the increase, which is not permissible due to usury. Unlike the non-generated, because it does not have the ruling of the sold item, being generated from benefits, and benefits are not self-subsisting, unlike substances, so they differ in ruling. Thus the generated is a preventer for this reason.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of earlier concepts regarding types of increases (ziyada) and their effect on the right to return a defective item. It first addresses a sub-issue: dyeing black vs red. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), black dye is an increase, so it prevents return; according to Abu Hanifa, black dye is a deficiency, so the seller may take the item back. Then it discusses the classification of increases. Connected increase (muttasila) that is not generated (ghayr mutawallida) like planting, building, grinding prevents return. Connected increase that is generated (mutawallida) like fatness does not prevent return in the apparent narration. Separate increase (munfasila) that is generated (e.g., offspring, milk, fruit) prevents return like connected non-generated increase. There is also discussion of the option before and after possession. The passage corrects a statement in Fath al-Qadir, noting inconsistency, and cites al-Bazzaziyyah for clarification. It also addresses the difference between Muhammad and the two companions regarding whether the buyer can recover the increase when return is prevented. Finally, al-Ramli critiques a scholar's overcorrection, explaining the true difference: generated increases have the ruling of the sold item, so returning the origin without them would involve usury; non-generated increases do not have this ruling.

Key Terms
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyāda al-muttaṣila): An increase that is physically connected to the sold item, such as planting, building, grinding.
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyāda al-munfaṣila): An increase that is separate from the sold item, such as offspring, milk, fruit, earnings.
الزيادة المتولدة (al-ziyāda al-mutawallida): An increase that is generated from the sold item itself, like offspring from an animal, fruit from a tree.
الزيادة غير المتولدة (al-ziyāda ghayr al-mutawallida): An increase that is not generated from the sold item, such as earnings from labor or separate acquisitions.
العقر (al-‘aqr): Compensation for a slave woman who has been deflowered, often estimated as a portion of her value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Connected increase that is generated, like fatness.
B) Connected increase that is not generated, like planting.
C) Separate increase that is generated, like offspring.
D) Separate increase that is not generated, like earnings.

Key Takeaway
The classification of an increase as muttasila/munfasila and mutawallida/ghayr mutawallida determines whether it prevents the return of a defective item, with the underlying rationale being prevention of usury.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Increases Before Delivery in the Option of Defect
Classifies increases and states how each type affects the right to return a defective item before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify increases before delivery into four types and apply the Hanafi ruling for each type regarding the right to return a defective item.

Core Idea
Only conjoined non-generated increases before delivery block the right to return a defective item.

When a sold item increases before the buyer takes delivery, does that affect the buyer's right to return the item if a defect is later discovered? The Hanafi school classifies increases into four categories — conjoined or separate, generated or non-generated — each with a distinct ruling. This lesson unpacks those categories and their practical consequences, including a well-known difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Classify the four types of increase
Identify the two axes: conjoined (muttasil) vs. separate (munfasil), and each can be generated (mutawallid) from the item or non-generated. This gives four types.
Generated means natural produce; non-generated means external additions like a gift or sewing.
2
Apply the default rule: before delivery, conjoined non-generated prevents return
The text states that only conjoined non-generated increases (e.g., sewing fabric) prevent the buyer from returning the defective original. All other three types do not prevent return.
This is the core principle.
3
Trace the sub-rulings for separate increases
For generated separate increases (e.g., picked fruit), the buyer may return both or keep both at the full price. For non-generated separate increases (e.g., a gift), the buyer may return the original and keep the increase without paying, but the increase is not legally permissible for him.
These details depend on whether delivery has occurred.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on non-generated separate increases
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer returns only the original; the two companions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say the increase must be returned as well because it occurred before delivery. This affects the buyer's obligation.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.
5
Apply the consensus rule: if the increase perishes, the item is returned for full price
If the separate increase is destroyed (e.g., the gift is lost) and the original has a defect, the buyer returns the original for the full price by consensus.
This is a corollary of the increase having no share of the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المبيع إما قبل القبض أو بعده، وكل منهما على أربعة أوجه متصلة ومنفصلة وكل منهما إما متولدة أم لا . فأما قبل القبض فالمتصلة المتولدة لا تمنع والمتصلة غير المتولدة تمنع، وأما المنفصلة المتولدة لا تمنع فإن شاء ردهما أو رضى بهما بجميع الثمن. ولو وجد بالزيادة عيباً لا يردها إلا إذا أوجب نقصاناً في المبيع فله خيار الرد لنقصان المبيع، ولو قبض الزيادة والأصل ثم وجد بالمبيع عيباً برده بحصته من الثمن لأنه صار حصة للزيادة بعد قبضها، ولو وجد بها عيباً خاصة يردها خاصة بحصتها من الثمن. وأما المنفصلة التي لم تتولد منه كالهبة والصدقة والكسب فلا تمنع الرد فإذا رده فالزيادة للمشتري بغير ثمن عند أبي حنيفة ولا تطيب له. ولو قبض المبيع مع هذه الزيادة ووجد بالمبيع عيباً فعند أبي حنيفة يرد المبيع خاصة، وعندهما يرد مع الزيادة لأنها حدثت قبل القبض. ولو وجد بالزيادة عيباً يردها لأن لا حصة لها من الثمن فلو ردها لردها بغير شيء، ولو هلكت خاصة الزيادة والمبيع بعيب يرده بجميع الثمن بالإجماع.
Concerning the sold item, either before delivery or after, and each of them has four aspects: conjoined and separate, and each of them is either generated or not. As for before delivery: the generated conjoined does not prevent, and the non-generated conjoined prevents. As for the generated separate, it does not prevent; so if he wishes, he returns both, or is satisfied with them for the entire price. If he finds a defect in the increase, he does not return it unless it causes a deficiency in the sold item, then he has the option to return due to the deficiency of the sold item. If he takes delivery of the increase and the original, then finds a defect in the sold item, he returns it for its share of the price because a portion has been allotted to the increase after its receipt. If he finds a defect specifically in the increase, he returns it alone for its share of the price. As for the separate that is not generated from it, like gift, charity, and earning, it does not prevent return. So if he returns, the increase belongs to the buyer without price according to Abu Hanifa, and it is not permissible for him. If he takes delivery of the sold item with this increase and finds a defect in the sold item, then according to Abu Hanifa he returns the sold item alone, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he returns with the increase because it occurred before delivery. If he finds a defect in the increase, he returns it because it has no share of the price; so if he were to return it, he would return it for nothing. If the increase alone perishes and the sold item has a defect, he returns it for the entire price by consensus.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement expanding on the classification of increases (زيادة) in the context of the option of defect (خيار العيب) before delivery. The author categorizes increases into four types: conjoined generated, conjoined non-generated, separate generated, and separate non-generated. For before delivery, conjoined non-generated increases (like sewing fabric) prevent the buyer from returning the defective original; the other three types do not prevent return. Detailed sub-rulings are given: if the increase has a defect, it cannot be returned separately unless it also reduces the original's value; if the buyer already took delivery of both, the return is apportioned. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is noted regarding non-generated separate increases (e.g., gift, earning): Abu Hanifa says the buyer returns only the original, while the two say the increase must be returned with it because it arose before delivery. The passage concludes with consensus on returning the full price if the increase perishes.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyādah): An increase or addition that occurs to the sold item, either before or after delivery, and can be conjoined (متصل) or separate (منفصل), and generated (متولد) or not.
متصل (muttasil): An increase that is physically attached to the original item, such as sewing a garment or fattening an animal.
منفصل (munfasil): An increase that is separate from the original item, such as fruits picked, milk, or earnings from the item.
متولد (mutawallid): An increase that naturally originates from the sold item, such as milk, offspring, or fruit from a tree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a buyer takes delivery of a defective garment and also receives a separate gift (non-generated increase) before delivery, what happens to the gift upon returning the garment?
A) The buyer must return the gift together with the garment.
B) The buyer keeps the gift but must pay its value.
C) The buyer returns only the garment and keeps the gift without payment, but it is not permissible for him.
D) The buyer returns only the garment and the gift belongs to him validly.

Key Takeaway
Only conjoined non-generated increases before delivery block the right to return a defective item; other increases do not, and the buyer may keep separate non-generated increases without paying, though they are not halal without compensation.

Compensation for Defect After Slave's Death or Emancipation
States the ruling on claiming compensation after death or emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on claiming compensation for a defect after a slave's death or emancipation, distinguishing between qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
Emancipation is like death for claiming defect compensation: the slave was never permanently ownable.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in sales law: a buyer purchases a slave, later discovers a defect, but the slave has already died or been emancipated. The Hanafi school rules that the buyer cannot return the item (since ownership ended) but can claim compensation for the defect. The rationale differs for death and emancipation, and involves a departure from strict analogy through istihsan. Understanding this ruling illustrates how Hanafi fiqh balances textual rules and equitable reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Recognize that the buyer discovers a defect in a slave after ownership has ended due to death or the buyer's own act of emancipation.
The slave is no longer in the buyer's possession, so return is impossible.
2
Understand the rule for death
Death naturally ends ownership. Since the buyer did not cause the death, compensation is allowed.
The prevention of return is legal (ḥukmī), not by the buyer's action.
3
Analyze the qiyas for emancipation
By strict qiyas, emancipation is like killing the slave: both are voluntary acts by the buyer that end ownership, so compensation should be barred.
The asl is killing the slave (no compensation), the far' is emancipation.
4
Apply istihsan to override qiyas
Istihsan considers that humans are not naturally ownable; ownership is temporary until emancipation, making emancipation a natural termination like death.
Thus, compensation is allowed despite the buyer's act.
5
Derive the Hanafi position
The mufta bihi position is that the buyer claims compensation (naqṣān) in both death and emancipation cases.
This ruling is based on istihsan and is specific to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أو مات العبد أو أعتقه) يعني يرجع بالنقصان إذا اطلع على عيب به بعد موته أو إعتاقه، أما الموت فلأن الملك انتهى به والامتناع حكمي لا بفعله وأما الإعتاق فالقياس أن لا يرجع لأن امتناع الرد بفعله فصار كالقتل وفي الاستحسان يرجع لأن العتق إنهاء الملك لأن الآدمي ما خلق في الأصل محلاً للملك وإنما يثبت فيه الملك موقتاً إلى الإعتاق فكان إنهاء كالموت.
His saying: (or the slave dies or he emancipates him) means he (the buyer) claims compensation for the deficiency if he becomes aware of a defect in him after his death or his emancipation. As for death, because ownership ends with it, and the prevention of return is legal, not by his action. As for emancipation, by analogy (qiyas) he should not claim compensation because the prevention of return is by his own action, so it is like killing. But by juristic preference (istihsan), he claims compensation because emancipation is a termination of ownership, for the human being was not originally created as a subject of ownership; rather, ownership is established in him temporarily until emancipation, so it is a termination like death.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased slave after the slave has died or been emancipated. The ruling is that the buyer cannot return the slave (since ownership has ended), but he can claim compensation for the reduction in value due to the defect. The reasoning differs for death and emancipation. Death naturally ends ownership, and the buyer's inability to return is not his fault, so compensation is straightforward. For emancipation, the buyer himself caused the ownership to end, so by strict analogy (qiyas) he should forfeit his right to compensation, just as if he killed the slave. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) and allows compensation because emancipation is considered a legitimate termination of ownership, akin to death. The key principle is that humans are not inherently ownable; ownership is temporary and ends upon emancipation, so the act of emancipation is like a natural termination rather than a wrongful act like killing.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): A method of legal reasoning that departs from strict analogy for a more equitable result, here used to allow compensation after emancipation.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning, here used to compare emancipation to killing, leading to a bar on compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school allow the buyer to claim compensation for a defect after emancipating the slave, despite the buyer's own act ending ownership?
A) Because emancipation is an act of worship that expiates sin.
B) Because by istihsan, emancipation is considered a termination of ownership like death, since humans were not created to be owned permanently.
C) Because the seller waived his right to object by remaining silent.
D) Because the defect existed before the sale, so the seller is always liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer can claim compensation for a defect after a slave's death or emancipation because istihsan treats emancipation as a natural termination of ownership, overriding the analogy to killing.

Ruling on Separate Possession and Post-Possession Increase in Returning Defective Goods
States two rulings: separate possession allows returning only the defective item; post-possession increase prevents return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning defective items when possession was taken separately, and explain why post-possession increase prevents return.

Core Idea
Separate possession permits itemized return; post-possession increase bars return.

In the chapter on option of defect (khiyar al-‘ayb), the Hanafi school addresses two practical scenarios: when a buyer takes possession of two items at different times, and when the purchased item generates an increase after delivery. This lesson clarifies both rulings from the classical text, focusing on how possession and subsequent changes affect the buyer's right to return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note that the passage covers two distinct cases: (1) two items bought in one contract, possession taken separately, defect found in one; (2) an item that gives birth or produces milk after possession.
These are separate rulings, but both involve the buyer's right to return defective goods.
2
Analyze the first scenario: separate possession
Since possession was taken separately, each item is considered independently. A defect in one item does not affect the other; the buyer returns only the defective item and recovers its portion of the price.
The key condition is that possession was separated in time.
3
Analyze the second scenario: post-possession increase
If the purchased animal gives birth or produces milk after the buyer has taken possession, this increase (ziyada) prevents the buyer from returning the original item, even if a defect is discovered. The increase becomes the buyer's property and is inseparable from the item.
Milk is treated like offspring in this ruling.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The increase arising after possession is a new property right belonging to the buyer. Returning the defective item would also require returning the increase, which is not permissible because the increase is already owned by the buyer. Thus, the right to return is blocked.
This is a preventive factor (mani') that nullifies the option of defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قبضهما فوجد بأحدهما عيباً يرده بحصته من الثمن لأنه قبضهما متفرقاً، ولو ولدت بعد القبض لا يرد لأن الزيادة الحادثة بعد القبض تمنع الرد واللبن كالولد اهـ.
When he takes possession of both [items] and finds in one a defect, he returns it with its portion of the price because he took possession of them separately. And if it gives birth after possession, he does not return [the defective one] because the increase occurring after possession prevents return, and milk is like the offspring.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect). It addresses two scenarios. First: if someone buys two items in one contract but takes possession of them separately, and later finds a defect in one, he may return the defective item only (not both) and recover its corresponding part of the price. Second: if the purchased animal gives birth after the buyer has taken possession, this post-possession increase (the offspring) prevents the buyer from returning the mother due to the defect. Similarly, milk produced after possession also prevents return. The principle: any increase (ziyada) arising after possession blocks the right to return the original item, because the increase is considered a separate property right that would be affected by returning the defective item.

Key Terms
الزيادة الحادثة بعد القبض (al-ziyāda al-ḥāditha ba‘d al-qabḍ): An increase (such as offspring or milk) that arises in the purchased item after the buyer has taken physical possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two sheep in one contract, takes possession of them one day apart, then finds a defect in the first sheep. What is the ruling?
A) He must return both sheep and get full refund
B) He returns only the defective sheep and gets its portion of the price
C) He cannot return either sheep because possession was separate
D) He can return the defective sheep only if he returns both

Key Takeaway
When possession is separate, defect affects only the specific item; post-possession increase blocks return entirely.

Classification of Increases and Their Effect on the Right of Return
Classifies increases and their effect on return rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify increases as connected/disconnected and generated/non-generated, and state the Hanafi ruling on their effect on the right to return (radd) for defect.

Core Idea
A connected generated increase does not prevent return; a disconnected generated increase does.

This passage from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn systematically classifies increases (ziyada) that may arise after a sale. Understanding this classification is crucial for khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect), because the presence of certain increases either preserves or extinguishes the buyer's right to return the defective item. The text also highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the right to compensation when a connected generated increase is present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main classification axes
The text divides increases into connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila). Each type is further divided into generated (mutawallida) and non-generated (ghayr mutawallida). Understand the definitions: connected means physically attached (e.g., fattening, growth), disconnected means separate (e.g., fruit, offspring). Generated means arising from the item itself (e.g., milk, offspring), non-generated means external addition (e.g., paint, extra fruit from another tree).
2
Apply the ruling: which increases prevent return?
The text states: (1) Connected non-generated: prevents return by agreement. (2) Connected generated: does NOT prevent return in the apparent transmission (zāhir al-riwāya). (3) Disconnected generated: prevents return. (4) Disconnected non-generated: does not prevent return. For each category, note the specific ruling and whether it applies to return (radd) or annulment (faskh).
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on connected generated increase
While connected generated increase does not prevent return, there is a dispute about whether the buyer may also claim compensation (rujū‘ bi-naqṣ) when returning. According to Imam Muhammad, the buyer may do so; according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Sahibayn), he may not. The text declares the correct view (as-sahih) as that connected does not prevent return, implying Muhammad's view is preferred.
4
Consider special cases: slave-girl birth vs. animal birth
The passage gives a specific rule: birth of a slave-girl (a disconnected generated increase) prevents return of the mother even if the child dies, because human birth is a significant change. For animals, if the offspring dies, the mother can still be returned because the change is not permanent. Also, birth nullifies khiyar al-ru’ya and khiyar al-shart unless the birth itself causes a decrease in value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : اعلم أن الزيادة نوعان : منفصلة ومتصلة وكل منهما متولدة أولاً، فالمتصلة التي لم تتولد تمنع الرد وفاقاً وإن قبله البائع وله الرجوع بنقصه والمتصلة المتولدة لا تمنع الرد في ظاهر الرواية، فإن أراد المشتري الرجوع بنقصه رده فله ذلك عند محمد لا عندهما، والمنفصلة المتولدة تمنع الرد وكذا تمنع الفسخ بسائر أسباب الفسخ والمنفصلة التي لم تتولد لا تمنع الرد والفسخ بسائر أسباب الفسخ ثم قال : الصحيح أن المتصلة لا تمنع الرد بالعيب، ولا فرق في كون الولد مانعاً من الرد بين ما إذا اشتراها حاملاً أو حائلاً فولدت عنده، ولدت الأمة امتنع ردها بعيب سواء هلك الولد أو لا بخلاف غيرها حيث لا يمنع رد الأم بعيب إذا هلك الولد إذ الولادة لا تنقص في غير بنات آدم، ولو شرى أمة حاملاً فولدت زال العيب ثم قال : خيار الرؤية والشرط يبطل بولادة الأمة مات الولد أو لا، والولد الميت والبيضة الفاسدة لا تبطل الخيار إلا إذا نقصت بالولادة .اهـ.
And in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: Know that increase is of two types: disconnected and connected, and each type is initially generated. The connected that is not generated prevents return by agreement, even if the seller accepted it, and he may claim compensation for the defect. The connected generated increase does not prevent return in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). If the buyer wants to claim compensation for the defect, he may, according to Muhammad, not according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa). The disconnected generated increase prevents return, and similarly prevents annulment by other causes. The disconnected non-generated increase does not prevent return or annulment by other causes. Then he said: The correct view is that the connected does not prevent return due to defect. There is no difference in the offspring preventing return between buying her pregnant or non-pregnant and she gives birth with him. If a slave-girl gives birth, her return due to defect is prevented whether the child lives or not, unlike other [animals] where the return of the mother due to defect is not prevented if the child dies, because birth does not decrease value in non-human beings. If he buys a pregnant slave-girl and she gives birth, the defect is removed. Then he said: The option of sight and condition are nullified by the birth of a slave-girl, whether the child dies or not. The dead child and the rotten egg do not nullify the option unless there is a decrease due to birth.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn that systematically classifies increases (ziyada) that can occur after a sale. The author divides increase into two types: connected (muttasila) like fattening, and disconnected (munfasila) like fruit. Each type may be either generated (mutawallida, i.e., arising from the item itself, like offspring) or non-generated (ghayr mutawallida, like an external addition). The rulings: (1) Connected but non-generated increase (e.g., fattening) prevents return unanimously, but the buyer may claim compensation. (2) Connected generated increase (e.g., growth of an organ) does not prevent return in the zāhir al-riwāya; here there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad allows returning the item and claiming compensation, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not. (3) Disconnected generated increase (e.g., offspring) prevents return and also prevents annulment for other defects. (4) Disconnected non-generated increase (e.g., fruit on a tree) does not prevent return. The passage then discusses specific cases: a slave-girl's birth always prevents return of the mother, even if the child dies, because human childbirth is considered a significant change. For animals, however, if the offspring dies, the mother can still be returned (since the change is not permanent). The text also notes that if a pregnant slave-girl gives birth, this may remove her defect. Finally, it addresses khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart: birth of a slave-girl terminates those options unless there is a decrease in value due to the birth itself. A dead fetus or rotten egg does not terminate the option.

Key Terms
الزيادة المنفصلة (az-ziyāda al-munfaṣila): An increase that is physically separate from the original item, such as offspring or fruit.
الزيادة المتصلة (az-ziyāda al-muttaṣila): An increase that is physically attached to the original item, such as fattening or growth.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāya): The well-known transmitted opinion from Imam Muhammad's six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent transmission (zāhir al-riwāya), what is the effect of a connected generated increase (e.g., the natural growth of a limb) on the right to return a defective item?
A) It prevents return completely
B) It does not prevent return
C) It prevents return unless the buyer accepts compensation
D) It only prevents return if the increase is substantial

Key Takeaway
A connected generated increase does not prevent return; a disconnected generated increase does; the intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether the buyer may also claim compensation when returning.

Effect of Transformative Alteration on Buyer's Right to Recover Price
States the ruling on sewing vs cutting alone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when cutting and sewing a garment prevents the buyer from recovering the price upon istihqaq, and distinguish it from cutting without sewing.

Core Idea
Sewing is transformative; cutting alone is not—only transformative alteration forfeits the buyer's right to the price.

This lesson clarifies a key principle in Hanafi sale law: the buyer's act of physically altering the purchased item can affect his legal remedies. Specifically, if a garment is cut and sewn, the buyer loses the right to claim back the price from the seller if the garment is later claimed by its true owner. However, if the buyer only cut the fabric without sewing, the right to recover the price remains. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the concept of istihlak (consumption) in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Read the passage and note the two scenarios: cutting+sewing vs cutting only. Identify what right is affected (the right to recover the price upon istihqaq).
Focus on the consequence, not the act itself.
2
Extract the underlying principle
The passage states: 'sewing... as it prevents return due to defect, similarly prevents claiming its price.' What makes sewing different from cutting? The idea of transformative change (istihlak).
Sewing creates a new product; cutting alone does not.
3
Apply the principle to the stated cases
Case 1: cut and sewn → no price recovery. Case 2: cut only → price recovery. Also note the additional case of partial ownership (some parts belong to different owners).
The ruling is consistent: any nontransformative alteration leaves the buyer's right intact.
4
Contrast with the defect return rule
The passage analogizes to the rule that sewing prevents returning the garment for a defect. This shows the same logic applies to price recovery upon istihqaq.
Both cases involve the buyer having transformed the item, making it impossible to return it in its original state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن خياطة الثوب كما تمنع رده بعيب تمنع الرجوع بثمنه عند استحقاقه، فلو اشترى قميصاً فقطعه وخاطه ثم برهن أن القميص له وقضى له به لم يرجع المشتري بالثمن على بائعه لكونه استحق بسبب حادث كما لو برهن أن الكلم له والآخر أن الدخريص له بخلاف ما إذا قطعه ولم يخطه فبرهن أن القميص له رجع بالثمن، وتمامه في تلخيص الجامع.
Then know that sewing a garment, as it prevents its return due to a defect, similarly prevents claiming its price when it is rightfully claimed [by another]. So if someone bought a shirt, cut it, and sewed it, then proved that the shirt belonged to him and it was adjudged to him, the buyer does not recover the price from his seller because it was rightfully claimed due to an incident, just as if he proved that the garment [or part] belonged to him and the other part belonged to someone else, unlike if he cut it without sewing, and then proved the shirt belonged to him: he recovers the price. Its completion is in Talkhis al-Jami‘.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of physically altering goods after purchase. The general principle: any change that constitutes a significant transformation (istihlāk) prevents the buyer from returning the item for defect. Here, sewing (khayāṭa) is given as an example: if a buyer cuts and sews a garment, this act is considered a transformative change. Therefore, if the buyer later discovers a defect or finds that the garment was stolen property and the true owner reclaims it, the buyer cannot sue the seller for the price. The buyer's act of sewing is an 'incident' (ḥādith) that severs his right to recover. In contrast, if the buyer only cut the fabric but did not sew it—that is, a non-transformative alteration—he retains the right to reclaim the price from the seller. The difference is that cutting alone is reversible, while sewing creates a new product. The ruling also covers the case where part of the garment is owned by one claimant and another part by another: similar logic applies.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): When a third party proves legal ownership of the purchased item, obligating its return to the true owner.
خياطة (khayāṭa): Sewing; in this context, a transformative alteration that prevents returning the garment in its original state.
حادث (ḥādith): Incident; here, the buyer's act of sewing that occurs after the sale and before the claim, which severs the buyer's right to recover the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a shirt, cuts it up but does not sew it, then discovers it belonged to another. What is the ruling?
A) He cannot recover the price because he altered it
B) He can recover the price from the seller
C) He must return the cut fabric to the true owner with no compensation
D) He can recover the price only if he returns the fabric to the seller

Key Takeaway
Transformative alteration (like sewing) forfeits the buyer's right to recover the price upon istihqaq; cutting alone does not.

7 min
Death or Emancipation: Buyer's Right to Compensation for Defect
Explains that death or emancipation does not bar defect compensation by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on defect compensation after death or emancipation of a slave and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Emancipation ends ownership like death, preserving the buyer's right to compensation by istihsan.

When a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after the slave dies or is freed, does the right to compensation survive? By strict analogy (qiyas), emancipation bars the claim because the buyer caused the impediment. However, Hanafi jurists prefer istihsan, treating emancipation like a natural termination of ownership. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the subsidiary dispute about multiple sellers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the effect of death or emancipation on the buyer's right to claim compensation (naqs) for a pre-existing defect.
The right exists at sale but may be lost if the buyer prevents return.
2
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) argument against compensation
By qiyas to killing the slave, emancipation is an act by the buyer that prevents return, so the right should be lost.
Killing = buyer's act causing total loss of object.
3
Examine the istihsan (juristic preference) reasoning
Istihsan treats emancipation like death: both end ownership definitively. Humans are not naturally ownable; slavery is temporary. Thus, the buyer's virtuous act does not forfeit the right.
The proof: the emancipator gains wala' (clientage), a trace of ownership.
4
Trace the ruling's proof (dalil)
The establishment of wala' proves original ownership, so the right to compensation remains even after ownership ends.
Wala' is an effect of ownership, indicating the owner's link persists.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on chain of sellers
When the buyer resells and the second buyer discovers a defect after the slave dies, Abu Hanifa denies the first buyer's recourse against his seller, while the disciples allow it.
Practical impact: chain of claims may be blocked by Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أو مات العبد أو أعتقه) يعني يرجع بالنقصان إذا اطلع على عيب به يعد موته أو إعتاقه، أما الموت فلأن الملك انتهى به والامتناع حكمي لا بفعله وأما الإعتاق فالقياس أن لا يرجع لأن امتناع الرد بفعله فصار كالقتل وفي الاستحسان يرجع لأن العتق إنهاء الملك لأن الآدمي ما خلق في الأصل محلاً للملك وإنما يثبت فيه الملك موقتاً إلى الإعتاق فكان إنهاء كالموت. وهذا لأن الشيء يتقرر بانتهائه فيجعل كأن الملك باق والرد متعذر، والدليل على ثبوت أصل الملك ثبوت الولاء للمعتق وهو أثر من آثار الملك. وفي الصغرى المشتري إذا باع من غيره فمات في يد الثاني ثم إطلع على عيب رجع بنقصان العيب على المشتري الأول، وليس للمشتري الأول أن يرجع على بائعه الأول بنقصان العيب عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما حتى لو صالح المشتري الأول مع بائعه عن ذلك على شيء لا يصح عند أبي حنيفة لأنه لا حق له اهـ؛ كذا في الكافي.
His statement: 'or the slave dies or he emancipates him' means he recovers compensation for the defect if he discovers a defect after his death or emancipation. As for death, because ownership ended with it, and prevention [of return] is legal, not by his act. As for emancipation, by analogy he should not recover because the prevention of return is by his act, so it is like killing. But by istihsan he recovers because emancipation ends ownership, for man is not originally a locus for ownership; ownership is established in him temporarily until emancipation, so ending it is like death. This is because a thing is confirmed by its end, so ownership is treated as if remaining and return is impossible. The proof of the original ownership is the establishment of wala' for the emancipator, which is an effect of ownership. And in al-Sughra: if the buyer sold [the slave] to another and he died in the latter's hand, then the latter discovered a defect, he recovers compensation for the defect from the first buyer. And the first buyer does not recover from his first seller for the defect according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), such that if the first buyer settles with his seller for something, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because he has no right. This is in al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the text 'or the slave dies or he emancipates him' from the matn. It clarifies that these events do not negate the buyer's right to compensation (naqs) for a pre-existing defect. The reasoning differs for death and emancipation. Death: the buyer did not cause it; it's a natural termination of ownership, so the buyer can still claim compensation. Emancipation: the buyer's own act caused the impediment to return, so by qiyas (analogy with killing), he should lose the right. But the author invokes istihsan (juristic preference): emancipation ends ownership just like death, because humans are not inherently ownable; their slavery is temporary until emancipation. Thus, the buyer is not penalized for exercising a right. The effect is to treat ownership as if it still existed for the purpose of defect compensation. The proof: the emancipator gains wala' (clientage) from the freed slave, which is a trace of ownership. The passage also addresses multiplicity of sellers: if the original buyer resells the slave and the second buyer discovers a defect after the slave dies, the second buyer can get compensation from the first buyer. But Abu Hanifa holds that the first buyer cannot then claim from his original seller, because he (the first buyer) is not considered to have a right (since he sold the slave knowingly? The text says 'because he has no right' which might mean his sale implied acceptance). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ, allowing the chain of recourse. A settlement by the first buyer with his original seller is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): A juristic preference overriding qiyas due to a stronger evidence or equity consideration.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared effective cause (illa).
الولاء (al-walā'): Clientage: the legal bond between the emancipator and freed slave, entitling the former to inherit under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer discovers a defect in a slave after he has emancipated him. What is the ruling according to istihsan?
A) He cannot claim compensation because he caused the impediment
B) He can claim compensation as if the slave were still owned
C) He can only claim if he had not seen the defect before emancipation
D) He must return the slave and cannot emancipate

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, emancipation does not forfeit the buyer's right to defect compensation because it is legally analogous to death, as proven by the wala' relationship.

Effect of Kitaba, Tadbir, and Istilad on Right to Return for Defect
Explains ruling and reasoning for tadbir, istilad, kitaba affecting return for defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effects of tadbir, istilad, and kitaba on the right to return for defect and apply the ruling to scenarios involving a maktab buyer.

Core Idea
Tadbir and istilad block return like full emancipation; kitaba blocks return but allows compensation only for umm walad.

In earlier lessons, you learned the general rule that a buyer can return a defective item. But what if the buyer himself is a slave with a kitaba contract, or the item is a mudabbar or umm walad? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the buyer's own emancipation status can affect his right to return, with different implications for tadbir, istilad, and kitaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three emancipation-related statuses
Recognize tadbir (postponed emancipation until master's death), istilad (slave-girl bearing child to master), and kitaba (contract to purchase freedom).
These all create partial or future freedom, affecting the slave's transferability.
2
Understand the core principle for tadbir and istilad
These are like full emancipation: the slave cannot be sold or transferred, so physical return is impossible. The buyer can only claim compensation (defect in value).
The ʿilla is that the 'ayn (object) cannot be returned because ownership is effectively severed.
3
Analyze kitaba: special case
Kitaba allows the maktab to be sold with his consent, so return is not impossible. However, kitaba prevents the maktab from exercising the right to return for certain purchases.
The maktab is considered to have accepted the ownership due to his kitaba status.
4
Distinguish between two sub-cases under kitaba
Case 1: Maktab buys his father or son → no return and no compensation. Case 2: Maktab buys his own umm walad → no return but compensation is allowed.
The ʿilla for compensation in umm walad is the special bond of motherhood.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Test your understanding: If a maktab buys a defective item that is not a close relative or umm walad, what is the ruling? The passage suggests he might be able to return if the master sells him, etc.
Use the principles of transferability and kitaba's effect on ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : ما المانع من جعله من آثار العتق؟ ولم يذكر المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى توابع الإعتاق وفيها تفصيل فالتدبير والاستيلاد كالعتق لتعذر النقل مع بقاء المحل بالأمر الحكمي، وأما الكتابة فمانعة من الرجوع لجواز النقل لجواز بيعه برضاه وتعجيزه نفسه فصار بها حابساً كالإعتاق على مال وقيد في السراج والوهاج بأداء بدل الكتابة ليعتق ليصير عتقاً على مال اهـ. وفي المحيط مكاتب اشترى أباه أو ابنه لا يرده بالعيب لأنه صار مكاتباً والكتابة تمنع زوال الملك بسائر الأسباب فكذلك الفسخ، ولا يرجع بنقصانه لأن الرجوع بالنقصان خلف عن الرد بدليل أنه لا يصار إليه مع القدرة على الرد وإنما يثبت الخلف إذا وقع اليأس عن الأصل ولم يقع لقبولها الفسخ بخلاف ما إذا دبره ثم وجد به عيباً، فإن عجز المكاتب بعدما علم بالعيب رده المولى ويتولاه المكاتب لزوال المانع، فإن باعه المولى أو مات المكاتب رده المولى بنفسه كالوكيل إذا مات فإن أبرأه المكاتب قبل العجز لا يرده المولى، وإن أبرأه المولى قبل عجز المكاتب جاز. ولو اشترى المكاتب أم ولده ومعها ولدها لا يردها بالعيب ويرجع بنقصانه، ولو أبرأه المكاتب جاز. ولو اشترى المولى من مكاتبه عبداً لا بالعيب ولا يخاصم البائع .اهـ.
And it may be said: What prevents making it an effect of emancipation? And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the subsidiaries of emancipation, and they have detail: Tadbir (postponed emancipation) and istilad (bearing a child to the master) are like emancipation because transfer is impossible with the locus remaining through a legal command. As for kitaba (contract of manumission), it prevents recourse because transfer is permissible, since it is permissible to sell him with his consent and his own incapacitation [for inability to pay], so he becomes like a restrainer similar to emancipation for money. It is restricted in al-Siraj and al-Wahhaj to payment of the kitaba price so that he becomes free, making it like emancipation for money. And in al-Muhit: A maktab (slave under kitaba) who buys his father or son does not return him due to defect because he has become a maktab, and kitaba prevents termination of ownership by all causes, similarly it prevents annulment. And he does not claim compensation for the defect because claiming compensation is a substitute for return, as a proof that it is not resorted to when return is possible; rather the substitute is established when there is despair of the original, and there is no despair because the contract accepts annulment? Unlike if he gave tadbir then found a defect: then if the maktab becomes unable after knowing the defect, the master returns him and the maktab takes charge? Because the impediment is removed. If the master sold him or the maktab dies, the master returns him himself like a proxy when he dies. If the maktab releases him before inability, the master does not return him. And if the master releases him before the maktab's inability, it is valid. And if the maktab buys his umm walad (mother of his child) and her child with her, he does not return her due to defect and claims compensation. And if the maktab releases him, it is valid. And if the master buys from his maktab a slave, he does not return due to defect and does not litigate with the seller.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage delves into the effects of different forms of emancipation-related contracts on the buyer's right to return a defective item. The author raises a question: Why isn't tadbir and istilad treated simply as emancipation? He explains that the author (of the matn) did not detail them, but here is the detail. Tadbir (declaration that the slave will be free upon the master's death) and istilad (slave-girl bearing a child to the master) are like full emancipation: they prevent the physical return of the slave because the master's ownership is effectively ended by these acts (the master cannot sell such a slave). Hence, the buyer cannot return the item but can claim compensation. Kitaba (a contract where the slave pays for his freedom in installments) is different: it does not prevent return completely because during kitaba the slave can still be sold (with his consent). Therefore, kitaba prevents the buyer from returning the defective slave because the maktab himself is considered to have accepted the sale (by becoming a maktab). The maktab cannot return a defective purchased item (his father/son) because kitaba prevents termination of ownership (the maktab's ownership is considered stable). However, if the maktab later becomes unable to pay (عجز), the impediment is removed, and the master can return the defective item. The passage also discusses the case of a maktab buying his own umm walad: he cannot return her because she is his own slave? Actually careful: 'buying his umm walad' is odd; it might mean the maktab buys a slave-girl who is the mother of his child (but a maktab can own property). The ruling is he cannot return her due to defect but can claim compensation. Various scenarios of release (ibrā') by maktab or master are also covered. Finally, if the master buys from his maktab, the master cannot return due to defect and cannot sue the seller.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitāba): A contract between master and slave whereby the slave will become free upon paying a specified sum; during kitaba, the slave may still be sold with his consent.
التدبير (at-tadbīr): A declaration by the master that a slave will be free upon the master's death; the slave is then called a mudabbar.
الاستيلاد (al-istīlād): The status of a slave-girl who has borne a child to her master; she becomes an umm walad and cannot be sold.
الرد بالعيب (ar-radd bi al-ʿayb): The buyer's right to return a defective item to the seller and claim a refund.
الرجوع بالنقصان (ar-rujūʿ bi an-nuqṣān): Claiming compensation for the decrease in value due to defect when return is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a maktab buys his own umm walad (mother of his child) and discovers a defect. What is the ruling?
A) He can return her due to defect
B) He cannot return her but can claim compensation
C) He cannot return her nor claim compensation
D) He can return her only if his master agrees

Key Takeaway
The right to return for defect is not absolute; the buyer's own emancipation status (tadbir, istilad, kitaba) can modify it, with kitaba being more nuanced—barring return and compensation for close relatives but allowing compensation for umm walad.

Perishing of Item Does Not Bar Compensation for Defect
Explains that perishing does not bar compensation, unlike emancipation and consumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for perishing, emancipation, and consumption regarding the right to compensation for a defect, and apply the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Perishing allows compensation; emancipation bars if after knowledge; consumption bars except eating per fatwa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical point in sales: when the sold item is destroyed or consumed, does the buyer lose the right to claim compensation for a hidden defect? The answer depends on whether the destruction is accidental (halak) or by the buyer's deliberate act (emancipation, consumption). The passage also resolves an intra-madhhab dispute on eating, with the fatwa according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts
Distinguish between perishing (halak), emancipation (itq), and consumption (istihlak, e.g., eating). Each has a different effect on the right to compensation.
Halak is not attributable to the buyer's choice; itq and istihlak are deliberate.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For halak: compensation is allowed both before and after knowledge. For itq: bars compensation only if after knowledge. For istihlak: generally bars all recourse, except eating according to the two disciples.
The exception for eating is the fatwa.
3
Understand the reasoning
Compensation is a substitute for return (khalaf 'an al-radd). When return is impossible due to the buyer's act, the substitute is not resorted to. But halak is not the buyer's act, so the right remains.
The kitaba discussion reinforces this: when return becomes possible again (maktab unable), the substitute is abandoned.
4
Apply the fatwa
In practice, if a buyer eats defective food, he may claim compensation according to the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The same applies to analogous cases like slaughter by order.
Al-Waqi'at states the fatwa is on their view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال المؤلف أو هلك المبيع ليتناول هلاك غير الآدمي لكان أولى. وفي القنية : اشترى جداراً مائلاً فلم يعلم به حتى سقط فله الرجوع بالنقصان. وفي جامع الفصولين : ذهب به إلى بائعه ليرد بعيبه فهلك في الطريق هلك على المشتري ويرجع بنقصه، وقدمنا حكم ما إذا قضى برده على البائع بعيبه فهلك عند المشتري. والحاصل أن هلاك المبيع ليس كإعتاقه فإنه إذا هلك المبيع يرجع بنقصان العيب، سواء كان بعد العلم به أو قبله. وأما الإعتاق بعد العلم به فمانع من الرجوع بنقصانه بخلافه قبله، وليس الإعتاق كاستهلاكه فإنه استهلكه فلا رجوع مطلقاً إلا في الأكل عندهما، وقيل غير مانع من الرجوع بنقصه أيضاً لوجوب الضمان به فهو كبيعه ؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. وفي جامع الفصولين : ولو شرى بعيراً فلما أدخله في داره سقط فذبحه رجل بأمر المشتري فظهر عيبه يرجع بنقصه وبه أخذ المشايخ كما لو أكل طعاماً، ولو علم عيبه قبل الذبح فذبحه هو بأمره لا اهـ. وفي الواقعات: الفتوى على قولهما في الأكل فكذا هنا وفيه : ولو اشترى عندهما، يرجع يرده أو غيره أقول : قوله بعد رؤية العيب يعني ما لم يوجد منه ما يدل على الرضا به. قوله: (لأن الرجوع بالنقصان خلف عن الرد الخ) هذا التعليل يفيد عدم اشتراط أداء البدل كما لا يخفى ولذا قال في النهر قال الشارح : ولو عجز المكاتب ينبغي أن يرده بالعيب لزوال المانع كما لو اطلع على عيب في العبد الآبق لا يرجع بشيء لأن الرجوع خلف عن الرد فلا يصار إلى الخلف ما دام حياً فإذا رجع رده لزوال المانع، وبه اندفع ما في السراج من تقييد الكتابة بأداء بدلها ليصير كالعتق على مال إذ لو صح
And if the author had said 'or the sold item perishes' to include non-human destruction, it would have been better. In al-Qunyah: He bought a leaning wall and did not know of its defect until it fell; he has the right to claim compensation. In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: He took it to his seller to return it due to a defect, and it perished en route; the loss is upon the buyer and he claims compensation. And we have already presented the ruling when it was adjudged to be returned to the seller due to a defect and it perished with the buyer. The upshot is that perishing of the sold item is not like emancipating it: if the sold item perishes, he claims compensation for the defect, whether after knowledge of it or before. As for emancipation after knowledge, it prevents claiming compensation, unlike before. And emancipation is not like consuming it (istihlak), for if he consumes it, he has no recourse absolutely except in the case of eating according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). It is said that [emancipation] does not prevent claiming compensation because guarantee is obligatory, so it is like selling it. This is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: If he bought a camel, and when he brought it into his house it fell, and a man slaughtered it by the buyer's command, and then the defect appeared, he claims compensation; the mashayikh adopted this, just as if he ate food. And if he knew of the defect before slaughter and he ordered him, then no [claim]. In al-Waqi‘at: The fatwa is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding eating, so too here. And in it: If he bought, according to the two, he returns, he returns it or other? I say: His statement 'after seeing the defect' means as long as nothing indicates acceptance. His statement: 'Because claiming compensation is a substitute for return' etc. This reasoning shows that payment of the substitute is not required, as is clear. Hence in al-Nahr, the commentator said: If the maktab becomes unable, he should return it due to defect because the impediment is removed, just as if he discovers a defect in an absconding slave, he does not claim anything because claiming compensation is a substitute for return; the substitute is not resorted to as long as he is alive; so when he returns, he returns due to removal of impediment. And by this is refuted what is in al-Siraj about restricting kitaba to payment of its price to become like emancipation for money.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage consolidates several scenarios involving the destruction or consumption of the defective item. The main point: perishing (halak) of the item does not bar the buyer from seeking compensation for a defect, whether he knew of the defect before destruction or not. This is different from emancipation: if the buyer emancipates the slave after discovering the defect, that prevents compensation because the buyer's own action caused the impossibility of return. But if he emancipates before knowledge, he may still claim. Consumption (istihlak) like eating normally bars any recourse, except according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who allow compensation if the buyer ate food without knowledge of the defect. The fatwa is according to them. The passage gives examples: a leaning wall collapses before knowledge; a camel falls and is slaughtered by the buyer's order after the defect appears (if ordered before knowledge, claim is allowed). It also connects to the earlier discussion on kitaba: the rule that compensation is a substitute for return, and thus when return becomes possible again (as when the maktab becomes unable), the buyer must return the item rather than claim compensation. The final note refutes a restriction in al-Siraj that kitaba requires payment of the price before it becomes like emancipation; the commentator argues otherwise.

Key Terms
الهلاك (al-halāk): Complete destruction or perishing of the sold item, whether by natural causes or by an act not attributable to the buyer's own choice (e.g., animal dies).
الإعتاق (al-iʿtāq): Emancipation of a slave by the buyer.
الاستهلاك (al-istihlāk): Consumption of the item (e.g., eating, using up).
الخلف عن الرد (al-khalaf ʿan al-radd): The substitute for return; compensation takes the place of physical return when return is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a defective camel. Before discovering the defect, the camel falls into a well and dies. Can the buyer claim compensation for the defect?
A) No, because the item is gone.
B) Yes, he can claim compensation.
C) Only if he can prove the defect existed before sale.
D) He can only claim if he returns the carcass.

Key Takeaway
Perishing (halak) never bars compensation for a defect; emancipation bars it only after knowledge; consumption generally bars it except for eating, where the fatwa allows compensation according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
