Mukatib's Option of Defect When Purchasing Relatives
Rulings for a mukatib who buys a relative or umm walad and discovers a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that compensation is a substitute for return, distinguish between cases where a mukatib may or may not claim compensation for a defective purchase, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving relatives and umm walad.

Core Idea
Compensation is a substitute for return; it is only available when return is impossible.

When a mukatib (a slave on a manumission contract) buys a relative and finds a defect, the usual right to return (khiyar al-'ayb) is restricted. The kitaba contract prevents transfer of ownership, so the mukatib cannot return the item. However, whether he can claim compensation depends on whether return is truly impossible or merely blocked. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and the key distinction between father/son and umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Learn that compensation (al-ruju' bi al-nuqsan) is a substitute (khalaf) for return (radd). It is only resorted to when return is impossible.
This is a core Hanafi rule in khiyar al-'ayb.
2
Apply to a relative (father/son) purchase
A mukatib buys his father or son. He cannot return because kitaba prevents rescission. Also, he cannot claim compensation because return is not absolutely impossible (the sale itself is rescindable; kitaba is a personal impediment).
Key: the sale contract can still be rescinded in principle.
3
Apply to an umm walad purchase
A mukatib buys his umm walad (mother of his child). He cannot return her due to her special status (she cannot be transferred). Since return is impossible, he may claim compensation.
Impossibility here is inherent in the subject matter.
4
Apply to non-human items that perish
If the purchased item perishes or is lost before return, the buyer claims compensation because return is impossible. Examples: a leaning wall that collapses, goods lost in transit.
The text extends the same principle to non-human items.
5
Note the effect of later events
If the mukatib becomes incapacitated after knowing the defect, the impediment is removed and the master returns the item. If the master sells the slave or the mukatib dies, the master returns it. Discharge (ibra') before incapacity prevents return.
The right to return can be revived if the mukatib's status changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشتري الأول مع بائعه عن ذلك على شيء لا يصح عند أبي حنيفة لأنه لا حق له اهـ؛ كذا في الكافي. وقد يقال : ما المانع من جعله من آثار العتق؟ ولم يذكر المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى توابع الإعتاق وفيها تفصيل فالتدبير والاستيلاد كالعتق لتعذر النقل مع بقاء المحل بالأمر الحكمي، وأما الكتابة فمانعة من الرجوع لجواز النقل لجواز بيعه برضاه وتعجيزه نفسه فصار بها حابساً كالإعتاق على مال وقيد في السراج والوهاج بأداء بدل الكتابة ليعتق ليصير عتقاً على مال اهـ. وفي المحيط مكاتب اشترى أباه أو ابنه لا يرده بالعيب لأنه صار مكاتباً والكتابة تمنع زوال الملك بسائر الأسباب فكذلك الفسخ، ولا يرجع بنقصانه لأن الرجوع بالنقصان خلف عن الرد بدليل أنه لا يصار إليه مع القدرة على الرد وإنما يثبت الخلف إذا وقع اليأس عن الأصل ولم يقع لقبولها الفسخ بخلاف ما إذا دبره ثم وجد به عيباً، فإن عجز المكاتب بعدما علم بالعيب رده المولى ويتولاه المكاتب لزوال المانع، فإن باعه المولى أو مات المكاتب رده المولى بنفسه كالوكيل إذا مات فإن أبرأه المكاتب قبل العجز لا يرده المولى، وإن أبرأه المولى قبل عجز المكاتب جاز. ولو اشترى المكاتب أم ولده ومعها ولدها لا يردها بالعيب ويرجع بنقصانه، ولو أبرأه المكاتب جاز. ولو اشترى المولى من مكاتبه عبداً لا بالعيب ولا يخاصم البائع .اهـ. ولو قال المؤلف أو هلك المبيع ليتناول هلاك غير الآدمي لكان أولى. وفي القنية : اشترى جداراً مائلاً فلم يعلم به حتى سقط فله الرجوع بالنقصان. وفي جامع الفصولين : ذهب به إلى بائعه ليرد بعيبه فهلك في الطريق هلك على المشتري ويرجع بنقصه، وقدمنا حكم ما إذا قضى برده على البائع بعيبه فهلك عند المشتري.
The first buyer with his seller regarding that is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because he has no right; thus in al-Kafi. It may be said: what prevents making it from the effects of manumission? And the author did not mention the consequences of manumission, and there is detail: tadbir and istilad are like manumission due to impossibility of transfer with the subject remaining by legal order; and kitaba prevents return due to possibility of transfer because it is permissible to sell him with his consent and his incapacitating himself, so by it he becomes a detainer like manumission for consideration, and it is restricted in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj by payment of the kitaba substitute to become manumitted for consideration. In al-Muhit: a mukatib bought his father or his son, he does not return him due to defect because he became a mukatib, and kitaba prevents removal of ownership by all causes, so also rescission, and he does not claim compensation because claiming compensation is a substitute for return, as evidenced by the fact that it is not resorted to when return is possible, and the substitute is only established when despair of the original occurs, and it has not occurred because it accepts rescission, unlike if he had made him mudabbar then found a defect; if the mukatib becomes incapacitated after knowing of the defect, the master returns him and the mukatib takes charge due to removal of the impediment; if the master sells him or the mukatib dies, the master returns him himself like a deputy when he dies; if the mukatib discharges him before incapacity, the master does not return him; if the master discharges him before the mukatib's incapacity, it is valid. If the mukatib bought his umm walad and her child with her, he does not return her due to defect but claims compensation; if the mukatib discharges him, it is valid. If the master bought a slave from his mukatib, he does not return due to defect nor litigate with the seller. If the author said 'or the sold item perishes' to include perishing of non-human would be better. In al-Qunyah: he bought a leaning wall and did not know until it fell, he may claim compensation. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: he took it to his seller to return due to defect, and it perished on the way, it perished on the buyer and he claims compensation; and we have presented the ruling when he was ordered to return it to the seller due to defect and it perished with the buyer.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion on the option of defect for a mukatib (a slave on a contract of manumission). The author clarifies that a mukatib who purchases his father, son, or umm walad (mother of his child) cannot return them due to a defect, because the kitaba (contract) prevents the transfer of ownership—just as it prevents sale, it also prevents rescission. The buyer may only claim compensation (naqsan) for the defect. The reasoning: compensation is a substitute (khalaf) for return (radd), and a substitute is only resorted to when the original is impossible. Since the mukatib can still rescind the sale in principle, he cannot jump to compensation. However, if the mukatib becomes incapacitated ('ajz) after knowing the defect, the impediment is removed and the master returns the defective slave. If the master sells the slave or the mukatib dies, the master returns it himself. If the mukatib grants a discharge (ibra') before incapacity, the master cannot return; if the master grants discharge before incapacity, it is valid. The passage also extends the principle to non-human items: if a leaning wall collapses before the buyer knew, or the item is lost in transit while being taken back, the buyer claims compensation. The author notes that mentioning 'perishing' (halak) could include non-human items. This is a Hanafi position; the text references Abu Hanifa's view and later sources like al-Kafi and al-Muhit. The key legal maxim here is that compensation is a substitute for return and thus only applicable when return is impossible.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum to earn his freedom.
الرجوع بالنقصان (al-ruju' bi al-nuqsan): Claiming compensation for the reduction in value due to a defect, as a substitute for returning the item.
خلف عن الرد (khalaf 'an al-radd): A substitute for return; a secondary remedy used only when primary remedy (return) is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a mukatib who buys his umm walad get compensation but a mukatib who buys his father does not?
A) Because the father is a mahram and cannot be returned.
B) Because for the umm walad, return is impossible; for the father, return is possible but blocked by kitaba.
C) Because the father is bought with consent, but the umm walad is not.
D) Because the mukatib has more rights over his father.

Key Takeaway
Compensation (naqsan) is a substitute for return; a mukatib may only claim it when return is impossible, not when it is merely blocked by his own status.

Compensation for Defect After Consumption: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation after consumption, with fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi internal dispute on compensation after consuming a defective item and identify the mufta bihi position with its basis in istihsan.

Core Idea
Eating the item before knowing the defect does not bar compensation; the fatwa follows Sahibayn's istihsan over Abu Hanifa's qiyas.

When a buyer consumes or destroys a purchased item before discovering a defect, can they still claim compensation for the defect? This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Abu Hanifa, following strict qiyas, says no—because destruction prevents return, and compensation is its substitute. The Sahibayn, using istihsan, say yes—because eating is the intended use, and the defect is independent. The fatwa today relies on their view. We will trace the reasoning, the exceptions, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Read the passage and note the specific act in question: the buyer eats food (or consumes it) then discovers a defect. What is each imam's ruling?
The dispute also extends to wearing a garment until torn, planting seeds, and slaughtering.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa rules no compensation. His dalil is qiyas: eating is a guaranteed destruction (itlaf mamun) that makes return impossible, so the substitute (compensation) also falls. He analogizes it to killing or selling the item.
He relies on the zahir al-riwaya (apparent narration) from Imam Muhammad.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn allow compensation by istihsan. They argue that eating is the customary, intended use of food, so it should not be punished. They analogize it to manumission (itq) before knowledge of the defect, which also permits compensation.
Istihsan overrides the strict qiyas in this case.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer gets nothing. Under the Sahibayn, the buyer gets compensation for the defect. The difference affects numerous scenarios: wearing, planting, slaughter, and more.
The fatwa adopts the Sahibayn's view, making it the operative ruling.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its scope
The text states the fatwa is on the Sahibayn's saying for eating and analogous cases (e.g., wearing a garment until torn, planting seeds of the same genus). However, some acts like killing or sale still follow zahir al-riwaya even in the fatwa.
Later scholars (al-Ikhtiyar, al-Waqi'at) confirm the tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن هلاك المبيع ليس كإعتاقه فإنه إذا هلك المبيع يرجع بنقصان العيب، سواء كان بعد العلم به أو قبله. وأما الإعتاق بعد العلم به فمانع من الرجوع بنقصانه بخلافه قبله، وليس الإعتاق كاستهلاكه فإنه استهلكه فلا رجوع مطلقاً إلا في الأكل عندهما، وقيل غير مانع من الرجوع بنقصه أيضاً لوجوب الضمان به فهو كبيعه؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. وفي جامع الفصولين: ولو شرى بعيراً فلما أدخله في داره سقط فذبحه رجل بأمر المشتري فظهر عيبه يرجع بنقصه وبه أخذ المشايخ كما لو أكل طعاماً، ولو علم عيبه قبل الذبح فذبحه هو بأمره لا اهـ. وفي الواقعات: الفتوى على قولهما في الأكل فكذا هنا. ولو اشترى بيضا أو قثاء أو جوزاً فوجده براً على أنه ربيعي فزرعه فإذا هو خريفي اختار المشايخ أنه يرجع بنقص العيب وهو قولهما بناء على ما إذا اشترى طعاماً فأكله فظهر عيبه والفتوى على قولهما. ولو اشترى بزراً على أنه بزر بطيخ كذا فزرعه فظهر على صفة أخرى جاز البيع لاتحاد الجنس، واختلاف الصفة لا يفسد العقد، ولا يرجع بنقص العيب عند أبي حنيفة. ولو شرى على أنه بطيخ شتوي فزرعه فإذا هو صيفي بطل البيع فيأخذ المشتري ثمنه وعليه مثل ذلك البزر. ولو شرى بزر الدوين فزرعه في أرضه ولم ينبت رجع على بائعه بكل ثمنه إن كان لنقصان فيه، وكذا لو شرى بزر البطيخ فزرعه فنبت القثاء أو شرى بزر القثاء فوجده بزر القثاء البلخي بطل البيع جملة. ولو شرى حب القطن فزرعه ولم ينبت قيل يرجع بنقص عيبه، وقيل لا يرجع لأنه أهلك المبيع. وإذا رجع بالنقصان سلم له لأن النقصان لم يدخل تحت الوقف؛ كذا في البزازية أيضاً. قوله: (وإن أعتقه على مال أو قتله أو كان طعاماً فأكله أو بعضه لم يرجع بشيء) أما الأول فلأنه ما حبس هو بدله وحبس البدل كحبس المبدل منه وقدمنا أن الكتابة بمعناه فلا رجوع. وأما قتله وأكل الطعام فالمراد إتلاف المبيع من المشتري مانع من الرجوع بنقصان العيب وهو ظاهر الرواية لأن القتل لا يوجد إلا مضموناً وإنما يسقط هنا باعتبار الملك إن لم يكن مديوناً، فإن كان مديوناً ضمنه السيد؛ كذا في الكافي. فصار كالمستفيد به عوضاً بخلاف الإعتاق فإنه لا وقتل غيره مانع من الرجوع بنقصه أيضاً لوجوب الضمان به فهو كبيعه؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. وأما الأكل فالمذكور قوله، وأما عندهما فيرجع استحساناً، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا لبس الثوب حتى تخرق لهما أنه صنع بالمبيع ما يقصد بشرائه ويعتاد فعله له فأشبه الإعتاق، وله أنه تعذر الرد بفعل مضمون منه في المبيع فأشبه البيع والقتل ولا يعتبر بكونه مقصوداً ألا ترى أن المبيع إنما يقصد بالشراء، ثم هو يمنع الرجوع وأكل البعض كأكل الكل لكونه كشيء واحد فصار كبيع البعض، وعنهما يرجع بالنقصان في الكل، وعنهما يرد ما بقي لأنه لا يضره التبعيض ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل وعليه الفتوى؛ كذا في الاختيار.
The summary is that destruction of the sold item is not like its manumission: if the sold item perishes, he claims compensation for the defect, whether before or after knowledge. As for manumission after knowledge, it prevents claiming compensation, unlike before it. And manumission is not like consumption because he consumed it, so no claim at all, except in eating according to them (Sahibayn). It is said it does not prevent claiming compensation either, because it necessitates compensation, so it is like its sale. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he bought a camel, when he brought it into his house it fell, and a man slaughtered it at the buyer's order, and a defect appeared, he claims compensation, and the scholars took this, just as if he ate food; if he knew the defect before slaughter and slaughtered it at his order, no. In al-Waqi'at: the fatwa is on their saying (Sahibayn) regarding eating, so also here. If he bought eggs or cucumbers or walnuts and found them sour? Actually, if he found them as spring variety but planted them and they turned out autumn, the scholars chose that he claims compensation for the defect, and this is their saying (Sahibayn) based on the case of buying food and eating it and a defect appears, and the fatwa is on their saying. If he bought seeds as watermelon seeds and planted them and they appeared as another type, the sale is valid due to unity of genus, and difference in attribute does not invalidate the contract, and he does not claim compensation for the defect according to Abu Hanifa. If he bought as winter watermelon and planted it and it turned out summer, the sale is void; he takes his price and owes similar seeds. If he bought dhin? seeds and planted them in his land and they did not grow, he claims from his seller the full price if there is a defect; likewise if he bought watermelon seeds and planted them and they grew cucumbers, or bought cucumber seeds and found them to be Balkh cucumber, the sale is totally void. If he bought cotton seeds and planted them and they did not grow, some say he claims compensation, some say no because he destroyed the item. And if he claims compensation, it is valid for him because the deficiency did not enter into the waqf. His saying: 'If he manumitted for consideration, or killed it, or it was food and he ate it or part of it, he does not claim anything' – as for the first, because he did not detain its substitute and detaining the substitute is like detaining the substitute-for, and we have stated that kitaba is in its meaning, so no claim. As for killing and eating food, what is meant is that the buyer's destruction of the item prevents claiming compensation for the defect, and this is the apparent narration because killing is only found as guaranteed, but it is dropped here due to ownership if he is not in debt; if he is in debt, the master guarantees it. So he becomes like one who received a substitute, unlike manumission because it does not require compensation, and killing by another prevents claiming compensation too because it necessitates compensation, so it is like its sale. As for eating, it is as mentioned; according to them (Sahibayn), he claims by istihsan. And on this is the disagreement if he wore a garment until it tore – according to them, he did to the item what is intended by its purchase and accustomed, so it resembles manumission; and for him (Abu Hanifa), return became impossible by a guaranteed act of his in the item, so it resembles sale and killing, and it is not considered that it is intended – do you not see that the item is only intended by purchase, yet it prevents return? And eating part is like eating all because it is one thing, so it becomes like selling part; and from them (Sahibayn) there is a narration that he claims compensation for the whole; and from them that he returns what remains because partiality does not harm him and claims compensation for what he ate, and the fatwa is on this.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on whether a buyer who has disposed of the item—by destruction, manumission, killing, eating, planting, or making waqf—can still claim compensation for a defect. The author summarizes key principles: destruction (halk) of the item does not prevent claiming compensation, whether the buyer knew of the defect before or after. However, manumission (itq) after knowledge prevents compensation, though not before. Consumption (istihlak) generally bars all claim except in the case of eating, where the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule by istihsan that compensation is still possible, while Abu Hanifa says no. The text then extends to analogous cases: slaughtering an animal at the buyer's order, wearing a garment until torn, planting seeds that yield a different variety, and making the item waqf. In seed cases, if the genus remains the same but the attribute changes (e.g., spring vs. autumn), Abu Hanifa denies compensation, while Sahibayn allow it based on the eating analogy. The fatwa is on Sahibayn's view. The passage highlights the Hanafi internal dispute: the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) bars compensation for killing and eating, but Sahibayn use istihsan to allow it. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows them in many cases. The commentary also clarifies that if the buyer makes the item waqf, he can still claim compensation because the deficiency was not part of the waqf. This is a complex area balancing the principles of return (radd) and substitute (khalaf).

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that allows deviation from a strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling, used here by Sahibayn to permit compensation after eating.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in the six core books; the default madhhab position before istihsan or ijtihad.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction or consumption of property by the buyer, which is a guaranteed act (mamun) that may affect the right to return or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa, what is the ruling if a buyer eats food and then discovers a defect?
A) He cannot claim anything because he consumed the item
B) He can return the remaining food and claim compensation
C) He can claim compensation for the defect by istihsan
D) The sale is void automatically

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa permits a buyer to claim compensation for a defect even after consuming the item if the consumption is customary, following Sahibayn's istihsan, not Abu Hanifa's qiyas.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buyer's Use and the Right to Claim Deficiency: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Commentary on khiyar al-ayb when buyer consumes or destroys the item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's right to claim deficiency after consuming the item, and explain why the fatwa follows the Sahibayn using istihsan.

Core Idea
Consuming the item does not necessarily forfeit the right to claim deficiency—the Sahibayn use istihsan to preserve the buyer's right.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explores a critical dispute within the Hanafi school: what happens when a buyer uses the item (eats food, wears clothes, kills a slave) and only then discovers a hidden defect? Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer's guaranteed act prevents any claim. The Sahibayn argue by istihsan that normal intended use should not bar the claim. The fatwa ultimately follows the Sahibayn, but the reasoning reveals the school's approach to balancing qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal issue
Determine what the buyer did with the item (e.g., ate food, wore a garment, killed a slave). The legal issue is whether this action forfeits the right to claim deficiency (arish) for a pre-existing defect.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa: no claim for deficiency. His dalil is qiyas: the buyer's guaranteed act (madhmun) prevents return, and since return is impossible, claiming deficiency is also impossible—analogous to selling the item after discovery.
The act is considered 'madhmun' (guaranteed) because the buyer is liable for destruction.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: claim for deficiency by istihsan. Their dalil: the buyer's action is the normal intended use (e.g., wearing a garment), so it should not forfeit the right, just like emancipation (i'taq) does not forfeit it.
Istihsan overrides the qiyas because the action is intended and not a guarantee-worthy act.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
The fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view. The reason: istihsan is a stronger evidence in this context because the buyer's use is the very purpose of the sale, and denying the claim would cause hardship.
Al-Marghinani and later authorities prefer this view; it is the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the principle to distinguish acts
General rule: if the buyer's act is guaranteed (madhmun) and prevents return (e.g., killing, selling), he cannot claim deficiency. If the act is non-guaranteed (e.g., natural disaster, emancipation), he retains the right.
This distinction governs many furu' cases (e.g., endowment, partial consumption).

Source Text

قتله أو كان طعاماً فأكله أو بعضه لم يرجع بشيء ولو اشترى بيضا أو قثاء أو جوزاً فوجده براً على أنه ربيعي فزرعه فإذا هو خريفي اختار المشايخ أنه يرجع بنقص العيب وهو قولهما بناء على ما إذا اشترى طعاماً ازرعه فإن لم ينبت فأنا ضامن لهذا البذر فزرع فلم ينبت فعليه ضمان النقصان .اهـ. وأشار بالإعتاق إلى الوقف فإذا وقف المشتري الأرض ثم علم بالعيب رجع بالنقص، وفي جعلها مسجداً اختلاف والمختار الرجوع بالنقص ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين وعليه الفتوى كما في البزازية. وإذا رجع بالنقصان سلم له لأن النقصان لم يدخل تحت الوقف ؛ كذا في البزازية أيضاً قوله : (وإن أعتقه على مال أو قتله أو كان طعاماً فاكله أو بعضه لم يرجع بشيء) أما الأول فلأنه ما حبس هو بدله وحبس البدل كحبس المبدل منه وقدمنا أن الكتابة بمعناه فلا .رجوع وأما قتله وأكل الطعام فالمراد إتلاف المبيع من المشتري مانع من الرجوع بنقصان العيب وهو ظاهر الرواية لأن القتل لا يوجد إلا مضموناً وإنما يسقط هنا باعتبار الملك إن لم يكن مديوناً، فإن كان مديوناً ضمنه السيد؛ كذا في الكافي. فصار كالمستفيد به عوضاً بخلاف الإعتاق فإنه لا وقتل غيره مانع من الرجوع بنقصه أيضاً لوجوب الضمان به فهو كبيعه؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. وأما الأكل فالمذكور قوله، وأما عندهما فيرجع استحساناً، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا لبس الثوب حتى تخرق لهما أنه صنع بالمبيع ما يقصد بشرائه ويعتاد فعله له فأشبه الإعتاق، وله أنه تعذر الرد بفعل مضمون منه في المبيع فأشبه البيع والقتل ولا يعتبر بكونه مقصوداً ألا ترى أن المبيع إنما يقصد بالشراء، ثم . هو يمنع الرجوع وأكل البعض كأكل الكل لكونه كشيء واحد فصار كبيع البعض، وعنهما يرجع بالنقصان في الكل، وعنهما يرد ما بقي لأنه لا يضره التبعيض ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل وعليه الفتوى؛ كذا في الاختيار. والحاصل أن الفتوى على قولهما في الرجوع بالنقصان كما في الخلاصة ورد ما بقي قالوا: والأصل في هذه المسائل أن الرد متى امتنع بفعل مضمون من المشتري كالقتل والتمليك من غيره امتنع الرجوع بالنقصان ومتى امتنع لا من جهته أو من جهته بفعل مضمون كالهلاك بآفة سماوية أو انتقص أو ازداد زيادة مانعة للرد أو الإعتاق أو توابه كالتدبير والاستيلاد لا يمتنع الرجوع بالنقصان، وعلى هذا قال البزازي : لو وطئ المشتري الجارية ثم باعها بعد العلم بالعيب لا يرجع، وإن وطئها غير البائع ثم باعها يرجع بالنقصان اهـ. وفي المجتبى : لو أطعمه ابنه الكبير أو الصغير أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع بشيء، ولو أطعمه عبده أو مدبره أو أم ولده يرجع لأن ملكه باق. ولو اشترى سمناً ذائباً وأكله ثم أقر البائع أنه كانت وقعت فيه فأرة رجع بالنقصان عندهما وبه يفتي وفي الكفاية كل تصرف يسقط خيار الشرط يسقط خيار العيب إذا وجد في ملكه بعد العلم بالعيب ولا رد ولا أرش اهـ. وفي القنية : ولو كان غزلاً فنسجه أو القنية وقيد بكونه فعل بالمبيع لأنه لو أتلف كسب المبيع بعد العلم بالعيب لا يكون رضا ولا يسقط شيء من الثمن، وكذا لو كان كسب المبيع جارية فوطئها أو حررها بخلاف إعتاق ولد المبيعة فإنه يكون رضاً بعد العلم (والحاصل أن الفتوى على قولهما في الرجوع بالنقصان أي في مسألة أكل الكل ولبس الثوب حتى تخرق. وقوله «ورد ما بقي أي في مسألة أكل البعض وقد مر عن الرملي أن مثل ما في الخلاصة مذكور في النهاية وغاية البيان ومثله في الخانية أيضاً حيث قال : وإن اشترى طعاماً فأكل بعضه ثم علم بعيب كان عند البائع فإنه لا . يرد الباقي. وقال محمد : يرد الباقي ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل ويعطي لكل بعض حكم نفسه وعليه الفتوى وهذا لو كان الطعام في وعاء ،واحد ، فلو في وعائين فأكل ما في أحدهما أو باعه له رد الباقي بحصته في قولهم لأن المكيل والموزون بمنزلة أشياء مختلفة فكان الحكم فيه ما هو الحكم في العبدين والثوبين ونحو ذلك .اهـ. قال في النهر : لكن جعل صاحب الهداية قوله استحساناً مع تأخيره وجواب عنه دليلهما يقرر مخالفته في كون الفتوى على قولهما اهـ . وهذا الاستدراك مأخوذ من الفتح ويؤيده ما في الذخيرة حيث قال : ولو ولبس الثوب حتى تخرق من اللبس أو أكل الطعام ثم اطلع على عيب به قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى : لا . بنقصان يرجع
The chapter on sales, section on option of defect. If he kills it or it was food and he ate all or some of it, he does not claim anything. And if he buys eggs, cucumbers, or walnuts and finds them defective as being spring [produce] and plants them and they turn out to be autumn [produce], the scholars choose that he claims the deficiency, which is the position of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) based on the case where he buys food and eats it and then a defect appears, and the fatwa is on their position. And if he buys seeds thinking they are a certain type of watermelon and plants them and they turn out to be another type, the sale is valid due to unity of genus, and difference in quality does not invalidate the contract, and he does not claim the deficiency according to Abu Hanifa. If he buys winter watermelon seeds and plants them and they turn out to be summer, the sale is void, and the buyer takes his price and must return similar seeds. And if he buys دويّن seeds and plants them in his land and they do not grow, he recovers the full price from the seller if there was a deficiency in them. Similarly, if he buys watermelon seeds and they grow cucumbers, or buys cucumber seeds and finds them to be Balkhi cucumber seeds, the sale is completely void. If he buys cotton seeds and plants them and they do not grow, it is said he claims the deficiency, and it is said he does not because he destroyed the item. In al-Qunyah: He sold him a threshing floor for sowing and said 'plant it, and if it does not grow I guarantee this seed,' then he planted and it did not grow, so he must guarantee the deficiency. He pointed out that emancipation is like endowment: if the buyer endows the land then learns of the defect, he claims the deficiency. In making it a mosque there is a difference of opinion, and the chosen view is claiming the deficiency. ...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a detailed commentary on the rules of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) when the buyer has used the item. It begins by stating the general rule: if the buyer kills the slave or eats the food, he cannot claim any compensation for a latent defect. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. The reason is that the buyer has destroyed the item, and his action is guaranteed (madhmun), so it prevents returning the item and thus claiming the deficiency. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that he does claim the deficiency by istihsan (juristic preference), considering that the buyer intended the normal use. The fatwa is on the position of the Sahibayn. The passage then applies this to various cases: buying eggs or cucumbers and planting them only to find they are the wrong season, buying seeds that turn out to be a different variety, seeds that fail to grow, and the implications of endowment or emancipation by the buyer after purchase. A key principle is established: whenever the buyer's action makes return impossible and is a guaranteed action (like killing or selling), he cannot claim the deficiency. But if the action is not guaranteed (like a natural disaster) or is a non-guaranteed act (like emancipation), he retains the right. The passage also discusses cases of partial consumption, multiple containers, and other nuances, concluding that the fatwa is on the position of the Sahibayn for claiming the deficiency in most scenarios.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The buyer's option to return the item or claim compensation for a hidden defect existing at the time of sale.
رجوع بنقصان (ruju' bi nuqsan): The buyer's right to claim from the seller the difference in value caused by the defect (the deficiency).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning where a stronger evidence or need overrides a qiyas (analogy) leading to a more equitable ruling.
إعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation, setting a slave free. Here, it is discussed as an action that does not prevent the buyer from claiming deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling when a buyer eats food and then discovers a latent defect?
A) The buyer has no claim for deficiency (Abu Hanifa's view).
B) The buyer may claim the deficiency (Sahibayn's view).
C) The buyer must return the remaining food and can only claim deficiency if he did not consume it intentionally.
D) The seller must refund the full price regardless of defect.

Key Takeaway
The fatwa follows the Sahibayn: a buyer who uses the item as intended may still claim the deficiency by istihsan, because normal use does not forfeit the right to compensation for a pre-existing defect.

The Right to Claim Deficiency After Consumption: Fatwa on the View of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Explaining the fatwa rule and the principle of guaranteed acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the buyer's right to claim a reduction in price (nuqsan) after consuming the item and identify which acts (guaranteed vs non-guaranteed) affect that right.

Core Idea
Even after consuming the item, the buyer may claim a price reduction for a hidden defect—except when the consumption was through a 'guaranteed' act like selling or killing.

When a buyer discovers a defect after using or consuming the item, can he still claim compensation? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. Abu Hanifa held that consumption forfeits the right entirely. But Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allowed a claim for the deficiency (nuqsan). Later Hanafi jurists adopted their view as the operative fatwa. In this lesson, we unpack the principle that governs when the right is preserved or lost.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The core issue: if the buyer consumes or alters the item after purchase, does he lose the right to claim a reduction in price for a pre-existing defect? Abu Hanifa says yes; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no, with conditions.
The text opens by stating the fatwa is on the view of the Two, indicating the later preferred position.
2
Understand the 'guaranteed act' principle
The Two's view distinguishes between a 'guaranteed act' (al-fi'l al-madmun) by the buyer that prevents return—like killing or selling—which forfeits the right to claim deficiency, and other acts like eating or freeing a slave, which do not.
The principle is not explicitly from the Two but is derived from their rulings and adopted by later jurists.
3
Apply the principle to examples
Work through cases: buyer eats food, buys butter with a mouse, feeds the item to different persons, etc. In each, determine whether the act is guaranteed or not and whether the right to nuqsan is preserved.
Feeding a slave does not forfeit the right because ownership remains; feeding a guest does forfeit because possession transfers.
4
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, a buyer who eats an apple and then finds a worm gets nothing. Under the Two, he gets a reduction equal to the difference between the price of a sound apple and a defective one.
This practical difference shows why the Two's view is considered more equitable.
5
Note the fatwa authority
The text cites multiple sources (al-Khulasa, al-Nihaya, al-Kifaya) confirming 'الفتوى على قولهما' (the fatwa is on their view). Even though some like the author of al-Hidayah considered it istihsan, later practice settled on the Two's view.
This is a classic example of tarjih within the madhhab, where later scholars prefer the more lenient position.

Source Text

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب

جنس
الفتوى على قولهما في الرجوع بالنقصان كما في الخلاصة ورد ما بقي قالوا: والأصل في هذه المسائل أن الرد متى امتنع بفعل مضمون من المشتري كالقتل والتمليك من غيره امتنع الرجوع بالنقصان ومتى امتنع لا من جهته أو من جهته بفعل مضمون كالهلاك بآفة سماوية أو انتقص أو ازداد زيادة مانعة للرد أو الإعتاق أو توابه كالتدبير والاستيلاد لا يمتنع الرجوع بالنقصان، وعلى هذا قال البزازي : لو وطئ المشتري الجارية ثم باعها بعد العلم بالعيب لا يرجع، وإن وطئها غير البائع ثم باعها يرجع بالنقصان اهـ. وفي المجتبى : لو أطعمه ابنه الكبير أو الصغير أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع بشيء، ولو أطعمه عبده أو مدبره أو أم ولده يرجع لأن ملكه باق. ولو اشترى سمناً ذائباً وأكله ثم أقر البائع أنه كانت وقعت فيه فأرة رجع بالنقصان عندهما وبه يفتي وفي الكفاية كل تصرف يسقط خيار الشرط يسقط خيار العيب إذا وجد في ملكه بعد العلم بالعيب ولا رد ولا أرش اهـ. وفي القنية : ولو كان غزلاً فنسجه أو فيلقاً فجعله إبريسماً ثم ظهر أنه كان رطباً وانتقص وزنه رجع بنقصان العيب بخلاف ما إذا باع اهـ . قيد بالطعام لأنه لو اشترى كرماً بثمره وذكر الثمر وأكل منها ثم وجد بالكرم عيباً فله رد الكرم؛ كذا في القنية وقيد بكونه فعل بالمبيع لأنه لو أتلف كسب المبيع بعد العلم بالعيب لا يكون رضا ولا يسقط شيء من الثمن، وكذا لو كان كسب المبيع جارية فوطئها أو حررها بخلاف إعتاق ولد المبيعة فإنه يكون رضاً بعد العلم بالعيب؛ كذا . في البزازية قوله : (ولو اشترى بيضاً أو قثاء أو جواز فوجده فاسداً ينتفع به رجع بنقصان العيب وإلا بكل الثمن أي إن لم يكن منتفعاً به فإن الثمن لأنه يرجع بجميع ليس بمال فكان البيع باطلاً، ولا يعتبر في الجواز صلاح قشره على ما قيل لأن ماليته باعتبار اللب، وإن كان ينتفع به فساده لم يرده لأن الكسر عيب حادث ولكنه يرجع بنقصان
مع
(والحاصل أن الفتوى على قولهما في الرجوع بالنقصان أي في مسألة أكل الكل ولبس الثوب حتى تخرق. وقوله «ورد ما بقي أي في مسألة أكل البعض وقد مر عن الرملي أن مثل ما في الخلاصة مذكور في النهاية وغاية البيان ومثله في الخانية أيضاً حيث قال : وإن اشترى طعاماً فأكل بعضه ثم علم بعيب كان عند البائع فإنه لا . يرد الباقي. وقال محمد : يرد الباقي ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل ويعطي لكل بعض حكم نفسه وعليه الفتوى وهذا لو كان الطعام في وعاء ،واحد ، فلو في وعائين فأكل ما في أحدهما أو باعه له رد الباقي بحصته في قولهم لأن المكيل والموزون بمنزلة أشياء مختلفة فكان الحكم فيه ما هو الحكم في العبدين والثوبين ونحو ذلك .اهـ. قال في النهر : لكن جعل صاحب الهداية قوله استحساناً مع تأخيره وجواب عنه دليلهما يقرر مخالفته في كون الفتوى على قولهما اهـ . وهذا الاستدراك مأخوذ من الفتح ويؤيده ما في الذخيرة حيث قال : ولو ولبس الثوب حتى تخرق من اللبس أو أكل الطعام ثم اطلع على عيب به قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى : لا . بنقصان يرجع
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. 49. The fatwa is on the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the return of the deficiency, as in al-Khulasa, and the return of what remains. They said: The principle in these issues is that whenever the return is prevented by a guaranteed act of the buyer, like killing or transferring ownership to another, the recourse for deficiency is prevented. And whenever it is prevented not from his side, or from his side by a non-guaranteed act, like destruction by a heavenly affliction, or the item decreased or increased in a way that prevents return, or manumission or its equivalents like tadbir and istilad, the recourse for deficiency is not prevented. According to this, al-Bazzazi said: If the buyer had intercourse with the slave-girl and then sold her after knowing the defect, he does not have recourse; but if someone other than the seller had intercourse with her and then he sold her, he has recourse for deficiency. In al-Mujtaba: If he fed her (the slave-girl) to his adult son, or minor, or his wife, or his mukatab, or his guest, he does not have recourse for anything; but if he fed her to his slave, or his mudabbar, or his umm walad, he has recourse because his ownership remains. If he bought melted butter and ate it, then the seller confessed that a mouse had fallen into it, he has recourse for deficiency according to the two, and this is the fatwa. In al-Kifaya: Every disposal that invalidates the option of condition invalidates the option of defect if it occurs in his ownership after knowledge of the defect, and there is no return and no arsh. In al-Qunyah: If it was yarn and he wove it, or it was felt and he made it into brocade, then it appeared that it was moist and its weight decreased, he has recourse for the deficiency of the defect, unlike if he sold. Restricted to food because if he bought a vineyard with its fruit and mentioned the fruit and ate from it, then found a defect in the vineyard, he may return the vineyard; likewise in al-Qunyah. Restricted to the act being on the sold item because if he destroyed the earnings of the sold item after knowledge of the defect, that is not consent and does not nullify any part of the price; likewise if the earning was a slave-girl and he had intercourse with her or freed her, unlike freeing a child of the sold slave-girl, which becomes consent after knowledge of the defect. In al-Bazzaziyyah, his saying: (If he buys eggs, or cucumbers, or walnuts and finds them spoiled, if they are usable he recoups the deficiency, otherwise he recoups the full price) i.e., if it is not usable, he recoups the entire price because it is not property, so the sale is void; and in walnuts, the goodness of the peel is not considered according to what is said because its monetary value is based on the kernel. And if it is usable in its spoiled state, he doesn't return it because the breaking is a new defect, but he recoups the deficiency. (The summary is that the fatwa is on the view of the two regarding recourse for deficiency, i.e., in the case of eating all and wearing the garment until it tears. And his saying 'and he returns what remains' i.e., in the case of eating some. It has been mentioned from al-Ramli that something like in al-Khulasa is mentioned in al-Nihaya and Ghayat al-Bayan, and similar in al-Khaniyah as well, where he said: If he bought food and ate some of it, then knew of a defect that was with the seller, he does not return the remainder. Muhammad said: He returns the remainder and recoups the deficiency of what he ate, and gives each part its own ruling, and the fatwa is on this. This is if the food is in one vessel. If it is in two vessels, and he ate what was in one of them or sold it, he returns the remainder with its corresponding portion according to their opinion because measured and weighed items are like different things, so the ruling is the same as for two slaves and two garments, etc. In al-Nahr: But the author of al-Hidayah made his statement istihsan with delay, and a response to their proof establishes his opposition regarding the fatwa being on their view. This withdrawal is taken from al-Fath, and it is supported by what is in al-Dhakhirah, where he said: And if he wore the garment until it tore from wearing, or ate the food, then found a defect in it, Abu Hanifa said: He does not recoup by deficiency.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a buyer who has consumed or altered the sold item can still claim a reduction in price (النقصان) due to a pre-existing defect. The text opens by stating that the fatwa (operative ruling) is on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (قولهما), allowing recourse for deficiency. It then lays down a principle: if the buyer's act that prevents return is 'guaranteed' (مضمون) like killing or selling to another, he loses the right to claim deficiency. But if the prevention is due to a non-guaranteed act (like freeing a slave) or an external cause (like an act of God), he retains the right. Examples follow: a buyer who has intercourse with a slave-girl and then sells her loses the right; but if someone else had intercourse, he retains it. Feeding the item to certain persons (like children, wife) is considered consumption that forfeits the claim, but feeding it to one's own slaves does not. The text then references various sources like al-Kifaya, al-Qunyah, and al-Bazzaziyyah to illustrate nuances. A key point is the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding partial consumption: Abu Hanifa says no return at all, while Muhammad says the remainder can be returned with a reduction for what was consumed, and this is the preferred view. The passage also clarifies that measured items in separate vessels are treated like distinct items.

Key Terms
الرجوع بالنقصان (al-ruju' bi-l-nuqsan): The right of the buyer to claim a reduction in the purchase price proportional to the defect, without returning the item.
الرد (al-radd): Return of the sold item to the seller due to a defect, which is the primary remedy.
الفعل المضمون (al-fi'l al-madmun): An act by the buyer that is 'guaranteed' in the sense that it destroys the subject matter or transfers ownership, thereby forfeiting the right to claim deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following acts by the buyer would extinguish the right to claim deficiency (nuqsan) for a pre-existing defect?
A) Eating the food he bought.
B) Wearing the garment until it tears.
C) Selling the item to a third party after knowledge of the defect.
D) Freeing a slave he bought.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa allows a buyer to claim a reduction in price for a hidden defect even after consuming or altering the item, unless the buyer performed a 'guaranteed act' (like selling or killing) that forfeits the right.

Buying Eggs and Walnuts: Remedies for Spoiled but Usable Items
Ruling on spoiled but usable items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the remedy for a buyer who finds a purchased item spoiled but still usable, and identify the conditions that affect that remedy.

Core Idea
If the item is spoiled but usable, the buyer claims a price reduction; if unusable, he returns it for full refund.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common marketplace scenario: you buy eggs or walnuts, break them at home, and find them spoiled. The ruling depends on whether the item is still usable and whether you have consumed any part after discovering the defect. The Hanafi school provides precise conditions for when you can claim a deficiency or demand a full refund.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item's condition
Determine whether the item is spoiled but still usable (e.g., as animal feed) or completely unusable. The remedy differs accordingly.
Usability includes use by the buyer or others, such as giving to the poor or feeding to animals.
2
Determine the remedy
If usable, the buyer recoups the deficiency (al-ruju' bi-nuqsan); if unusable, he returns the item for a full refund.
This is the general rule for items like eggs, cucumbers, and walnuts.
3
Check if the buyer broke the item
The buyer must have broken the item to discover the defect. If he had seen the defect before breaking, he could have returned it intact. Breaking restricts his remedy to deficiency only.
The exception is ostrich eggs, where value is in the shell; the buyer returns the broken egg.
4
Check if the buyer consumed any part after knowledge
If the buyer, after breaking and tasting, consumes any part of the item, he loses the right to claim deficiency because his action indicates consent.
The disagreement on eating food after discovering a defect applies here: if the defect is known after eating, special rules apply.
5
Note the exception for ostrich eggs
Ostrich eggs are treated differently because their monetary value is in the shell. If found spoiled after breaking, the buyer claims a deficiency rather than returning the broken egg.
This exception highlights that the ruling depends on the nature of the item's value.

Source Text

فاسداً ينتفع به رجع بنقصان العيب وإلا بكل الثمن ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرده على بائعه ولو برضاه لا ولو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيباً لم يجبر على دفع الثمن ولكن العيب دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان إلا أن يقبلها البائع مكسوراً ويرد الثمن كما في البزازية . ولا بد من تقييد المسألة بكسره لأنه لو اطلع على عيبه قبل كسره كان له رده، فلو قال فكسره فوجده فاسداً أيضاً لكان أولى. ولا بد أيضاً من أن لا يتناول منه شيئاً بعد العلم بعيبه لأنه لو کسره فذاقه ثم تناول منه شيئاً لم يرجع بنقصانه لرضاه ،به وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام. وأطلق في الانتفاع فشمل انتفاعه به و انتفاع غيره من الفقراء والدواب علفا لهم. وأطلق البيض واستثنوا منه بيض النعامة إذا وجده فاسداً بعد الكسر فإنه يرجع بنقصان العيب لأن ماليته باعتبار القشر بخلاف غيره وقيد بوجود المبيع أي جميعه لأنه لو وجد البعض منه فاسداً فإن كان قليلاً جاز البيع لعدم خلوه عنه عادة ولا خيار له، وإن كان كثيراً فالصحيح عنده البطلان وعندهما يجوز في حصة الصحيح منه والقليل الثلاثة وما دونها في المائة، والكثير ما زاد والفاكهة من هذا القبيل ؛ كذا في المعراج وفي فتح القدير : ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه وظهر أنه مرّ ردّ ما بقي ورجع بنقصان ما خبز اهـ. وفي الواقعات : وهو المختار ولو قال المصنف فوجده معيباً لكان أولى لأن من عيب الجوز قلة لبه وسواده في البزازية. وصرح في الذخيرة بأنه عيب وليس من باب الفساد وفيها : اشترى عدداً من البطيخ أو الرمان أو السفرجل فكسر واحداً واطلع على عيب رجع بحصته من الثمن لا غير ولا يرد الباقي إلا أن يبرهن أن الباقي فاسد .اهـ. ولهذا قال «فوجده» أي المبيع احترازاً عما إذا كسر البعض فوجده فاسداً فإنه يرده أو يرجع بنقصه فقط ولا يقيس الباقي عليه قوله : (ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرد على بائعه ولو برضاه لا) أي لا يرده على بائعه الأول لأنه بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل فجعل البيع كأن لم يكن غاية الأمر أنه أنكر قيام العيب لكنه صار مكذباً شرعاً بالقضاء كما في الهداية ومنهم من جعله قول أبي يوسف. وعند محمد ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه لتناقضه وعامتهم على أنه إن سبق منه جحود نصاً بأن بعته وما به هذا العيب وإنما حدث عندك ثم رد علي بقضاء ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه، ومنهم من حملها على ما إذا كان ساكناً والبيئة تجوز على الساكت ويستحلف الساكت أيضاً لتنزيله منكراً؛ كذا في المعراج أطلقه فشمل القضاء بإقرار وببينة ونكول عن اليمين. ومعنى القضاء بالإقرار أنه أنكر الإقرار فأثبت بالبينة كما في الهداية، أو أقر وأبي القبول فقضى عليه كما قال العيب. وقالا : يرجع والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة .اهـ. وقال العلامة قاسم : لم تتفق المشايخ على اختيار قولهما بل من نظر إلى ثبوت الرواية وقوة الدليل صحح قول الإمام، ومن نظر إلى الرفق بالناس اختار قول محمد .اهـ. قوله: (وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام) كذا قاله الزيلعي واعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأن الخلاف في الطعام إذا علم العيب بعد الأكل لا قبله. قوله :
...spoiled yet usable, he recoups the deficiency of the defect; otherwise, he recoups the entire price. And if he sells the sold item and then the buyer returns it to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his own seller; but if he returns it willingly, he does not. And if the buyer takes possession of the sold item and claims a defect, he is not forced to pay the price, but the defect (is considered) to remove harm as much as possible, unless the seller accepts it broken and returns the price, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. The case must be restricted to his breaking it, because if he had seen the defect before breaking it, he would have the right to return it. So if he said he broke it and found it spoiled, that would be better. Also, he must not have taken anything from it after knowing the defect, because if he broke it and tasted it and then took something from it, he does not recoup the deficiency due to his consent. The disagreement should run in it as it runs in eating food. He made 'usability' unrestricted, including his own use and the use of others, such as giving it to the poor or animals as fodder. He made 'eggs' unrestricted, but they made an exception for ostrich eggs: if he finds it spoiled after breaking it, he recoups the deficiency because its monetary value is based on the shell, unlike others. It is restricted by the existence of the sold item (i.e., all of it) because if he finds some of it spoiled, if it is a small amount, the sale is valid since it is usually unavoidable and he has no option; if it is a large amount, the correct view according to him (Abu Hanifa) is that the sale is void; according to the two, it is valid for the sound portion, and a small amount is three or less in a hundred, a large amount is more; fruits are of this category. In al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir: If he bought flour and baked some of it and it appeared bitter, he returns what remains and recoups the deficiency of what he baked. In al-Waqi'at: This is the chosen view. If the author had said 'he found it defective' it would have been better, because defects of walnuts include small kernel and blackness, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. In al-Dhakhirah it is explicitly stated that it is a defect and not of the category of spoilage. And therein: He bought a number of watermelons, pomegranates, or quinces, broke one and found a defect; he recoups a portion of the price only and does not return the rest unless he proves the rest is spoiled. Therefore he said 'he found it' i.e., the sold item, to avoid the case where he breaks some and finds it spoiled, then he returns it or recoups only its deficiency and does not analogize the rest to it. His saying: (If he sells the sold item and it is returned to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his seller; but if willingly, he does not) meaning, he does not return it to his first seller because the court order is a rescission from the origin, making the sale as if it never happened. The point is that he denied the existence of the defect but was legally contradicted by the court order, as in al-Hidayah. Some attribute this to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, he may not litigate against his seller due to inconsistency. Most scholars hold that if he previously explicitly denied (saying 'I sold it and it had no such defect, it arose with you') and then it was returned by court order, he cannot litigate against his seller. Some interpret it as when he remained silent, and the judge ruled against the silent one and also administered an oath to him deeming him a denier. In al-Mi'raj, he made it unrestricted, including court order by confession, by evidence, and by refusal to take an oath. The meaning of court order by confession: he denied the confession, then it was established by evidence as in al-Hidayah, or he confessed and refused to accept it, so the judge ruled against him. As for the defect, the two said: he recoups (the deficiency), and the correct view is that of Abu Hanifa. Allamah Qasim said: The scholars did not agree on choosing the view of the two; rather, those who look at the transmission and strength of evidence prefer the imam's view, and those who look at leniency for people choose Muhammad's view. His saying: (The disagreement should run in it as it runs in eating food) — al-Zayla'i said this, and some scholars objected that the disagreement in food is when the defect is known after eating, not before.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the chapter on option of defect, focusing on specific cases. It begins with the ruling on buying eggs, cucumbers, or walnuts: if the item is found spoiled but still usable (e.g., as animal feed), the buyer claims a reduction in price; if completely unusable, he returns it for a full refund. The commentary adds details: the buyer must have broken the item to discover the defect (because if he had seen it before breaking, he could have returned it intact). If after breaking and tasting, he consumes any part, he loses the right to claim deficiency. The passage then discusses the situation where the buyer himself sells the item and the subsequent buyer returns it due to a defect by court order: the first buyer (now seller) can then return it to his own original seller only if the return was by court order, not by mutual consent. This is because the court order effectively rescinds the sale ab initio. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf allows the first buyer to litigate against his seller, while Muhammad does not if the first buyer had previously denied the defect. The passage also addresses issues of partial spoilage in bulk goods (like flour or fruits): if a small portion is spoiled, the sale remains valid; if a large portion, Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale, while the two uphold it for the sound portion. Finally, it notes that the distinction between 'defect' and 'spoilage' matters for walnuts: the defect is in the kernel's quality, not mere spoilage.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): Spoilage or corruption of the item, as opposed to a mere defect (عيب). For walnuts, the issue is defect (قلّة اللب), not spoilage.
الرجوع بنقصان (al-ruju' bi-nuqsan): Claiming a reduction in the purchase price proportional to the defect, without returning the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the buyer's remedy if he finds a purchased item spoiled but still usable?
A) He must return the item and get a full refund.
B) He can claim a reduction in the price.
C) He has no remedy because he broke it.
D) He can choose between return and reduction.

Key Takeaway
For spoiled but usable items, the buyer claims a price reduction; for unusable items, he returns for a full refund; and he must not have consumed any part after discovering the defect.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Returning a Defective Item by Court Order and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on return by court order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding whether a buyer who sold a defective item and had it returned by court order can return it to his original seller, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
A court order rescinds the sale ab initio, but if the buyer previously denied the defect, he cannot later litigate against his seller.

This passage delves into a nuanced scenario: a buyer (B) purchases an item from A, then sells it to C. C discovers a defect and returns the item to B by court order. Now, can B return the item to A? The Hanafi school has three positions, creating a practical dispute that affects the buyer's ability to seek recourse. Understanding this dispute is crucial for anyone involved in chains of sale with defective goods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Buyer B buys from A, then sells to C. C returns to B by court order due to a defect existing at the time of sale. Can B then return to A?
The return to B is by court order (qada), not by mutual consent.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that B cannot return the item to A. Reason: B previously denied the defect (he sold it as sound), and the court order makes him a liar; he cannot contradict himself.
This is the 'asahh' (most sound) view according to the passage.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that B can return to A because the court order rescinds the sale from the root, as if the sale never happened. B never truly owned the item, so he can revert to A.
This view emphasizes the retrospective effect of qada.
4
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that B cannot litigate against A if B explicitly denied the defect. If B remained silent or did not explicitly deny, he may have a case.
Muhammad's view adds the condition of explicit denial.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The passage states 'والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة' – the correct view is that of Abu Hanifa. However, some later scholars preferred Muhammad's view for leniency towards people.
There is no complete consensus; the tarjih depends on the strength of evidence or consideration of ease.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the chapter on option of defect, focusing on specific cases. It begins with the ruling on buying eggs, cucumbers, or walnuts: if the item is found spoiled but still usable (e.g., as animal feed), the buyer claims a reduction in price; if completely unusable, he returns it for a full refund. The commentary adds details: the buyer must have broken the item to discover the defect (because if he had seen it before breaking, he could have returned it intact). Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): A court order or judgment. In this passage, it refers to a judicial decision returning the sold item due to a defect.
الرجوع بنقصان (al-ruju' bi-nuqsan): Claiming a reduction in the purchase price proportional to the defect, without returning the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view is considered the correct one (mufta bihi) regarding the buyer's right to return to his seller after a court order return?
A) Abu Yusuf's view that he can return.
B) Muhammad's view that he can return only if silent.
C) Abu Hanifa's view that he cannot return.
D) The view that he can return regardless of denial.

Key Takeaway
If a buyer sells a defective item and it is returned by court order, he cannot return it to his own seller if he had previously denied the defect; this is the mufta bihi view (Abu Hanifa).

Partial Spoilage in Bulk Goods and Defect vs. Spoilage
Ruling on partial spoilage in bulk goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a small and large quantity of spoilage in bulk goods, explain the Hanafi ruling on the validity of the sale in each case, and differentiate between a defect (ayb) and spoilage (fasad) for items like walnuts.

Core Idea
If a small fraction is spoiled, the sale is valid; if a large fraction is spoiled, Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale, while the Sahibayn allow it for the sound portion.

When buying bulk goods like flour, fruits, or walnuts, it is common to find some items spoiled. The Hanafi school provides a threshold for when the sale remains valid and when it is void. Additionally, the passage clarifies that for walnuts, the issue is often a defect (like small kernel) rather than spoilage, affecting the remedy. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion between different types of loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity of spoilage
Determine whether the spoiled portion is small (ثلاثة فما دونها في المائة – three or less per hundred) or large (more than that).
This threshold applies to bulk goods like flour, fruits, and similar items.
2
Apply the ruling for small quantity
If the spoilage is small (3% or less), the sale is valid and the buyer has no option to reject; he must accept the item as is.
This is because such a small amount is usually unavoidable in bulk goods.
3
Apply the ruling for large quantity
If the spoilage is large, Abu Hanifa holds that the entire sale is void (batal). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sale is valid for the sound portion and void for the spoiled portion.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view here, according to the passage.
4
Distinguish between defect and spoilage for walnuts
Walnuts may have a defect (e.g., small kernel, blackness) which is not spoilage. The ruling for defect is different: the buyer can claim a deficiency or return, but it is not automatically void even if widespread.
The passage cites al-Dhakhirah stating that walnut kernel issues are 'ayb', not 'fasad'.
5
Apply to other fruits
The same rules apply to watermelons, pomegranates, quinces, etc. If one piece is broken and found defective, the buyer only recoups a portion for that piece, and does not return the rest unless he proves the rest is also defective.
The exception: if the broken sample shows spoilage, the rest is not presumed spoiled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاسداً ينتفع به رجع بنقصان العيب وإلا بكل الثمن ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرده على بائعه ولو برضاه لا ولو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيباً لم يجبر على دفع الثمن ولكن العيب دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان إلا أن يقبلها البائع مكسوراً ويرد الثمن كما في البزازية . ولا بد من تقييد المسألة بكسره لأنه لو اطلع على عيبه قبل كسره كان له رده، فلو قال فكسره فوجده فاسداً أيضاً لكان أولى. ولا بد أيضاً من أن لا يتناول منه شيئاً بعد العلم بعيبه لأنه لو کسره فذاقه ثم تناول منه شيئاً لم يرجع بنقصانه لرضاه ،به وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام. وأطلق في الانتفاع فشمل انتفاعه به و انتفاع غيره من القبيل ؛ كذا في المعراج وفي فتح القدير : ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه وظهر أنه مرّ ردّ ما بقي ورجع بنقصان ما خبز اهـ. وفي الواقعات : وهو المختار ولو قال المصنف فوجده معيباً لكان أولى لأن من عيب الجوز قلة لبه وسواده في البزازية. وصرح في الذخيرة بأنه عيب وليس من باب الفساد وفيها : اشترى عدداً من البطيخ أو الرمان أو السفرجل فكسر واحداً واطلع على عيب رجع بحصته من الثمن لا غير ولا يرد الباقي إلا أن يبرهن أن الباقي فاسد .اهـ. ولهذا قال «فوجده» أي المبيع احترازاً عما إذا كسر البعض فوجده فاسداً فإنه يرده أو يرجع بنقصه فقط ولا يقيس الباقي عليه قوله : (ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرد على بائعه ولو برضاه لا) أي لا يرده على بائعه الأول لأنه بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل فجعل البيع كأن لم يكن غاية الأمر أنه أنكر قيام العيب لكنه صار مكذباً شرعاً بالقضاء كما في الهداية ومنهم من جعله قول أبي يوسف. وعند محمد ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه لتناقضه وعامتهم على أنه إن سبق منه جحود نصاً بأن بعته وما به هذا العيب وإنما حدث عندك ثم رد علي بقضاء ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه، ومنهم من حملها على ما إذا كان ساكناً والبيئة تجوز على الساكت ويستحلف الساكت أيضاً لتنزيله منكراً؛ كذا في المعراج أطلقه فشمل القضاء بإقرار وببينة ونكول عن اليمين. ومعنى القضاء بالإقرار أنه أنكر الإقرار فأثبت بالبينة كما في الهداية، أو أقر وأبي القبول فقضى عليه كما قال العيب. وقالا : يرجع والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة .اهـ. وقال العلامة قاسم : لم تتفق المشايخ على اختيار قولهما بل من نظر إلى ثبوت الرواية وقوة الدليل صحح قول الإمام، ومن نظر إلى الرفق بالناس اختار قول محمد .اهـ. قوله: (وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام) كذا قاله الزيلعي واعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأن الخلاف في الطعام إذا علم العيب بعد الأكل لا قبله. قوله :
...spoiled yet usable, he recoups the deficiency of the defect; otherwise, he recoups the entire price. And if he sells the sold item and then the buyer returns it to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his own seller; but if he returns it willingly, he does not. And if the buyer takes possession of the sold item and claims a defect, he is not forced to pay the price, but the defect (is considered) to remove harm as much as possible, unless the seller accepts it broken and returns the price, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. The case must be restricted to his breaking it, because if he had seen the defect before breaking it, he would have the right to return it. So if he said he broke it and found it spoiled, that would be better. Also, he must not have taken anything from it after knowing the defect, because if he broke it and tasted it and then took something from it, he does not recoup the deficiency due to his consent. The disagreement should run in it as it runs in eating food. He made 'usability' unrestricted, including his own use and the use of others, such as giving it to the poor or animals as fodder. He made 'eggs' unrestricted, but they made an exception for ostrich eggs: if he finds it spoiled after breaking it, he recoups the deficiency because its monetary value is based on the shell, unlike others. It is restricted by the existence of the sold item (i.e., all of it) because if he finds some of it spoiled, if it is a small amount, the sale is valid since it is usually unavoidable and he has no option; if it is a large amount, the correct view according to him (Abu Hanifa) is that the sale is void; according to the two, it is valid for the sound portion, and a small amount is three or less in a hundred, a large amount is more; fruits are of this category. In al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir: If he bought flour and baked some of it and it appeared bitter, he returns what remains and recoups the deficiency of what he baked. In al-Waqi'at: This is the chosen view. If the author had said 'he found it defective' it would have been better, because defects of walnuts include small kernel and blackness, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. In al-Dhakhirah it is explicitly stated that it is a defect and not of the category of spoilage. And therein: He bought a number of watermelons, pomegranates, or quinces, broke one and found a defect; he recoups a portion of the price only and does not return the rest unless he proves the rest is spoiled. Therefore he said 'he found it' i.e., the sold item, to avoid the case where he breaks some and finds it spoiled, then he returns it or recoups only its deficiency and does not analogize the rest to it. His saying: (If he sells the sold item and it is returned to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his seller; but if willingly, he does not) meaning, he does not return it to his first seller because the court order is a rescission from the origin, making the sale as if it never happened. The point is that he denied the existence of the defect but was legally contradicted by the court order, as in al-Hidayah. Some attribute this to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, he may not litigate against his seller due to inconsistency. Most scholars hold that if he previously explicitly denied (saying 'I sold it and it had no such defect, it arose with you') and then it was returned by court order, he cannot litigate against his seller. Some interpret it as when he remained silent, and the judge ruled against the silent one and also administered an oath to him deeming him a denier. In al-Mi'raj, he made it unrestricted, including court order by confession, by evidence, and by refusal to take an oath. The meaning of court order by confession: he denied the confession, then it was established by evidence as in al-Hidayah, or he confessed and refused to accept it, so the judge ruled against him. As for the defect, the two said: he recoups (the deficiency), and the correct view is that of Abu Hanifa. Allamah Qasim said: The scholars did not agree on choosing the view of the two; rather, those who look at the transmission and strength of evidence prefer the imam's view, and those who look at leniency for people choose Muhammad's view. His saying: (The disagreement should run in it as it runs in eating food) — al-Zayla'i said this, and some scholars objected that the disagreement in food is when the defect is known after eating, not before.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from the chapter on option of defect, focusing on specific cases. It begins with the ruling on buying eggs, cucumbers, or walnuts: if the item is found spoiled but still usable (e.g., as animal feed), the buyer claims a reduction in price; if completely unusable, he returns it for a full refund. The commentary adds details: the buyer must have broken the item to discover the defect (because if he had seen it before breaking, he could have returned it intact). If after breaking and tasting, he consumes any part, he loses the right to claim deficiency. The passage then discusses the situation where the buyer himself sells the item and the subsequent buyer returns it due to a defect by court order: the first buyer (now seller) can then return it to his own original seller only if the return was by court order, not by mutual consent. This is because the court order effectively rescinds the sale ab initio. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf allows the first buyer to litigate against his seller, while Muhammad does not if the first buyer had previously denied the defect. The passage also addresses issues of partial spoilage in bulk goods (like flour or fruits): if a small portion is spoiled, the sale remains valid; if a large portion, Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale, while the two uphold it for the sound portion. Finally, it notes that the distinction between 'defect' and 'spoilage' matters for walnuts: the defect is in the kernel's quality, not mere spoilage.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): Spoilage or corruption of the item, as opposed to a mere defect (عيب). For walnuts, the issue is defect (قلّة اللب), not spoilage.
عيب (ayb): A defect that reduces the value or desirability of the item, without rendering it completely unusable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the threshold for 'small' spoilage in bulk goods?
A) One item per hundred.
B) Three or less per hundred.
C) Five per hundred.
D) Ten per hundred.

Key Takeaway
In bulk goods, if the spoiled portion is small (≤3%), the sale is valid; if large, Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale, and walnuts' defects are treated as 'ayb' not 'fasad'.

Ruling on Defect Discovered After Breaking: Compensation and Implied Consent
Explaining the buyer's right to compensation after breaking a defective item and the loss of that right due to implied consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer discovers a defect after breaking or using part of the item, and identify when the buyer loses the right to compensation due to implied consent (rida).

Core Idea
Breaking an item does not forfeit the right to compensation, but using it after knowing the defect indicates acceptance.

This lesson explores the scenario where a buyer breaks open a purchased item (like an egg or nut) and finds it defective. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules on whether the buyer can still claim compensation or must return the item, depending on whether the item is usable and whether the buyer used any part after discovering the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify defect category
Determine if the defective item is still usable or completely unusable. This classification decides whether the buyer gets full price or compensation.
Usable means the item has some functional value despite the defect.
2
Check for implied consent
If the buyer, after knowledge of the defect, takes any part of the item (e.g., tastes, uses, feeds to others), this constitutes rida (satisfaction) and he loses all rights to return or claim compensation.
The act of breaking alone does not imply rida. Only actual use after knowledge.
3
Apply the exception
Ostrich eggs are an exception: their value is in the shell, so breaking does not destroy usability. The buyer still gets compensation for a defective interior.
This exception highlights that the ruling depends on the nature of the item's value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب فاسداً ينتفع به رجع بنقصان العيب وإلا بكل الثمن ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرده على بائعه ولو برضاه لا ولو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيباً لم يجبر على دفع الثمن ولكن العيب دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان إلا أن يقبلها البائع مكسوراً ويرد الثمن كما في البزازية . ولا بد من تقييد المسألة بكسره لأنه لو اطلع على عيبه قبل كسره كان له رده، فلو قال فكسره فوجده فاسداً أيضاً لكان أولى. ولا بد أيضاً من أن لا يتناول منه شيئاً بعد العلم بعيبه لأنه لو كسره فذاقه ثم تناول منه شيئاً لم يرجع بنقصانه لرضاه به وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام. وأطلق في الانتفاع فشمل انتفاعه به و انتفاع غيره من الفقراء والدواب علفا لهم. وأطلق البيض واستثنوا منه بيض النعامة إذا وجده فاسداً بعد الكسر فإنه يرجع بنقصان العيب لأن ماليته باعتبار القشر بخلاف غيره وقيد بوجود المبيع أي جميعه لأنه لو وجد البعض منه فاسداً فإن كان قليلاً جاز البيع لعدم خلوه عنه عادة ولا خيار له، وإن كان كثيراً فالصحيح عنده البطلان وعندهما يجوز في حصة الصحيح منه والقليل الثلاثة وما دونها في المائة، والكثير ما زاد والفاكهة من هذا القبيل ؛ كذا في المعراج وفي فتح القدير : ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه وظهر أنه مرّ ردّ ما بقي ورجع بنقصان ما خبز اهـ. وفي الواقعات : وهو المختار ولو قال المصنف فوجده معيباً لكان أولى لأن من عيب الجوز قلة لبه وسواده في البزازية. وصرح في الذخيرة بأنه عيب وليس من باب الفساد وفيها : اشترى عدداً من البطيخ أو الرمان أو السفرجل فكسر واحداً واطلع على عيب رجع بحصته من الثمن لا غير ولا يرد الباقي إلا أن يبرهن أن الباقي فاسد .اهـ. ولهذا قال «فوجده» أي المبيع احترازاً عما إذا كسر البعض فوجده فاسداً فإنه يرده أو يرجع بنقصه فقط ولا يقيس الباقي عليه
The book of sale, chapter of option of defect. If [the item] is defective but still usable, he gets compensation for the defect; otherwise, the full price. If he sold the item and it was returned to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his seller; but if by consent, he does not (return it). If the buyer took delivery and claimed a defect, he is not forced to pay the price, but (he must pay) for the defect to avoid harm as far as possible, unless the seller accepts it broken and returns the price, as in al-Bazzaziyya. It is necessary to restrict the case to breaking because if he discovered its defect before breaking, he could return it. So if he said: he broke it and found it defective, that would be more appropriate. It is also necessary that he did not take anything from it after knowledge of the defect, because if he broke it and tasted it and then took something from it, he does not get compensation for the defect due to his satisfaction with it. The disagreement should apply as in the case of eating food. The term 'using' is general, so it includes his use and the use of others, like feeding the poor or animals. The term 'eggs' is general, but they exempted ostrich eggs: if he finds them defective after breaking, he gets compensation for the defect because their value is in the shell, unlike others. It is conditioned on the existence of the entire item; if he finds part of it defective, if it is little, the sale is valid (since items are not normally free from it) and he has no option; if it is much, the correct view of Abu Hanifa is that the sale is void; while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is valid for the sound portion. 'Little' is three or less per hundred; 'much' is more. Fruits are of this category. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in Fath al-Qadir: If he bought flour and baked some and it turned out to be bitter, he returns the remainder and gets compensation for what he baked. End. In al-Waqi'at: It is the chosen view. If the author had said 'he found it defective' it would have been better because among the defects of walnuts is being hollow and black, as in al-Bazzaziyya. And it is explicitly stated in al-Dhakhira that it is a defect and not of the type of corruption. In it: If he bought a number of watermelons, pomegranates, or quinces, and broke one and discovered a defect, he only gets his share of the price and does not return the rest unless he proves that the rest are defective. End. Hence the author said 'he found it' i.e., the item, as a precaution against the case where he breaks part and finds it defective: then he returns it or only gets compensation for the reduction, and does not extrapolate the rest accordingly.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rulings when a buyer discovers a defect after breaking or using part of the purchased item. The ruling: if the item is defective but still usable, the buyer gets compensation (naqsan); if unusable, the full price. The case where the buyer breaks an egg or nut and finds it rotten: if he breaks it first, he can still claim compensation, but only if he did not use any part after knowing the defect. The exception for ostrich eggs is because their value is in the shell. If only part of a batch is defective, if the defective portion is minor (3% or less), the sale is valid; if major, Abu Hanifa says the whole sale is void, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say only the defective part is void. The text also covers examples like flour and fruit, emphasizing that the buyer does not extrapolate the defect from one piece to the rest without proof.

Key Terms
نقصان العيب (nuqsan al-'ayb): Compensation for the reduction in value due to a defect; the difference between the sound and defective value.
كسر (kasr): The act of breaking open an item (like an egg or nut) to inspect it; relevant for determining when the buyer's right to return is affected.
رضى (rida): Implicit consent: if the buyer does any act indicating satisfaction after knowledge of the defect, he loses the right to return or claim compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer breaks an egg and finds it rotten, then tastes a small part of it, what is his right?
A) He can return the egg and get full refund.
B) He can claim compensation for the defect (naqsan).
C) He loses all rights because tasting implies acceptance.
D) He can only return the egg if he breaks another to confirm.

Key Takeaway
Breaking alone does not waive the right to compensation; using after knowledge does.
