Court-Ordered Return for Defect: Rescission or New Sale?
Critical refinement of the legal nature of court-ordered return for defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on court-ordered return for defect, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule that rescission operates prospectively only.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return for defect is a rescission, but only for the future—prior transactions remain valid.

When a buyer returns a defective item by court order, the seller may wish to pass that return back to his own seller. But is the court-ordered return a full rescission that wipes the slate clean, or does it have limits? This lesson explores an important intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on whether the rescission operates retroactively or only prospectively. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly handling chains of sales and preserving third-party rights.

Guided Path
1
State the core issue
Identify the dispute: when an intermediate seller has the item returned to him by court order due to a defect, can he in turn return it to his original seller?
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says yes, because the court order makes the sale void ab initio (faskh min al-asl). The intermediate seller's denial of the defect is overridden by the court's ruling.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no, because the intermediate seller's prior denial of the defect contradicts his later attempt to return, so he cannot litigate against his seller.
This position is based on consistency and avoiding contradiction.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the intermediate seller can recover the price from his own seller; under the Sahibayn, he bears the loss. The text also notes that the rescission is prospective, so past transactions (e.g., pre-emption, assignment) remain valid.
5
Apply the ruling to edge cases
Consider the exceptions: currency exchange (sarf) is always rescission; return by consent is a new sale for third parties, not rescission. Understand the conditions: after taking delivery, before discovery of defect, etc.
These conditions refine the application of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء يرد على بائعه ولو برضاه لا) أي لا يرده على بائعه الأول لأنه بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل فجعل البيع كأن لم يكن غاية الأمر أنه أنكر قيام العيب لكنه صار مكذباً شرعاً بالقضاء كما في الهداية ومنهم من جعله قول أبي يوسف. وعند محمد ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه لتناقضه وعامتهم على أنه إن سبق منه جحود نصاً بأن بعته وما به هذا العيب وإنما حدث عندك ثم رد علي بقضاء ليس له أن يخاصم بائعه، ومنهم من حملها على ما إذا كان ساكناً والبيئة تجوز على الساكت ويستحلف الساكت أيضاً لتنزيله منكراً؛ كذا في المعراج أطلقه فشمل القضاء بإقرار وببينة ونكول عن اليمين. ومعنى القضاء بالإقرار أنه أنكر الإقرار فأثبت بالبينة كما في الهداية، أو أقر وأبي القبول فقضى عليه كما قال العيب. وقالا : يرجع والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة .اهـ. وقال العلامة قاسم : لم تتفق المشايخ على اختيار قولهما بل من نظر إلى ثبوت الرواية وقوة الدليل صحح قول الإمام، ومن نظر إلى الرفق بالناس اختار قول محمد .اهـ. قوله: (وينبغي جريان الخلاف فيها كما لو أكل الطعام) كذا قاله الزيلعي واعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأن الخلاف في الطعام إذا علم العيب بعد الأكل لا قبله. قوله : كما في الكافي وصورة الإقرار أن يقول اشتريته وبه ذلك العيب ولم أعلم به وقضى به ثم ادعاه على بائعه وبرهن ببينة أو استحلف بائعه ؛ كذا في الولوالجية. وليس المراد منه أنه بمجرد القضاء عليه بإقراره يرده فليتأمل وإن قبله بغير قضاء ليس له رده على بائعه لأنه جديد في حق الثالث وإن كان فسخاً في حقهما والأول ثالثهما وأطلقه فشمل ما يحدث مثله، وما لا يحدث مثله وهو قول العامة، وتقييده في الجامع الصغير بما يحدث ليعلم حكم ما لا يحدث بالأولى، وفي بعض روايات الأصل أن ما لا يحدث مثله فالرضا به كالقضاء . وترك المصنف قيداً آخر وهو أن يكون بعد قبض المبيع لأنه لو كان قبل قبضه فهو فسخ في حق الكل، سواء كان بقضاء أو رضا؛ كذا في المعراج معزياً إلى المبسوط . وقيد آخر يكون البيع قبل الإطلاع على العيب إذا لو كان بعده ليس له الرد على بائعه ولو رد عليه بما هو فسخ ؛ كذا في الصغرى. وأورد على كونه فسخاً مسائل : الأولى لو كان المبيع عقاراً فرد بعيب لم يبطل حق الشفيع في الشفعة الثانية لو باع أمته الحبلى وسلمها ثم ردت بعيب بقضاء ثم ولدت فادعاه أبو البائع لم تصح دعوته ولو كان فسخاً لصحت كما لو لم يبعها . الثالثة لو أحال البائع غريمه على المشتري بالثمن ثم رد المبيع بعيب بقضاء لم تبطل الحوالة ولو كانت فسخاً لبطلت وأجاب في المعراج بأنه فسخ فيما يستقبل لا في الأحكام الماضية ولهذا قال شيخ الإسلام : قول القائل الرد بالقضاء يجعل العقد كأن لم يكن تناقض لأن العقد إذا جعل كأن لم يكن جعل الفسخ كأن لم يكن لأن الفسخ بدون العقد لا يتصور، فإذا انعدم أصله انعدم الفسخ من الأصل، وإذا انعدم الفسخ من الأصل عاد العقد لانعدام ما من العقد ينافيه ولكن يقال يجعل العقد كأن لم يكن في المستقبل لا في الماضي اهـ. والدليل على أن الفسخ إنما هو في المستقبل أن زوائد المبيع للمشتري ولا يردها مع الأصل ولهذا لو وهب مالاً قبل تمام الحول ثم رجع الواهب بعد الحول لا تجب الزكاة عليه فيما مضى؛ كذا في المعراج. ولو وهب داراً وسلمها فبيعت دار بجنبها فأخذها الموهوب له بالشفعة ثم رجع الواهب فيها لم يكن له الأخذ بشفعة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد كتبنا في الفوائد أن الرد بالعيب بقضاء فسخ إلا في مسألة وإذا لم يرده في صورة الرضا لا رجوع له بالنقصان أيضاً كما في المعراج. وإذا كان له الرد فله الرجوع بالنقصان كما في التهذيب يعني لو حدث عيب ورده بقضاء فله الأرش ولو برضا لا. وقيد بالمبيع وهو العين احترازاً عن الصرف فإنه يجعل فسخاً إذا رد بعيب لا فرق القضاء والرضا لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً جديداً لأن بين الدينار هنا لا يتعين في العقود، فإذا اشترى ديناراً بدرهم ثم باع الدينار من آخر ثم وجد (وليس المراد منه الخ) أي بل لا بد فيه من المخاصمة كما سيذكره في هذه السوادة. قوله : (فيكون
His statement: (If he sold the item and it was returned to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his seller; but if by consent, he does not) i.e., he does not return it to his original seller because with a court order it is a rescission from the beginning, making the sale as if it never occurred; the only issue is that he denied the existence of the defect, but he is legally deemed a liar by the court, as in al-Hidayah. Some attribute this view to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, he may not litigate against his seller due to contradiction. The majority hold that if he previously made a clear denial saying 'I sold it without this defect; it arose with you,' then it is returned by court order, he cannot litigate against his seller. Some interpret it as the case where he remained silent; the court decides against the silent party, and the silent one is also made to swear because he is treated as a denier. Thus in al-Mi'raj. He made it general, including court orders based on confession, evidence, or refusal to swear. The meaning of court order by confession is that he denied the confession, and then it was proven by evidence as in al-Hidayah, or he confessed and refused to accept, and was adjudged against. (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he returns (to the first seller). The correct view is that of Abu Hanifa. Allamah Qasim said: The scholars did not agree on preferring their view; some, looking at the strength of transmission and evidence, affirm the Imam's view; others, considering leniency for people, chose Muhammad's view. His statement: (The disagreement should also apply as in the case of eating food) — al-Zayla'i said this, but some scholars objected because the disagreement in the food case is when the defect is known after eating, not before. His statement: As in al-Kafi, the form of confession is that he says: 'I bought it with this defect and did not know, and the court so ruled, then he claimed it against his seller and provided evidence or made his seller swear.' Thus in al-Walwaljiyya. The intent is not that by mere court order against him on his confession, he returns it; reflect. If he accepts it without court order, he may not return it to his seller because it is a new sale with respect to the third party, even though it is a rescission between the two, and the first seller is a third party. He made it general, including things that can recur and those that cannot; the latter is the common view. The restriction in al-Jami' al-Saghir to things that recur is to indicate the ruling for things that do not recur by priority. In some transmissions of the original, for things that do not recur, satisfaction with it is like a court order. The author omitted another condition: that it be after taking delivery, because if it were before delivery, it is a rescission against all, whether by court order or consent. Thus in al-Mi'raj citing al-Mabsut. Another condition: the sale before discovery of the defect; if after, he cannot return to his seller even if the return is a rescission. Thus in al-Sughra. Objections were raised that it is a rescission: First, if the property was real estate and returned due to defect, the pre-emptor's right to pre-emption is not invalidated. Second, if he sold his pregnant slave woman and delivered her, then she was returned due to defect by court order and then gave birth, and the seller's father claims the child, his claim is not valid; if it were a rescission, it would be valid as if he had not sold her. Third, if the seller assigned his creditor to the buyer for the price and then the item was returned due to defect by court order, the assignment is not void; if it were a rescission, it would be void. Al-Mi'raj answered that it is a rescission with respect to the future, not past rulings. Hence Shaykh al-Islam said: The statement 'the return by court order makes the contract as if it never existed' is contradictory because if the contract is made as if it never existed, the rescission is made as if it never existed, since rescission without a contract is inconceivable. So if its origin is negated, the rescission is negated ab initio; and if the rescission is negated ab initio, the contract returns due to the removal of what contradicts it. But it is said: it makes the contract as if it never existed in the future, not in the past. The evidence that rescission is only for the future is that the increase (produce) of the item belongs to the buyer and he does not return it with the item. Similarly, if someone gifts property before the completion of the year, then the donor revokes after the year, zakat is not due on the past. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And if someone gifts a house and delivers it, then a neighboring house is sold and the donee takes it by pre-emption, then the donor revokes the gift, he does not have a right to pre-emption. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. We have written in al-Fawa'id that return due to defect by court order is a rescission except in one case. And if he does not return it in the case of consent, he has no right to compensation either, as in al-Mi'raj. And if he has the right to return, he has the right to compensation, as in al-Tahdhib, meaning if a defect occurs and he returns by court order, he gets the compensation; but if by consent, not. It is restricted to the item (sold), to exclude currency exchange, for it is made a rescission if returned due to defect, with no difference between court order and consent, because a new sale is not possible since dinars and dirhams are not specific in contracts. So if he buys a dinar for a dirham, then sells that dinar to another, then finds (defect)... (His statement: 'The intent is not that, etc.') i.e., rather it requires litigation, as he will mention in this margin. His statement: 'so it becomes'
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a super-commentary critically refining the rulings regarding court-ordered return for defect. It discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: When a person sells an item, and a third buyer returns it to him by court order due to a defect, can he in turn return it to his own original seller? Abu Hanifa rules that he can, because the court order makes the sale void ab initio (faskh min al-asl), treating it as if the sale never happened. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold he cannot return it to his original seller due to contradiction if he denied the defect. The text clarifies that the court order does not retroactively nullify the contract; it only operates prospectively (fi al-mustaqbal). This is supported by rulings: the buyer keeps the produce (e.g., fruit) from the item without returning it, and previous transactions like hawala (assignment) or shuf'a (pre-emption) remain unaffected. The exception is currency exchange (sarf), where return for defect always constitutes rescission because a new sale is impossible due to non-specificity of the money. The commentary also distinguishes between return by court order (which is rescission) and return by consent (which is a new sale in the eyes of third parties).

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract; legal annulment. Here, it means the contract is treated as void from the start (ab initio) or from the time of court order (prospectively).
رد بعيب (radd bi 'ayb): Returning a defective item to the seller due to a defect, either by court order (bi qada) or by mutual consent (bi rida).
قضاء (qada'): Court order or judicial decision. In this context, the ruling of a qadi that the item is defective and must be returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical effect of a court-ordered return for defect on a pre-emption (shuf'a) right if the item was real estate?
A) The pre-emption right is invalidated because the sale is rescinded.
B) The pre-emption right remains valid because the rescission is prospective only.
C) The pre-emption right is suspended until the return is completed.
D) The pre-emption right becomes a new right for the original seller.

Key Takeaway
Court-ordered return for defect is a rescission that operates prospectively, allowing the intermediate seller to recover from his upstream seller, but it does not retroactively invalidate past transactions.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Returning a Defective Item Without a Court Order in a Chain of Sales
Dispute on effect of returning without qada in chain sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi opinions on returning a defective item without a court order in a chain of sales and identify the practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
Returning a defective item without a court order may be valid for further return, but under a distinct sales view, it signals acceptance.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a buyer in a chain of sales who returns a defective item to his immediate seller without a court order can then have that seller return it further up the chain. The disagreement hinges on whether the defective item is considered the same as the sold item or a separate entity. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying khiyar al-'ayb in multi-party transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
We have a chain: first seller, first buyer (who becomes a seller), and second buyer. The second buyer discovers a defect and returns the item to his seller without a court order (qada). The question is whether that seller (the first buyer) can then return it to his own seller.
The defect is in the physical item, but the sale contracts are separate.
2
Compare the Two Positions
According to al-Muhit and al-Khaniyya: the defective item is not the actual sold item (the sold item was sound), so returning it is simply returning a different thing, and the first buyer can return it. According to al-Kafi: there are two distinct sales; returning without qada implies the second buyer accepted the defect, so he cannot return it, and thus the first buyer also cannot return it because he received the item back with acceptance.
The key distinction: are the sold items considered one or two?
3
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under the first view, the defect can be passed up the chain; under the second, the second buyer's return without qada cuts off further recourse. A Hanafi mufti must know which view is preferred in his school.
The passage does not specify tarjih, so both views are recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشتري الثاني بالدينار عيباً ورده على المشتري بغير قضاء فإنه يرده على بائعه لما ذكرنا كما في المحيط والخانية. وفي الكافي: المبيعان هنا واحد لأن المعيب ليس بمبيع بل المبيع السليم فيكون المبيع ملك البائع فإذا رده على المشتري يرده على بائعه، أما هنا المبيعان موجودان فإذا قيل بغير قضاء فقد رضي بالعيب فلا يرده على بائعه اهـ. المبيع ملك البائع حق التعبير أن يقول فيكون المعيب تأمل.
The second buyer returns the dinar due to a defect and returns it to the buyer without a court order; then he returns it to his seller as we mentioned, as in al-Muhit and al-Khaniyya. In al-Kafi: The sold items here are one because the defective is not the sold item; rather the sold item is sound, so the sold item belongs to the seller. When he returns it to the buyer, he returns it to his seller. But here the sold items are two; so if it is said without a court order, he has accepted the defect, so he does not return it to his seller. The sold item is the property of the seller—the correct expression is to say "so the defective becomes"—ponder.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a chain of sales where a subsequent buyer discovers a defect and returns the item to his immediate seller without seeking a court order (qada). According to al-Muhit and al-Khaniyya, the second buyer can return the defective item without qada, and his seller (the first buyer) can then return it to his own seller (the original seller). The reasoning is that the defective item is not the actual sold item; the sold item was sound, so the actual sold item remains with the buyer, and the defective one is returned. However, according to al-Kafi, when there are two separate sales (i.e., the chain involves distinct transactions), if the second buyer returns without qada, he is deemed to have accepted the defect (because he had the option to seek a court order but did not), and thus he cannot return it further up the chain. The marginal note hints at a refinement in expression: the sold item (as the property of the seller) should be properly phrased.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Court order or judicial decree
عيب ('ayb): A defect that diminishes the value or utility of the sold item, giving the buyer the option of return (khiyar al-'ayb)
مبيع (mabi'): The sold item/object of sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Kafi, if a second buyer returns a defective item to his seller without a court order, what is the ruling?
A) He can return it further to the first seller
B) He has accepted the defect and cannot return it further
C) He must seek a court order first
D) The seller must accept the return unconditionally

Key Takeaway
In a chain of sales, returning a defective item without a court order may cut off the right to further return if the sales are considered distinct, as per al-Kafi.

Returning Defective Currency and Prior Admission of Receipt
Analogical extension of defect return rules to currency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between zuyuf, nubahrja, and satuqa and apply the rule that a prior admission of receipt bars a later claim of defect.

Core Idea
Admitting receipt of 'dirhams' does not preclude claiming defect unless the admission specifies soundness; satuqa are not dirhams.

This passage extends the principles of returning defective items to currency transactions, a common issue in trade. Using qiyas (analogy) from rent to sales and debts, it establishes a key rule: if a person admits receiving dirhams, he cannot later claim they were defective if the admission was specific or if the coins are of a different genus. The text provides a detailed classification of dirham quality, which determines whether a claim is contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling from ijara
The passage starts with a scenario from rent: if the lessor admits receiving the rent, he cannot later claim the dirhams were defective. This is because the general term 'dirhams' in rent implies good dirhams.
The principle: no contradiction after a specific admission.
2
Extend via qiyas to sales and debts
Al-Tarsusi extends the same rule to all transactions where a price or debt is admitted. The 'illa is the contradiction between admitting receipt and claiming defect.
Qiyas is used because the cause is the same.
3
Distinguish types of defective dirhams
Zuyuf (slightly defective, accepted by some money-changers), nubahrja (more defective but more silver), and satuqa (mostly copper, not dirhams). The ruling differs: for zuyuf and nubahrja, the receiver's claim is accepted if no admission of soundness; for satuqa, the claim is always contradictory.
Satuqa are not of the same genus.
4
Apply the rule to a case
If a seller admits receiving 'dirhams' (general) and later claims they were zuyuf, his statement is accepted. If he admits receiving 'good dirhams', he cannot. If he claims satuqa after admitting dirhams, it is contradictory.
The admission's wording is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المبسوط : وإذا كان أجر الدار عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة موصوفة وأشهد المؤجر أنه قبض من المستأجر عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة ثم ادعى أن الدراهم نبهرجة وأن الطعام معيب فالقول قوله لأنه منكر استيفاء حقه فإن ما في الذمة يعرف بصفة ويختلف باختلاف الصفة فلا مناقضة في كلامه، قاسم الدراهم يتناول النبهرجة، واسم الحنطة يتناول المعيب وإن كان حين أشهد قال قبضت من أجر الدار عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة لم يصدق بعد ذلك على ادعاء العيب والزيف، وكذلك لو قال استوفيت أجر الدار ثم قال وجدته زيوفاً لم يصدق ببيئتة ولا غيرها لأنه قد سبق منه الإقرار بقبض الجياد فإن أجر الدار من الجياد فيكون هو مناقضاً في قوله وجدته زيوفاً، والمناقض لا قول له ولا تقبل بينته اهـ. كذا نقله الإمام الطرسوسي في أنفع الوسائل ثم قال: وإذا تقرر لنا هذا في الإجارة والأجرة عديناه إلى استيفاء الأثمان في البياعات والديون في المعاملات فإن العلة تجمع الكل فنقول إذا دفع إليه دراهم وهي ثمن متاع ثم جاء البائع وأراد أن يرد عليه شيئاً وأنكر المشتري فإن كان البائع أقر بقبض الثمن لم يقبل قوله ولا يلزم المشتري دفع عوضه، وينبغي أن البائع لو اختار تحليف المشتري أنه ما يعلم أن هذا من دراهمه يحلفه القاضي، فإن نكل يردها عليه، وإن كان البائع لم يقر بقبض الثمن أو الحق الذي على المشتري من جهة هذا البيع وإنما أقر بقبض دراهم مثلاً فالقول للبائع لأنه منكر استيفاء حقه، ولم يتقدم منه ما يناقض دعواه فيقبل قوله مع يمينه، وكذلك اليوم أيضاً. وهذا كله إذا كان الذي يرده زيوفاً أو نبهرجة، فإذا كان ستوقة لم يقبل قوله لأنه ناقض. كلامه لأن الستوقة ليس من جنس الدراهم وحاصل ما قالوه في تفسير ذلك أن الزيوف أجود الكل، وبعدها النبهرجة وبعدها الستوقة فيكون الزيوف بمنزلة الدراهم التي يقبلها بعض الصيارف دون بعض والنبهرجة ما يردها الصيارف وهي التي تسمى معيرة ولكن الفضة فيها أكثر، والستوقة بمنزلة الزغل وهي التي نحاسها أكثر من فضتها فالزيوف والنبهرجة يكون القول فيهما قول القابض إذا لم يقر باستيفاء الحق أو الأجرة أو الجياد بل يكون أقر بقبض كذا من الدراهم ثم يدعي أن بعضها زيوف أو نبهرجة فيقبل قوله ويردها وأما إذا قال إنها ستوقة بعد ما أقر بقبض الدراهم لا يقبل قوله ولا يردها اهـ. ما في أنفع الوسائل ملخصاً
It is mentioned in al-Zahiriyya, then he said afterward: Based on this, if a man receives dirhams owed to him from a man and pays them to his creditor, and the creditor finds them defective (zuyuf) and returns them to him without a court order, then he may return them to the first (debtor). He said in al-Mabsut: If the rent of a house is ten dirhams or a specified measure of wheat, and the lessor testifies that he received from the lessee ten dirhams or a measure of wheat, then claims that the dirhams are nubahrja or the food is defective, his statement is accepted because he is denying the receipt of his due; for what is in the dhimmah is known by description and varies by description, so there is no contradiction in his speech. The general term 'dirhams' includes nubahrja, and the term 'wheat' includes defective. But if at the time of testifying he said 'I received from the rent of the house ten dirhams or a measure of wheat,' he is not believed afterward in claiming defect or zayf. Similarly, if he said 'I collected the rent of the house' then said 'I found it defective,' he is not believed by evidence or otherwise, because he has previously admitted receiving good dirhams, for the rent of a house is from good dirhams; thus he is contradicting his statement 'I found it defective,' and a contradicting person has no say and his evidence is not accepted. Al-Tarsusi in Anfa' al-Wasa'il said: When this is established for rental and rent, we extend it to the receipt of prices in sales and debts in transactions, for the cause unites all. So we say: If dirhams are given as the price of goods, then the seller comes and wants to return something and the buyer denies, then if the seller admitted receipt of the price, his statement is not accepted and the buyer is not required to pay compensation. But the seller may choose to have the buyer swear that he does not know that this is from his dirhams; the judge administers the oath, and if he refuses, he returns them. If the seller did not admit receipt of the price or the right owed by the buyer from this sale, but merely admitted receipt of dirhams in general, the seller's statement is accepted because he is denying receipt of his due, and no prior contradiction exists; so his statement is accepted with his oath, and likewise today. All this if what he is returning is zuyuf or nubahrja; if it is satuqa, his statement is not accepted because he is contradicting. His speech: because satuqa is not of the genus of dirhams. The summary of what they said in explanation: zuyuf are the best of all, after them nubahrja, after them satuqa. Zuyuf are like dirhams accepted by some money-changers but not others; nubahrja are those rejected by money-changers, called 'standards' but with more silver; satuqa are like dross, with more copper than silver. For zuyuf and nubahrja, the statement is that of the receiver if he did not admit receipt of the due or the rent or good dirhams; but if he admitted receiving such dirhams and then claims some are zuyuf or nubahrja, his statement is accepted and he returns them. If he says they are satuqa after admitting receipt of dirhams, his statement is not accepted and he does not return them. End of summary from Anfa' al-Wasa'il.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage provides an analogical extension of the rules for returning defective items to the case of defective currency (dirhams). It begins with a ruling from al-Zahiriyya: if a person receives dirhams from a debtor and pays them to his own creditor, and the creditor finds them defective (zuyuf) and returns them without a court order, the original recipient can return them to his debtor. This is analogized from the earlier discussion on khiyar al-'ayb. Then a detailed exposition from al-Mabsut explains the principle of contradiction in admission. If a person admits receiving a specific quantity of dirhams or wheat as rent, he cannot later claim they were defective because his admission implies they were sound (since rent is typically paid in good dirhams). However, if he only admits receiving 'dirhams' without specifying quality, he can later claim they were defective because the general term includes both good and defective. This is based on the rule that a person cannot contradict his own admission. The passage then extends this to sales and debts via qiyas (analogy), unifying the 'illa (effective cause): the contradiction between admission of receipt and claim of defect. It concludes with a tripartite classification of dirham quality: zuyuf (slightly defective, accepted by some money-changers), nubahrja (more defective, rejected by money-changers but with more silver), and satuqa (mostly copper, not of the same genus as dirhams). For zuyuf and nubahrja, the receiver's claim is accepted if he did not admit receipt of good dirhams; for satuqa, the claim is never accepted because satuqa is not considered dirhams at all, so claiming it contradicts the admission of receiving dirhams.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Slightly defective dirhams that some money-changers accept; of lower quality than standard but still considered dirhams.
نبهرجة (nubahrja): Defective dirhams rejected by money-changers but with higher silver content; considered dirhams by name.
ستوقة (satuqa): Low-quality coins with more copper than silver, not considered dirhams; they are a different genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements is correct according to the passage?
A) Zuyuf are coins with more copper than silver.
B) Nubahrja are not considered dirhams at all.
C) Satuqa are not of the genus of dirhams.
D) All defective dirhams have the same ruling.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of returning defective currency depends on the wording of prior admission and the type of defect, with satuqa treated as a different genus.

Effect of Prior Judgment vs. Admission on Subsequent Buyers
Ruling on the effect of prior judgment vs. admission on subsequent buyers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effect of a court judgment from a party's admission regarding a defect in a sale, and apply the rule that a court judgment binds all subsequent parties while a prior buyer's admission does not.

Core Idea
A court judgment about a defect binds all; a buyer's admission binds only himself.

When a slave is sold and later found to have a defect like running away, what happens when the slave is resold? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the answer depends on whether the earlier defect was established by a court judgment or merely admitted by a prior buyer. The ruling hinges on the binding nature of judicial decisions versus personal admissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and pick out the two distinct situations: one with a court judgment (ḥukm bi raddihi) and one with a mere admission (iqrār) by the first buyer.
2
Note the outcome for each
In the first scenario (court judgment), the subsequent buyer can return the slave based on that judgment. In the second (admission), the admission does not bind the subsequent buyer.
3
Understand the exception for seller's admission of debt
Contrast the first buyer's admission of a defect with the original seller's admission of a debt on the slave. The latter does bind subsequent buyers, allowing them to return the slave.
Debt is a financial liability that attaches to the slave, unlike a physical defect.
4
Summarize the rule
A court judgment creates a legal status binding on all; a party's admission only affects that party unless it concerns a debt admitted by the original seller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن القن إذا حكم برده بعيب الإباق على بائعه فاشتراه آخر فأبق عنده فله الرد على بائعه بالاباق السابق المحكوم به كما في الظهيرية وإقرار المشتري الأول بإباقه لا ينفذ على من لم يشتر منه من الباعة بخلاف إقرار البائع الأول بدين على العبد فإن للمشتري الآخر أن يرده على بائعه بإقرار الأول كما فيها أيضاً.
Know that if the slave is judicially returned due to the defect of running away (i'q) to his seller, and then another buys him and he runs away while with him, he may return him to his seller based on the previous judicially-established running away, as in al-Zahiriyya. And the first buyer's admission of his running away does not bind those who did not buy from him among the sellers, unlike the first seller's admission of a debt on the slave, for then the subsequent buyer may return him to his seller based on the first seller's admission, as mentioned there as well.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding a slave who has a defect of running away (i'q). If a court has previously ordered the return of the slave to his seller due to this defect, and then another person buys the slave and the slave runs away again while in his possession, the subsequent buyer can return the slave to his seller (the one from whom he bought) based on the court's earlier ruling. However, if the first buyer (who returned the slave) merely admitted that the slave had the defect (without a court judgment), that admission does not affect subsequent buyers or sellers in the chain—it only binds him personally. In contrast, if the original seller admits that the slave owes a debt (i.e., the slave is burdened with a debt), that admission is effective against subsequent buyers, and the later buyer can return the slave based on that admission. The distinction is that the first buyer's admission about a physical defect is subjective and does not create a binding legal status, whereas the original seller's admission of a debt is a general declaration that can be relied upon by later parties.

Key Terms
إباق (i'q): The defect of being a runaway (slave), which gives the buyer the option of return.
حكم برده (hukm bi raddihi): A court judgment ordering the return of a defective item (here, the slave) to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The subsequent buyer can return the slave based on that admission
B) The admission has no effect on the subsequent buyer
C) The admission binds all sellers in the chain
D) The admission must be confirmed by a court to be effective

Key Takeaway
A court judgment about a defect binds all subsequent parties; a prior buyer's admission does not, unless it is the original seller's admission of a debt.

Agent's Liability in Returning Defective Goods
Rules for an agent (wakeel) returning a defective item on behalf of the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an agent's return of a defective item binds the principal and distinguish when the agent is personally liable.

Core Idea
An agent's admission of a defect binds only the agent; a court order or procedural nukul binds the principal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a key distinction in agency law: when a buyer returns a defective item to the agent, does the return affect the owner (principal) or the agent personally? The answer hinges on how the defect is established—whether by court order, nukul (refusal to swear), or the agent's own admission. Understanding this distinction prevents unintended liability and clarifies the limits of an agent's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Recognize the buyer, the agent (wakil bi al-bay'), and the principal (owner). The agent is authorized to sell, but his authority regarding returns is limited.
2
Distinguish direct seller from agent
Note that if a direct seller returns a defective item, it binds himself. For an agent, the effect may differ based on how the defect is proven.
The text uses an analogy from gift (hiba) return to illustrate.
3
Analyze the three modes of return
List the modes: (a) court order with evidence, (b) nukul (refusal to swear), (c) admission by the agent. Determine which bind the principal.
The absolute statement says all three bind, but this is qualified.
4
Apply the qualification by Fakhr al-Islam
Understand that for a non-recurring defect, admission does not bind the principal. For recurring defects, admission also does not bind; only court order or nukul binds.
The text distinguishes between defects that do and do not recur (yuhdathu mithluhu).
5
Summarize the relied-upon rule
The later authorities (al-Bazzaziyya, al-Wilwaljiyya) hold that an agent's admission never binds the principal without a court order. The agent becomes personally liable.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التهذيب للقلانسي : لو وهب وسلم ثم رجع فيه بقضاء أو رضا فله الرد .اهـ. ثم معنى قوله يرد على بائعه أن له أن يخاصم الأول ويفعل ما يجب أن يفعل عند قصد الرد ولا يكون الرد عليه رداً على بائعه بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع فإنه إذا رد عليه ما باعه بعيب بقضاء ببينة أو نكول أو بإقرار من المأمور بالبيع حيث يكون رداً على موكله من غير حاجة إلى خصومة لأن تعدادها عند تعدد البيع، وهنا البيع لا يحدث مثله، أما واحد فإذا ارتفع رجع إلى الموكل. وهذا الإطلاق قيده فخر الإسلام بعيب فيما يحدث مثله لا يرده بإقرار المأمور وإنما تعدى النكول إلى الموكل مع أنه إما إقرار أو بذل وليس له البذل لكونه ليس إقراراً ولا بذلاً حقيقة وإنما جرى مجراه بدليل أنه لو عاد وحلف بعد نكوله صح، ولو كان إقراراً لم يصح وصح القضاء بنكول المأذون عنها، ولو كان بذلاً حقيقة لم يصح فلا يلزم اجراؤه في كل الأحكام. وفي الإيضاح إن رد على الوكيل بعيب لا يحدث مثله بإقراره لا يرد وهو أوجه وفي البزازية والوكيل بالعيب رد عليه بعيب بلا قضاء اقتصر عليه وأن لا يحدث مثله في المدة هو الصحيح، وإن بقضاء ولا يحدث مثله في المدة ينظر جوابه والرد على الوكيل رد على الموكل مطلقاً، وإن يحدث مثله في المدة فإن بنكول أو ببينة فرد على الموكل، وإن بإقرار فعلى الوكيل وله أن يخاصم الموكل والوكيل بالشراء له أن يخاصم قبل الدفع إلى الموكل كالمضارب فإن برهن البائع على رضا الأمر أو أقر به الوكيل سقط الرد ولا يحلف الآمر على الرضا ولا وكيله ويرده الموكل بعد موت الوكيل بعيب، وإذا رده المشتري على الوكيل استرد الثمن منه إن كان نقده إليه إلا فمن الموكل اهـ. وفي مطلقاً، الولوالجية : إذا رد على الوكيل بإقراره بالعيب بلا قضاء لزمه دون الموكل هو الصحيح وظاهر ما في البزازية من الوكالة وهنا أن له أن يخاصم الموكل فليراجع. وقيد بخيار العيب
In al-Tahdhib of al-Qalanisi: If he gave as a gift and handed over, then retracted it by a court order or consent, he may return it. Then the meaning of his statement 'he returns it to his seller' is that he may litigate against the first (seller) and do what must be done with the intention of return, and the return upon him is not a return upon his seller, unlike the agent in sale; for if he returns what he sold due to a defect by a court order with evidence or refusal to swear, or by the admission of the person ordered to sell, it is a return upon his principal without need for litigation, because their number increases when the sale is multiple; here the sale does not create its like; for a single sale, when it is terminated, it reverts to the principal. This absolute statement was qualified by Fakhr al-Islam to a defect that does not recur; he does not return it by the admission of the agent. Rather, the refusal to swear (nukul) extends to the principal, even though it is either an admission or a concession, and the agent has no right to concede, because it is neither an admission nor a concession in reality, but only similar to them, evidenced by the fact that if he later swears after his refusal, it is valid; if it were an admission, it would not be valid; and the judgment by the refusal of one who is permitted (ma'dhun) is valid; if it were a concession in reality, it would not be valid, so it is not necessary to apply it in all rulings. In al-Idah: If he returns to the agent due to a defect that does not recur, by his admission, it is not returned, and it is more correct. In al-Bazzaziyya: The agent for a defect returns to him a defect without a court order, confined to it, and that it does not recur within the period; that is correct. If with a court order and it does not recur within the period, his answer is to be considered. The return to the agent is absolutely a return to the principal. If it recurs within the period, then if by refusal or evidence, the return is to the principal; if by admission, it is upon the agent, and he may litigate against the principal. The agent for purchase may litigate before handing over to the principal, like the mudarib. If the seller proves the principal's consent, or the agent admits it, the return is dropped. The principal is not sworn regarding consent, nor his agent. The principal returns after the agent's death due to a defect. If the buyer returns to the agent, he takes back the price from him if he had paid him, otherwise from the principal. In al-Wilwaljiyya: If he returns to the agent by his admission of the defect without a court order, it is binding on him, not the principal; that is correct. And the apparent meaning of what is in al-Bazzaziyya of agency and here is that he may litigate against the principal; so refer. And it is restricted to khiyar al-'ayb.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage deals with the rules governing an agent (wakeel) who sells goods and then the buyer wants to return them due to a defect. It distinguishes between the direct seller and the agent. If a direct seller (not an agent) returns a defective item, the return effects the seller himself. But for an agent, the return may or may not bind the principal (the owner) depending on the mode: if the return is based on a court order (with evidence or nukul), or if the defect does not recur, then the return binds the principal directly. However, if the agent admits the defect without a court order, the return binds the agent personally, not the principal, and the agent must then litigate against the principal if he wants to recover. The passage cites several authorities: al-Tahdhib, Fakhr al-Islam, al-Idah, al-Bazzaziyya, and al-Wilwaljiyya, showing a nuanced consensus with minor differences. The key principle is that an agent's admission does not bind the principal unless it is backed by a court ruling or the defect is such that it could not have arisen after the sale (i.e., it existed at the time of sale). The text ends by restricting this discussion specifically to khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect).

Key Terms
وكيل بالبيع (wakil bi al-bay'): An agent appointed to sell goods on behalf of the owner.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath in court, which is treated as equivalent to admission of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent admits a defect in the sold item without a court order, who bears the liability for the return?
A) The principal (owner)
B) The agent personally
C) The buyer loses the right to return
D) Both agent and principal jointly

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent's admission of a defect without court order renders the agent personally liable, not the principal; only a court judgment or procedural nukul can make the return bind the principal.

Types of Defective Dirhams and the Rule on the Recipient's Claim
Explaining three types of defective dirhams and the rule on accepting the recipient's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of defective dirhams (ziyūf, nubahrajah, satūqah) and apply the Hanafi rule on when the recipient's claim about defects is accepted.

Core Idea
Recipient's claim is accepted for hidden defects (ziyūf, nubahrajah) but not for obvious defects (satūqah).

In Hanafi fiqh, when someone receives dirhams in payment and later claims some are defective, the acceptance of that claim depends on the severity of the defect. Classical texts classify defective coins into three grades. Understanding this classification is crucial for resolving disputes in sales and debt repayment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three coin types
Learn the Arabic terms and English equivalents: ziyūf (mixed but mostly silver), nubahrajah (rejected by money-changers but still more silver), and satūqah (mostly copper).
Order: ziyūf is best, then nubahrajah, then satūqah.
2
Understand the legal basis
Satūqah is not considered part of the genus of dirhams because its copper content exceeds silver, making it obviously defective. Ziyūf and nubahrajah still have more silver and are not immediately obvious.
This distinction determines the rule.
3
Apply the acceptance rule
If the recipient acknowledges receiving dirhams and later claims some are ziyūf or nubahrajah, his word is accepted because the defect is hidden. He can return them. But if he claims satūqah, his word is not accepted because the defect would have been obvious at receipt.
The claim is only considered if he did not already acknowledge receipt of good ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه لأن الستوقة ليس من جنس الدراهم وحاصل ما قالوه في تفسير ذلك أن الزيوف أجود الكل، وبعدها النبهرجة وبعدها الستوقة فيكون الزيوف بمنزلة الدراهم التي يقبلها بعض الصيارف دون بعض والنبهرجة ما يردها الصيارف وهي التي تسمى معيرة ولكن الفضة فيها أكثر، والستوقة بمنزلة الزغل وهي التي نحاسها أكثر من فضتها فالزيوف والنبهرجة يكون القول فيهما قول القابض إذا لم يقر باستيفاء الحق أو الأجرة أو الجياد بل يكون أقر بقبض كذا من الدراهم ثم يدعي أن بعضها زيوف أو نبهرجة فيقبل قوله ويردها وأما إذا قال إنها ستوقة بعد ما أقر بقبض الدراهم لا يقبل قوله ولا يردها اهـ. ما في أنفع الوسائل ملخصاً
His words: because the ستوقة is not of the same kind as dirhams. The summary of what they said in its explanation is that الزيوف is the best of all, after it النبهرجة, and after it الستوقة. So الزيوف is like dirhams that some money-changers accept and some do not; النبهرجة is what money-changers reject, and it is called معيرة, but it has more silver; الستوقة is like زغل, which is coin whose copper content is more than its silver. So for الزيوف and النبهرجة, the word is the word of the recipient if he did not acknowledge receipt of the full right or payment or the good ones. Rather, he acknowledged receipt of a certain number of dirhams and then claims that some of them are زيوف or نبهرجة, then his word is accepted and he returns them. But if he says they are ستوقة after acknowledging receipt of dirhams, his word is not accepted, and he does not return them. End. From 'أنفع الوسائل' summarized.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage explains three categories of defective coins: الزيوف (mixed but mostly silver), النبهرجة (rejected by money-changers but still mostly silver), and الستوقة (mostly copper). The ruling is about when a person admits receiving dirhams but later claims some are defective. For زيوف and نبهرجة, his claim is accepted because these are acceptable to some extent and the defect is not immediately obvious. He can return them. But for ستوقة, after acknowledging receipt of dirhams, his claim is not accepted because ستوقة is so base that it would have been obviously defective at the time of receipt. This distinction protects transactions while allowing relief for hidden defects.

Key Terms
زيوف (ziyūf): Coins that are acceptable by some money-changers but not others; they contain a high percentage of silver.
نبهرجة (nubahrajah): Coins that are universally rejected by money-changers, but still have more silver than copper; called معيرة.
ستوقة (satūqah): Coins whose copper content exceeds silver, like زغل; they are of the lowest quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) For all three types
C) For زيوف and نبهرجة only
D) Only for نبهرجة

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the recipient's claim for hidden defects (ziyūf, nubahrajah) but not for obvious defects (satūqah) after acknowledging receipt.

Return of Defective Goods Through Agents: Court Order vs Confession
Legal expansion on return of defective goods through agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between when a return for defect binds the agent versus the principal, based on the mode of proof and the nature of the defect.

Core Idea
Return by confession binds only the agent; return by court order binds the principal, unless the defect cannot appear anew.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item, he returns it to his seller. But what if the seller was an agent? Does the return affect the principal who owns the goods? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on how agency affects the return, focusing on whether the defect was proven by court order (with evidence or refusal to swear) or by the agent's confession, and whether the defect could have arisen after the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize that the 'seller' could be the owner (principal) or an agent. The buyer returns to his immediate seller, but the legal effect differs.
The passage focuses on when the immediate seller is an agent.
2
Classify the mode of return
Determine whether the defect was established by court ruling (beyyinah or nukul) or by the agent's confession (iqrar). This is the key distinction.
Court order includes evidence or refusal to swear; confession is the agent's own admission.
3
Check the nature of the defect
Ask: Could this defect have appeared anew after the sale? If yes, the agent's confession may still bind the principal; if not, confession binds only the agent.
A defect that cannot appear anew (e.g., missing limb) must have existed at sale.
4
Apply the ruling
Use the rules: (1) Court order → return binds principal. (2) Confession + defect can appear anew → return binds principal? (3) Confession + defect cannot appear anew → return binds agent only.
There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi imams on some details; the relied-upon view is that confession binds only the agent when the defect cannot appear anew.
5
Note the practical consequences
Understand who bears liability: if the agent is bound, the buyer recovers the price from the agent; if the principal is bound, from the principal. Also, the agent may later litigate with the principal.
The passage includes details about the right to litigate and the effect of the principal's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معنى قوله يرد على بائعه أن له أن يخاصم الأول ويفعل ما يجب أن يفعل عند قصد الرد ولا يكون الرد عليه رداً على بائعه بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع فإنه إذا رد عليه ما باعه بعيب بقضاء ببينة أو نكول أو بإقرار من المأمور بالبيع حيث يكون رداً على موكله من غير حاجة إلى خصومة لأن تعدادها عند تعدد البيع، وهنا البيع لا يحدث مثله، أما واحد فإذا ارتفع رجع إلى الموكل. وهذا الإطلاق قيده فخر الإسلام بعيب فيما يحدث مثله لا يرده بإقرار المأمور وإنما تعدى النكول إلى الموكل مع أنه إما إقرار أو بذل وليس له البذل لكونه ليس إقراراً ولا بذلاً حقيقة وإنما جرى مجراه بدليل أنه لو عاد وحلف بعد نكوله صح، ولو كان إقراراً لم يصح وصح القضاء بنكول المأذون عنها، ولو كان بذلاً حقيقة لم يصح فلا يلزم اجراؤه في كل الأحكام. وفي الإيضاح إن رد على الوكيل بعيب لا يحدث مثله بإقراره لا يرد وهو أوجه وفي البزازية والوكيل بالعيب رد عليه بعيب بلا قضاء اقتصر عليه وأن لا يحدث مثله في المدة هو الصحيح، وإن بقضاء ولا يحدث مثله في المدة ينظر جوابه والرد على الوكيل رد على الموكل مطلقاً، وإن يحدث مثله في المدة فإن بنكول أو ببينة فرد على الموكل، وإن بإقرار فعلى الوكيل وله أن يخاصم الموكل والوكيل بالشراء له أن يخاصم قبل الدفع إلى الموكل كالمضارب فإن برهن البائع على رضا الأمر أو أقر به الوكيل سقط الرد ولا يحلف الآمر على الرضا ولا وكيله ويرده الموكل بعد موت الوكيل بعيب، وإذا رده المشتري على الوكيل استرد الثمن منه إن كان نقده إليه إلا فمن الموكل اهـ. وفي مطلقاً، الولوالجية : إذا رد على الوكيل بإقراره بالعيب بلا قضاء لزمه دون الموكل هو الصحيح وظاهر ما في البزازية من الوكالة وهنا أن له أن يخاصم الموكل فليراجع. وقيد بخيار العيب
Then the meaning of his statement 'he returns it to his seller' is that he may litigate with the first [seller] and do what must be done when intending return, and the return is not a return upon his seller unlike the agent in sale, because if [the agent] returns what he sold due to a defect by a court ruling with evidence or refusal to swear or by confession of the one ordered to sell, it becomes a return upon his principal without need for litigation, because [the sales] are multiple when there are multiple sales, and here the sale does not produce a similar [return]. As for a single [sale], if it is rescinded, it goes back to the principal. This absolute [ruling] was restricted by Fakhr al-Islam to a defect that can appear anew: he does not return it by confession of the agent, but refusal to swear extends to the principal even though it is either a confession or a payment, and he does not have the right to payment because it is not a confession nor a payment in reality; it merely takes their place, evidenced by the fact that if he later returns and swears after his refusal, it is valid, but if it were a confession it would not be valid. And the court ruling based on the refusal of the authorized woman is valid, and if it were a payment in reality it would not be valid, so it is not necessary to apply it in all rulings. In al-Idah: If he returns to the agent due to a defect that cannot appear anew by his confession, it is not returned, and that is more appropriate. In al-Bazzaziyyah: The agent by defect [meaning] he returned upon him due to a defect without a court ruling, that is restricted to [a defect] that cannot appear anew within the [warranty] period; that is correct. If by a court ruling and it cannot appear anew within the period, his answer is considered. And the return to the agent is a return to the principal absolutely. If it can appear anew within the period, then if by refusal to swear or by evidence, it is a return upon the principal; if by confession, it is upon the agent and he may litigate with the principal. The agent for purchase may litigate before delivery to the principal like the mudarib. If the seller proves the principal's consent or the agent confesses it, the return is dropped. The principal is not made to swear regarding consent, nor his agent. The principal returns it after the death of the agent due to a defect. If the buyer returns it to the agent, he recovers the price from him if he had paid him, otherwise from the principal. End. In [some works] absolutely, in al-Walwaljiyyah: If he returns to the agent by his confession of the defect without a court ruling, it binds the agent not the principal; that is correct. And the apparent meaning of what is in al-Bazzaziyyah from the agency and here is that he may litigate with the principal, so check. And he restricted [the chapter] to the option of defect.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that when a buyer returns a defective item, he returns it to his immediate seller. The commentary explains the situation where there are multiple sellers or agents. The key distinction is between a court-ordered return (with evidence or refusal to swear) and a return by confession. In the case of a sale by an agent, if the buyer returns the item due to a defect by court order (with evidence or refusal to swear by the agent), the return is considered to be upon the principal (the owner), not the agent. However, if the return is by the agent's own confession of the defect, then it binds only the agent, unless the defect is one that cannot appear anew (i.e., it must have existed at the time of sale), in which case the agent's confession does not bind the principal. The passage discusses various scenarios and cites authorities like Fakhr al-Islam, al-Idah, al-Bazzaziyyah, and al-Walwaljiyyah. The overall principle is that the return of defective goods is treated differently depending on the mode of proof and the nature of the defect.

Key Terms
بائعه (bā'i'ih): In this context, 'his seller' refers to the immediate seller from whom the buyer purchased, not necessarily the original owner.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath; treated as equivalent to confession or payment in certain contexts but not fully equivalent.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession or admission. In this context, the agent's admission of a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when an agent sells and the buyer returns the item due to a defect by the agent's confession (without a court ruling), and the defect is one that cannot appear anew, who does the return bind?
A) The principal only
B) The agent only
C) Both agent and principal
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The binding effect of a return for defect through an agent depends on how the defect is proven and whether it could have arisen after the sale; confession by the agent generally binds only the agent when the defect is pre-existing.

Buyer's Claim of Defect After Possession and Agent Rulings
Ruling with proof and expansion on agent liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer claiming a defect after possession, explain the procedural requirement of proof or oath, and apply the rules to cases involving an agent.

Core Idea
A buyer who claims a defect is not forced to pay until he proves the defect or the seller swears; returning to an agent without court order binds the agent alone.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario in sales: the buyer discovers a defect after taking possession. The Hanafi school protects the buyer from being forced to pay immediately, requiring either proof of the defect or the seller's oath. The author also refines the wording from al-Hidayah and extends the ruling to cases where an agent is involved in the return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the buyer claiming a defect after taking possession. The ruling is that he is not compelled to pay the price.
2
Understand the reasoning
The claim of defect denies the obligation to pay because the buyer's right is not fully determined. Also, if payment were ordered and the defect later appeared, the judgment would be overturned.
Analogy: judge should not rule without hearing both sides (hadith).
3
Distinguish author's wording from al-Hidayah
The author prefers 'but he must provide evidence or make his seller swear' over al-Hidayah's 'until his seller swears or he produces evidence'. The Hidayah wording could wrongly imply compulsion after evidence.
In Hanafi, proving the defect gives the buyer an option, not automatic rescission or compulsion to pay.
4
Apply to agent cases
If a buyer returns a defective item to an agent without a court order, it is treated like an iqalah (rescission) between buyer and agent, so it does not bind the principal. The agent is personally liable.
Exception: if the defect is of a type that cannot appear anew (old defect), some narrations differ, but the majority binds the agent.
5
Note the epistemic weight
The author's position is from zahir al-riwayah and aligns with al-'Inayah and Idah al-Ishtilah. The mufta bihi is that the buyer is not forced to pay, and after proof he has a choice.
The hadith 'Do not judge for one litigant without hearing the other' is cited as dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيباً لم يجبر على دفع الثمن ولكن يبرهن أو يحلف بائعه) أي لم يجبر المشتري على دفع الثمن بعد دعوى العيب لأنه أنكر وجوب دفع الثمن حيث أنكر تعين حقه بدعوى العيب ودفع الثمن أولاً ليتعين حقه بإزاء تعين المبيع، ولأنه لو قضى بالدفع فلعله يظهر العيب فينقض القضاء فلا يقضى به صوناً لقضائه وتعبير المصنف بـ«لكن أولى من تعبير الهداية بقوله «لم يجبر حتى يحلف بائعه أو يقيم بينة لما يلزم على ظاهرها فساد من وجهين : أحدهما أنه يقتضي أن المشتري إذا أقام بينة على ما ادعاه يجبر على دفع الثمن وليس كذلك. ثانيهما أنه يقتضي أن البائع إذا طلب منه الحلف يجبر المشتري وإن لم يحلف وليس كذلك، وإنما يجبر بعد الحلف. ولا يلزم شيء مما ذكرناه على عبارة الكتاب والمعنى ولكن الأمر لا يخلو من أحد شيئين : إما بينة المشتري فيتبين براءته بالرد على البائع، أو يمين البائع عند عجزه فيلزمه الدفع، ولكن بإقامة البيئة لا يتعين رد الثمن بل إما هو أورد المبيع كما في العناية لأن العيب إذا ثبت خير المشتري فلم يتعين الفسخ وأحسن الوجوه في تأويل الهداية أن معنى عدم الجبر عدم الحكم بشيء حتى يتبين الحال إما ببينة المشتري أو بيمين البائع. وفي إيضاح الاصطلاح إقامة المشتري بينة على دعواه غاية لتعين عدم الجبر كالتحليف لا لعدم الجبر حتى يلزم الجبر على دفع الثمن عند إقامة البيئة على العيب وإنما قلنا إنه غاية لتعيين عدم الجبر لاحتمال عدم قبول البينة فيجبر المشتري على دفع الثمن، ويحتمل أن تقبل فيبقى عدم الجبر كما كان ونظيره قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا تقض لأحد الخصمين حتى تسمع كلام الآخر» : فإن خوصم في عيب فقبل المبيع بغير قضاء لزم الوكيل ولا يلزم الموكل ولا يكون للوكيل أن يخاصم الموكل، فإن خاصمه وأقام البينة على أن هذا العيب كان عند الموكل لا تقبل بينته لأن الرد بالعيب بغير قضاء بمنزلة الإقالة فيجعل في حق الموكل كأن الوكيل اشتراه من المشتري. هذا إذا كان عيباً يحدث مثله فلو قديماً لا يحدث مثله ففي بعض روايات البيوع أنه يلزم الأمر، وفي عامة روايات البيوع والرهن والوكالة والمأذون أنه يلزم الوكيل دون الموكل وهو الصحيح وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو بكر
His statement: (If the buyer takes possession of the sold item and claims a defect, he is not compelled to pay the price, but he must provide evidence or make his seller swear.) That is, the buyer is not compelled to pay the price after claiming a defect because he denies the obligation to pay the price since he denies the determination of his right by the claim of defect, and paying the price first would determine his right in exchange for the determination of the sold item. And also, if he were ordered to pay, perhaps the defect would appear and the ruling would be overturned, so it is not ordered out of respect for the judgment. The author's expression with 'but' is better than the Hidayah's expression 'he is not compelled until his seller swears or he produces evidence' because on its face it implies corruption in two ways: first, it implies that if the buyer produces evidence on his claim, he is compelled to pay the price, which is not so. Second, it implies that if the seller asks him to swear, the buyer is compelled even if he does not swear, which is not so; rather, he is compelled after swearing. None of what we mentioned is necessary on the wording of the book and the meaning, but the matter is not free from one of two things: either the buyer's evidence, so his innocence is shown by returning to the seller, or the seller's oath when unable, so he must pay. But by producing evidence, the return of the price is not determined; rather, he may either return the price or the sold item as in al-'Inayah because when the defect is proven, the buyer has a choice, so rescission is not determined. The best way in interpreting al-Hidayah is that the meaning of 'not compelled' is that no ruling is given until the situation becomes clear, either by the buyer's evidence or the seller's oath. In Idah al-Ishtilah, the buyer's production of evidence on his claim is a limit for the determination of non-compulsion, like the request for oath, not for the non-compulsion itself, so that compulsion to pay the price would be necessary when evidence of the defect is produced. We only said it is a limit for the determination of non-compulsion because of the possibility that the evidence may not be accepted, and then the buyer is compelled to pay the price; and it may be accepted, so the non-compulsion remains as it was. Its analogy is his statement (peace be upon him): 'Do not judge for one of the two litigants until you hear the speech of the other.' For if he is litigated regarding a defect and he accepts the sold item without a court ruling, it binds the agent and does not bind the principal, and the agent has no right to litigate with the principal. If he litigates and produces evidence that this defect existed with the principal, his evidence is not accepted because the return due to a defect without a court ruling is akin to an Iqlah (mutual rescission), so it is made in relation to the principal as if the agent bought it from the buyer. This is if the defect can appear anew. If it is an old defect that cannot appear anew, then in some narrations of sales it binds the principal, but in the majority of narrations of sales, pledge, agency, and authorized [slave], it binds the agent, not the principal, and this is the correct view, and it is what Abu Bakr al-Faqih adopted.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a buyer, after taking possession of the purchased item, claims that it has a defect. The ruling is that he is not forced to pay the price immediately. The reasoning is that his claim of defect challenges the obligation to pay because the right to the item is not fully determined. Additionally, if a judge were to order payment and the defect later appears, the judgment would be overturned, so it is avoided. The author prefers the wording 'but he must provide evidence or make his seller swear' over the Hidayah's wording. He explains that the Hidayah's wording could be misinterpreted to imply that if the buyer provides evidence, he must pay, whereas in reality, proving the defect gives him the option to either keep the item with compensation or return it. Similarly, the seller's demand for an oath does not force the buyer to pay; payment is only compelled after the oath. The passage then continues with a related ruling: if a buyer returns a defective item to an agent without a court ruling (i.e., by mutual agreement), it is treated like an Iqlah (rescission) between the two, so it does not bind the principal. If the agent later tries to sue the principal, his evidence is not accepted because the transaction is seen as the agent buying from the buyer. This applies to defects that can appear anew. For defects that cannot appear anew, the majority view is that it still binds the agent, not the principal, and this is considered the correct position.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a contract by consent of both parties.
الرد بالعيب (ar-radd bil-'ayb): The return of defective goods by the buyer to the seller, exercising the option of defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the buyer not compelled to pay the price when he claims a defect after taking possession?
A) Because the seller is always at fault
B) Because the buyer's claim denies the obligation to pay and paying first might lead to conflicting judgments
C) Because the buyer can always refuse payment
D) Because defects are common in sales

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school protects the buyer from immediate payment when claiming a defect, and treats defect-return to an agent as a purchase by the agent from the buyer.

Procedure for Claim of Ibaq: Oath and Burden of Proof
States the rule on when seller is sworn in claim of ibaq or defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedural rule for when a seller is required to swear an oath regarding a claim of ibaq or defect, and identify the burden of proof.

Core Idea
In a claim of ibaq, the seller is not sworn until the buyer proves the escape occurred during his possession.

This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on the procedure when a buyer claims a defect (specifically ibaq, the escape of a slave) after taking possession. The key rule is that the burden of proof falls on the buyer to first show the defect existed in his possession before the seller can be required to take an oath. This ensures fairness and prevents frivolous claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The buyer claims that the slave escaped (ibaq) after taking possession. This is a specific defect claim.
2
Determine the burden of proof
The buyer must first prove that the escape occurred during his possession. This is his legal obligation before the seller is required to respond.
Proof typically requires evidence (bayyinah) such as two witnesses.
3
Apply the oath rule
If the buyer proves, then the seller must swear an oath: 'By Allah, it did not escape from you at all.' This oath denies the defect.
If the buyer cannot prove, the seller is not sworn and the claim is dismissed.
4
Note the pre-possession exception
Before possession, the buyer can rescind the sale unilaterally and does not need to prove a defect. After possession, the above procedure applies.
The passage notes this restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بائعه حتى يبرهن المشتري أنه أبق عنده فإن برهن حلف بالله ما أبق عندك قط والقول في قدر جبر حلف بائعه سماع كلام الآخر غاية لتعين عدم القضاء لا لعدم القضاء حتى يتعين القضاء لأحدهما عند سماع كلام الآخر .اهـ. وقيد بقبض المبيع لأن المشتري يستبد بالفسخ قبل القبض كما ذكرنا ولا ها هنا؛ كذا في المعراج وقد يقال إنه اتفاقي لأن للبائع المطالبة بالثمن قبل تسليم المبيع. فإذا طالبه به قبل قبضه فادعى عيباً لم يجبر فصدق عدم الجبر قبل القبض أيضاً. وفي الصغرى: إذا قال المشتري وجدت المبيع معيباً لا يجبر على أداء الثمن حتى يقيم البينة أو يحلفه، وكذا المديون إذا ادعى إيفاء الدين اهـ
His seller (is not sworn) until the buyer proves that it escaped while with him. If he proves, then (the seller) swears 'By Allah, it did not escape from you at all.' And the statement regarding the extent of compulsion? The hearing of the other's speech is a limit for determining non-judgment, not for non-judgment itself, so that judgment is determined for one of them upon hearing the other's speech. End. And it is restricted by taking possession of the sold item because the buyer has exclusive right to rescind before possession as we mentioned, and not here; thus in al-Mi'raj. And it may be said that it is agreed upon because the seller can demand the price before delivery. So if he demands it before his possession and the buyer claims a defect, he is not forced, so the truth of non-compulsion also before possession. And in al-Sughra: If the buyer says 'I found the sold item defective', he is not forced to pay the price until he provides evidence or swears him, and similarly the debtor if he claims repayment of the debt.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a commentary. It explains the procedure when a buyer claims a defect after purchase, specifically the claim of ibaq (escape of a slave). The core ruling: the seller is not required to swear an oath until the buyer first proves that the defect (escape) occurred while the item was in his possession. If the buyer proves this, then the seller swears 'by Allah, it did not escape from you at all.' The rule is restricted by possession: before possession, the buyer can rescind unilaterally, but after possession, he must prove the defect. The passage then compares to a general defect claim: if the buyer claims a defect, he is not forced to pay the price until he either proves the defect or swears. This is analogized to a debtor who claims he has repaid the debt.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): The act of a slave running away from his master; a specific defect that requires proof of occurrence during the buyer's possession.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, required to prove a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) After the buyer presents a medical report.
D) The seller never swears in ibaq claims.

Key Takeaway
The seller's oath in a claim of ibaq is only required after the buyer proves the escape occurred in his possession; otherwise, the seller is not sworn.

Buyer Claims Witnesses Far Away: Payment and Later Evidence
Explains rule when buyer claims witnesses are far; seller ordered to pay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer claims absent witnesses, explain the rationale, and identify the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on admitting later evidence.

Core Idea
A buyer who claims absent witnesses must pay now; later evidence overrides a prior oath per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a practical scenario in sales disputes: a buyer claims a defect but says his witnesses are far away (in Sham). How should the judge proceed to avoid harm to the seller while preserving the buyer's right to prove his claim? The Hanafi ruling requires the buyer to pay the price immediately, but he can later bring evidence and recover his money. We'll also explore an internal Hanafi disagreement about the effect of an earlier denial of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The buyer sues the seller for a defect but says his witnesses are absent from the city (e.g., in Sham). The seller admits the defect exists but claims it is old (pre-existing).
If the seller denies the defect entirely, a different rule applies (buyer must prove first).
2
Determine the Immediate Ruling
The judge orders the buyer to pay the price to the seller. Why? Because waiting for the witnesses would harm the seller (delay of payment), and payment does not harm the buyer because he still has his proof to present later.
If the buyer refuses to pay, the judge may compel him.
3
Analyze the Effect of Later Evidence
If the buyer later brings his witnesses, their testimony is accepted. This is not a case where the judgment is final because it is a claim of wealth, not a contract. The buyer can then recover the price.
This is distinct from cases where the judgment is effective both outwardly and inwardly (e.g., marriage contracts).
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
What if the buyer initially said 'I have no evidence' and the seller swore an oath? Then the buyer finds evidence. Abu Hanifa accepts the later evidence; Muhammad does not. The text notes this dispute without settling it.
The dispute is recorded in Fath al-Qadir; the relied-upon view may be Abu Hanifa's.
5
Extract the Legal Principle
The ruling balances two principles: (1) avoiding harm (darar) to the seller by delay, and (2) the strength of evidence (bayyinah) which can override a prior oath.
This principle reappears in other chapters like da'wa (claims).

قوله : (وإن قال شهودي بالشام دفع . لأن في الانتظار ضرراً بالبائع وليس في الدفع كبير ضرر به لأنه على حجته فإن نكل التزم العيب لأنه حجة منه وتحليف البائع في المسألتين إنما هو فيما إذا أقر بقيام العيب به ولكن أنكر قدمه لما سيأتي. والمراد بقوله شهودي بالشام أنه قال إن له بينة غائبة عن المصر سواء كانوا بالشام أو بغيرها، والشام بلاد من مسامة القبلة وسميت لذلك أو لأن قوماً من بني كنعان تشاءموا إليها أي ساروا أو سمي بشام بن نوح فإنه بالشين بالسريانية أو لأن أرضها شامات بيض وحرم وسود وعلى هذا لا يهمز وقد يذكر، وهو شامي وشآم وشآمي، وأشام أتاها، وتشأم انتسب إليها، وشامهم تشئيماً سيرهم إليها؛ كذا في القاموس. وقيد بدعواه غيبتهم عن المصر لأنه لو قال لي بينة حاضرة أمهله القاضي إلى المجلس الثاني إذ لا ضرر فيه على البائع، ولو طلب الإمهال إلى ثلاثة أيام أمهله وإذا حلف بائعه في مسألة الكتاب وقضى بالدفع عليه ثم وجد المشتري بينة فأقامها تقبل وليس هذا مما ينفذ فيه القضاء ظاهراً وباطناً عند أبي حنيفة لأن ذلك في العقود والفسوخ ولم يتناكرا العقد بل حقيقة الدعوى هنا دعوى مال على تقدير فالقضاء هنا بدفع الثمن إلى غاية حضور الشهود بالمسقط. ولا خلاف في مثله أعني ما إذا قال لي بينة غائبة أو قال ليس لي بينة حاضرة ثم أتى ببينة تقبل، وأما إذا قال لا بينة لي فحلف خصمه ثم أتى ببينة في أدب القاضي تقبل في قول أبو حنيفة، وعند محمد لا تقبل ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وستأتي بشعبها في كتاب الدعوى
His statement: 'And if he says: My witnesses are in Sham, he pays, because in waiting there is harm to the seller, and in payment there is no great harm to him because he is upon his proof. If he refuses, he is liable for the defect because it is a proof from him. And swearing the seller in the two cases is only when he admits the existence of the defect with him but denies its antiquity, as will come. And the meaning of his statement 'my witnesses are in Sham' is that he says he has evidence absent from the city, whether they are in Sham or elsewhere. Sham is a land... (etymology). And it is restricted by his claim of their absence from the city because if he said 'I have present evidence', the judge would postpone him to the second session since there is no harm to the seller. If he requests a postponement of three days, he is granted it. And if the seller swore in the case of the book and the judge ruled for payment, then the buyer finds evidence and presents it, it is accepted. And this is not among matters in which the judgment is effective apparently and actually according to Abu Hanifa because that is in contracts and rescissions, and they did not contradict the contract. Rather the claim here is a claim of wealth based on estimation, so the judgment here is for payment of the price until the arrival of the witnesses for dismissal. There is no disagreement in its like, meaning if he said 'I have absent evidence' or said 'I have no present evidence' then brings evidence, it is accepted. But if he said 'I have no evidence' so his opponent swore, then he brings evidence, according to the etiquette of the judge it is accepted in the view of Abu Hanifa, while according to Muhammad it is not accepted; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And it will come with its branches in the Book of Claims.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a buyer claims a defect but says his witnesses are in Sham (a distant land). To prevent harm to the seller from waiting indefinitely, the judge orders the buyer to pay the price despite his claim, because the buyer's inability to present evidence now does not invalidate his right to later prove the defect. If the buyer later brings witnesses, his evidence is accepted, and the seller must refund. However, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding when a buyer initially says he has no evidence: Abu Hanifa accepts later evidence, Muhammad does not. The passage also clarifies that the ruling applies only when the seller admits the defect but claims it is old; otherwise, the buyer must first prove the defect existed.

Key Terms
بينة غائبة (bayyinah gha'ibah): Evidence (witnesses) that are not present in the city; the buyer claims he has proof but cannot produce it immediately.
نكل (nakala): Refused to swear an oath; if the seller refuses, he is deemed liable for the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer is given unlimited time to bring them.
B) The seller must wait until the witnesses arrive.

D) The buyer's claim is rejected outright.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who claims absent witnesses must pay the price now but can later prove the defect; evidence overrides oath according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad disagrees if the buyer initially denied having evidence.
