When Is the Seller Sworn? Defect Claims and Oath
Harmonizes two defect/oath scenarios and analyzes the intra-Hanafi dispute on the need for proof before swearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural rule for swearing the seller in defect claims and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding the requirement of prior proof.

Core Idea
In defect claims, the seller is sworn only after proof—unless he admits the defect but denies its antiquity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses an apparent contradiction in Hanafi rulings on defect claims. On one hand, the seller is sworn immediately on a claim of defect; on the other, he is not sworn until the buyer proves the defect existed. The author harmonizes these scenarios and then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: does the buyer need to first establish the defect to make the seller a litigant, or does the claim itself suffice for swearing?

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting rulings
One ruling states the seller is sworn; another states he is not sworn until proof. Find both in the passage.
The first appears in the phrase 'تحليف البائع في المسألتين'; the second in 'لم يحلف بائعه حتى يبرهن المشتري'.
2
Understand the harmonization
The conflict is resolved by distinguishing two scenarios: when the seller admits the defect's existence but denies its newness (oath without proof), and when the seller denies the defect entirely (proof required).
Key phrase: 'محمل ما هنا ... على ما إذا أقر بقيام العيب ... وأنكر قدمه'.
3
State the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa requires proof to make the seller a litigant; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the claim alone is sufficient for oath.
The text explicitly contrasts 'عند الإمام' and 'عندهما'.
4
Analyze the reasoning of each side
Examine the dalil: Abu Hanifa cites that oath cuts off litigation and requires a valid claim against a litigant; the companions emphasize the validity of the claim itself.
Also note the analogy to debt claims and the response distinguishing defect as easily provable.
5
Evaluate the objection by Ibn al-Humam
Ibn al-Humam rejects the distinction between defect and debt, arguing both require the defendant to answer and proof from the claimant. The text sides with the earlier distinction.
This shows the critical refinement: Ibn al-Humam's objection is noted but not refuted decisively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن ادعى إباقاً لم يحلف بائعه حتى يبرهن المشتري أنه أبق عنده فإن برهن حلف بالله ما أبق عندك قط) أي إذا عيباً يطلع عليه الرجال ويمكن حدوثة فلا بد من إقامة البيئة أولاً على قيامه بالمبيع مع ادعی البلخي لأن الرد بغير قضاء في حق الموكل بمنزلة الإقالة، سواء كان العيب قديماً أو لا الخ. قوله : (وتحليف البائع في المسألتين) أي في هذه والتي قبلها ومراده دفع المنافاة بين قوله «يحلف بائعه» وبين قوله الآتي في دعوى الإباق لم يحلف بائعه حتى يبرهن المشتري الخ فإن ما يأتي من إفراد دعوى العيب وبيان الدفع أن محمل ما هنا من المسألتين على ما إذا أقر بقيام العيب عند المشتري ولكن أنكر قدمه فلا يحتاج إلى برهان المشتري على قيام العيب عنده نفسه، وما سيأتي من دعوى الإباق على ما إذا أنكر قيامه عند المشتري. واعترضه في النهر بأنه مما لا دليل في كلامه عليه قال : وقد ظهر لي أنه قطع النظر عن قدمه وحدوثه لينتصب البائع خصماً فإن لم يبرهن لا يمين له على البائع عند الإمام على الصحيح، وعندهما يحلف على نفي العلم لأن الدعوى معتبرة حتى تترتب عليها البينة فكذا يترتب التحليف . وله أن الحلف يترتب على دعوى صحيحة ولا تصح إلا من خصم ولا يصير خصماً فيه إلا بعد قيام العيب. وأورد عليه لزوم ذلك في دعوى الدين مع أن فراغ الذمة عن الدين أصل والشغل عارض كالعيب عارض وأجيب لو شرط إثباته لم يتوصل المدعي إلى إثبات حقه لأنه ربما تعذرت عليه بخلاف العيب لأنه مما يعرف بآثار تعاين أو بقول الأطباء أو القابلة ؛ كذا في المعراج والحاصل أنه لا يلزم من ترتب البينة ترتب اليمين فقد ذكر في القنية المواضع التي يكون الإنسان فيها خصماً بالبينة دون اليمين وكتبناها في الفوائد، ولأن التحليف إنما شرع لقطع الخصومة لا لأنشائها. ولو استحلف البائع فحلف نشأت خصومة أخرى في قدمه وحدوثه، وأورد الشارح على هذا التعليل مسألة الشفعة فإن المشتري إذا أنكر ملك الشفيع يحلف، فإذا حلف نشأت خصومة أخرى في الشراء والايراد على هذا التعليل لا يضر في صحة الدليل السابق مع كونه مردوداً من جهة أخرى أنه لا يضر أن تنشأ خصومة أخرى من اليمين وكثيراً ما يقع ذلك في الخصومات، ولم يظهر للمحقق ابن الهمام ما نقلناه عن المعراج من الفرق بين دعوى العيب ودعوى الدين فقال : إنه يلزمه الجواب للدعوى فيهما وعلى المدعي البرهان فيهما فالوجه التسوية بينهما في اليمين أيضاً فيحلف البائع كما هو قولهما. وقوله على قول البعض ولذا قالوا: إن القاضي يسأل البائع فإن أقر بقيامه توجهت الخصومة في القدم والحدوث وهو يدل على أنه يلزمه الجواب فالفرق بينهما غلط
His statement: 'And if he claims it escaped, his seller is not sworn until the buyer proves that it escaped while with him. If he proves, he swears: By Allah, it did not escape from you at all.' That is, if it is a defect that men can see and can occur, then evidence must first be established on its existence in the sold item with the claim of al-Balkhi? because the return without judgment regarding the principal is like rescission, whether the defect is old or not, etc. His statement: 'And swearing the seller in the two cases' means in this and the previous one, and his intent is to remove the contradiction between his statement 'he swears his seller' and his subsequent statement in the claim of ibaq: 'his seller is not sworn until the buyer proves...' For what will come regarding the claim of defect and the explanation of payment is that the interpretation of what is here in the two cases is when he admits the existence of the defect with the buyer but denies its antiquity, so there is no need for the buyer's proof of the existence of the defect with him. And what will come of the claim of ibaq is when he denies its existence with the buyer. And al-Nahr objected that there is no evidence in his statement for that, and said: It has become clear to me that he disregards its antiquity and newness to make the seller a litigant. If he does not prove, there is no oath for him against the seller according to the Imam on the correct view, while according to the two companions he swears on negation of knowledge because the claim is considered such that evidence is based on it, so similarly oath is based. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that oath is based on a valid claim, and it is not valid except from a litigant, and one does not become a litigant except after the establishment of the defect. And it was objected that this would apply to claims of debt, whereas freedom from liability is the original and obligation is an exception, like defect. And it was answered: If proof were required, the claimant would not be able to prove his right because it might be difficult for him, unlike defect because it is known by visible traces or by the statement of doctors or midwives; thus in al-Mi'raj. The conclusion: it does not follow from the ordering of evidence the ordering of oath. In al-Qunyah, it mentions the places where a person is a litigant by evidence without oath, and we have written them in the benefits. And because oath is only legislated to cut off litigation, not to establish it. If the seller is sworn and swears, another litigation arises regarding its antiquity and newness. The commentator objected to this reasoning with the case of pre-emption: if the buyer denies the ownership of the pre-emptor, he swears, and if he swears, another litigation arises regarding the purchase. The objection to this reasoning does not harm the validity of the previous proof, even though it is refutable from another angle: it does not harm that another litigation arises from the oath, and that often occurs in litigations. And the investigator Ibn al-Humam did not find clear what we transmitted from al-Mi'raj of the distinction between claim of defect and claim of debt, so he said: He is required to answer the claim in both, and the claimant must provide proof in both, so the appropriate is to equate them in oath as well, so the seller swears as per their (the two companions') statement. And his statement according to some: For this reason they said: The judge asks the seller; if he admits its existence, the litigation is directed to antiquity and newness, which indicates that he is required to answer, so the distinction between them is mistaken.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing an apparent contradiction in earlier rulings. The author harmonizes two seemingly conflicting rules: one says the seller is sworn (on defect claim), the other says he is not sworn until proof (on ibaq claim). The resolution: if the seller admits the defect exists but claims it is old (i.e., pre-existing), then the buyer does not need to prove the defect's existence; the seller can be sworn immediately on the issue of antiquity. But if the seller denies the defect entirely (as in ibaq), the buyer must first prove the defect existed during his possession. The passage then discusses a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) about whether the buyer needs to first establish the defect to make the seller a litigant. Abu Hanifa says yes, so no oath without proof; the companions say no, the claim itself suffices for oath. The text also examines the analogy to debt claims and concludes that the distinction stands because defect claims can be proven through visible signs or expert testimony, unlike debt claims which may be harder to prove. Finally, Ibn al-Humam criticizes this distinction, arguing that both types of claims should be treated the same.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): A litigant; a party against whom a valid claim has been made, requiring them to respond or be sworn.
نفي العلم (nafy al-'ilm): Swearing that one has no knowledge of the fact; used when the defendant cannot affirm or deny the claim with certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the harmonization in the text, in which scenario is the seller sworn without the buyer first proving the defect?
A) When the seller admits the defect exists but claims it is old.
B) When the seller denies the defect entirely.
C) When the buyer's witnesses are in Sham.
D) When the defect is ibaq (escape).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between a seller who admits the defect's existence and one who denies it, affecting whether the seller is sworn immediately or only after proof; and within the school, Abu Hanifa and his companions differ on whether prior proof is necessary at all.

Imam's Sale of Booty and Defect Claims
Ruling on Imam's sale of booty and defect procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the Imam's sale of booty and explain the special procedure for defect claims.

Core Idea
The Imam's sale of booty is a judicial ruling, not a commercial contract — so defect claims are handled by appointed litigants, not by return.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies an important exception: the Imam may validly sell war booty even before division in Dar al-Harb. When a buyer discovers a defect, he cannot return the property because the Imam's act is a hukm (judicial ruling). Instead, the Imam appoints a special agent to litigate the claim. We examine how this differs from ordinary agency and why the agent's admission of defect is not binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its normal restriction
Normally, selling booty before division in Dar al-Harb is invalid, but the Imam's sale is an exception. Identify the general rule and the exception.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on the Imam's sale
The Imam's sale is valid (sahih) even before division and in Dar al-Harb. This is established in al-Talkhīṣ and its commentary.
3
Understand the legal nature: a ruling (ḥukm), not a contract
Because the Imam's act is a judicial ruling, the buyer cannot return the item for defect. Instead, the Imam appoints a litigant (manṣūb) to handle the claim.
4
Analyze the role of the appointed agent (manṣūb al-Imām)
The manṣūb is not a regular agent: his admission of the defect is not accepted, no oath is upon him, and he only acts to prove the defect by evidence, similar to a guardian (father or trustee) for a minor.
5
Distinguish from ordinary agency
An ordinary agent’s admission is binding. The manṣūb al-Imām differs because his role is to litigate, not to represent the Imam in a private capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الإمام يصح بيعه للغنائم ولو في دار الحرب كما التلخيص وشرحه. وقولهم لا يصح بيعها قبل القسمة وفي دار الحرب محمول على غير الإمام وأمينه، فلو اطلع المشتري على عيب لا يرده على البائع لأن تصرفه حكم ولكن ينصب الإمام رجلاً للخصومة معه ولا يقبل إقراره بالعيب ولا يمين عليه لو أنكر وإنما هو خصم لإثباته بالبينة كالأب ووصيه في مال الصغير بخلاف الوكيل فإن إقراره مقبول فيه. وإذا أقر منصوب الإمام بالعيب انعزل كالوكيل بالخصومة إذا أقر على موكله في غير مجلس القضاء فإنه هي في موضوع هذه المسألة في عيب لا يشترط تكراره كالولادة فإن ادعاه المشتري ولا برهان له حلف بائعه. وقوله بعد «ولو ادعى إباقاً بيان لما يشترط تكرره وإلا كان الثاني حشواً فتدبره فإني لم أر من عرج عليه اهـ. قلت: وهذا التوفيق قد أشار إليه المؤلف بعينه بقوله فيما يأتي في الصفحة الثانية: وليس مراده خصوص عيب الإباق إلى آخره وهو ما أشار إليه هنا بقوله لما سيأتي ولكن كان عليه أن يقول وتحليف البائع في المسألة الآتية بدل قوله في المسألتين تأمل قوله: (لأنه قال أنها مما تطارحناه)
Then know that the Imam's sale of booty is valid even in the abode of war, as in al-Talkhis and its commentary. Their statement that its sale before division and in the abode of war is not valid is applied to other than the Imam and his trustee. So if the buyer discovers a defect, he does not return it to the seller because his action is a ruling, but the Imam appoints a man for litigation with him, and his admission of the defect is not accepted, nor is there an oath upon him if he denies; rather he is a litigant for establishing it by evidence, like the father and his trustee in the property of a minor, unlike the agent, for his admission is accepted. If the Imam's appointee admits the defect, he is removed, like the agent for litigation if he admits against his principal outside the court. The subject of this case is a defect that does not require repetition, like childbirth. If the buyer claims it without proof, his seller swears. And his statement later 'And if he claims ibaq' is an explanation of what requires repetition, otherwise the second would be redundant, so contemplate it, for I have not seen anyone who touched upon it. I said: This harmonization was indicated by the author himself in his statement in the second page: 'And his intent is not specifically the defect of ibaq...' which he pointed to here with his statement 'as will come'. But he should have said 'and swearing the seller in the subsequent case' instead of 'in the two cases'. Reflect on his statement: 'Because he said that it is among what we have discussed.'
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage addresses two related topics. First, the sale of war booty by the Imam (head of state) is valid even before division and in Dar al-Harb, contrary to the general prohibition on selling booty before division. If the buyer finds a defect, he cannot return it to the Imam because the Imam's act is a judicial ruling (hukm). Instead, the Imam appoints a person to handle litigation with the buyer. This appointee is like a guardian (father or trustee) in that his admission of a defect is not binding on the Imam, and he is not sworn; he is only a litigant to establish the defect by evidence. This differs from an ordinary agent, whose admission is accepted. Second, the passage returns to the topic of defect claims that do not require repetition (like childbirth) vs. those that do (like ibaq). The buyer's claim of such a non-repetitive defect without proof results in the seller being sworn. The author notes that the later statement about ibaq is meant to illustrate a defect that requires repetition, and clarifies that the earlier harmonization was not specifically about ibaq but about the distinction between two types of claims.

Key Terms
منصوب الإمام (mansub al-Imam): A person appointed by the Imam to handle litigation with a buyer who claims a defect in booty purchased from the Imam.
حكم (hukm): A ruling or judicial act; the Imam's sale is considered a ruling (not a private contract), so return for defect is not allowed directly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer can return it directly to the Imam.
B) The Imam appoints a person to litigate; the buyer must prove the defect by evidence.
C) The buyer's claim is automatically accepted.
D) The buyer cannot claim any defect because the sale is a ruling.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's sale of booty is a judicial act, so defect claims are litigated through a special appointee, not by direct return.

Oath on Certainty for Defect Claims: The Hanafi Rule and Its Proof
Rule for oath-taking in defect claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a seller swears on certainty (batat) when claiming knowledge of a defect's absence, and apply the analogy with depositary and agent to justify the rule.

Core Idea
Knowledge demands certainty: a seller who claims knowledge of soundness must swear on batat, not on mere ignorance.

When a buyer claims a hidden defect like ibaq, the seller must take an oath. The critical question is whether the oath is on certainty (batat) or on knowledge ('ilm). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rule: anyone who claims knowledge of another's act swears on certainty. We will examine the proof from analogy, the objection by al-Sarakhsi, and the resolution of two problematic cases from Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Determine what type of oath the seller must take when the buyer claims a defect like ibaq that existed before delivery.
The majority Hanafi position requires an oath on certainty (batat).
2
Understand the Proof: Analogy
The rule is proven by analogy (qiyas) with cases like the depositary (mudi') and the agent (wakil). They swear on certainty when claiming knowledge of another's act.
The common 'illa is the claim of knowledge of another's act.
3
Note the Objection: Al-Sarakhsi's View
Al-Sarakhsi argues that the oath is about the seller's own act (delivering soundly), not the slave's act of running away, so it should be an oath on knowledge.
This objection is rebutted by analyzing the phrase 'delivering soundly' as implying knowledge of the slave's history.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response
The phrase 'I delivered it while it had not stolen with me' indicates the oath is on the act of another (the slave's non-escape), thus batat is required.
The majority view is upheld by Fath al-Qadir.
5
Apply to Problematic Cases
Two cases from Muhammad al-Shaybani (partner sale with death/absence) appear to split the oath between certainty and knowledge. Fath al-Qadir resolves these by interpreting separate periods of possession.
This confirms the rule: the oath is on certainty when the seller has direct knowledge.

مسألتان : ادعى علماً بفعل غيره والتحليف في العلم والدليل على أنها قاعدة اعتبارها في مسائل أخرى منها ما في الخلاصة : لو قال إن لم يدخل فلان الدار اليوم فكذا ثم ادعى دخوله حلف على البتات بالله أنه دخلها. ومنها أن الوكيل إذا باع وادعى المشتري عيباً فإن الوكيل يحلف على نفي العلم والوصي لو باع وادعى المشتري عيباً يحلف على البتات لأنه في الأول لا يدعي علماً لكونه ليس في يده وهو في يد الوصي فيعلم And at the time of delivery, he is not fulfilling his oath if the defect existed in one of them as indicated in al-Mabsut. The safest and purest expression is that of al-Jami' and what follows it, as is not hidden. And it was criticized in al-Muhit regarding the expression of al-Jami' by the permissibility of the buyer's consent and his acquittal. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The reliance is on what is narrated from the second (Abu Yusuf): 'By Allah, you have no right of return against this buyer by the claim he makes' — an oath on the outcome. End. And in al-Mabsut, the expression of al-Jami' is deemed correct. And in al-Hidaya: If the claim is about the running away of an adult, he swears: 'By Allah, he did not run away since he reached the age of men' because running away in childhood does not necessitate his return after puberty. End. There is no specificity for running away; rather every case where the situation differs between childhood and adulthood, the ruling is the same as in Fath al-Qadir. And the oath here with his saying 'he never ran away' is an oath of certainty even though it is about the act of another. Some said that it is because he claims knowledge of it. And whoever claims knowledge of another's act swears on certainty, not on negation of knowledge, like the depositary if he claims that the depositor received the deposit, he swears on his receipt, which is an act of another, and the agent if he claims that the principal received the price of what he sold, the agent swears on the principal's receipt. And some said: Its essence is not the act of another but his own act, namely delivering it soundly, which is the saying of al-Sarakhsi. And the first is more appropriate, because the meaning of 'delivering it soundly' is not intended as soundness at the time of delivery, but rather 'I delivered it while it had not stolen with me' — thus it reverts to swearing on the act of another. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir. And Imam Zahir al-Din objected to the first, saying: This is not supported by two issues: One: Two men sold a slave to another in a single transaction, then one of them died and his heirs (were) the other seller, then the buyer claimed a defect — he swears on certainty regarding his own portion and on knowledge regarding the portion of the deceased according to Muhammad, even though he claims knowledge of its absence. The second: Two partners in an unlimited partnership (mufawada) sold a slave and one of them went away, then the buyer claimed a defect — the present partner swears on certainty regarding his own portion and on knowledge regarding the portion of the absent one even though he claims to have knowledge of that. Likewise in al-Mi'raj and in Fath al-Qadir: The correct view in my opinion is that our case should be objected to these two issues, not the opposite, because his swearing on certainty for half and on knowledge for the other half while he is one person is the problem. And the two issues are problematic due to the equality of his knowledge and ignorance regarding the two halves, unless the meaning of the issue is that the slave was with each of the partners for a period, so he swears on certainty for the period it was with him (that it did not run away with me) and on negation of knowledge for the period of his partner. If it was only with one partner, he only swears on certainty and it suffices, except that this is not known — so he swears as they mentioned. And if it was only with someone other than the one swearing, because the contract necessitated the description of soundness. End. I say: What he mentioned of the first view is not the correct view because the previous speech is about the effect of their saying 'whoever claims knowledge of another's act and the oath is required of him, then he swears on certainty' — so this rule is challenged by two issues: he claims knowledge of another's act and the oath is on knowledge. And the evidence that it is a rule is its consideration in other issues, including what is in al-Khulasa: If he said 'If so-and-so does not enter the house today, then (such and such)' and then claimed he entered, he swears on certainty by Allah that he entered it. And also if an agent sold and the buyer claimed a defect, the agent swears on negation of knowledge; and if a guardian sold and the buyer claimed a defect, the guardian swears on certainty because in the first he does not claim knowledge as it is not in his hand, while it is in the hand of the guardian so he knows its defect, as in al-Qunyah. Then know that the madhhab of Abu Yusuf is swearing on certainty in the two issues, and they are from the issues of al-Jami' al-Kabir as in al-Muhit from the chapter of adversary procedure in return for defect.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the ruling on oath-taking when a buyer claims a defect in the purchased item, particularly focusing on defects like running away (ibaq) that may have occurred before or after the buyer took possession. The author begins by clarifying the proper wording of the oath: the seller should swear on certainty (batat) that the defect did not exist at the time of delivery, not merely on negation of knowledge. This is because the seller is effectively claiming knowledge that the item was sound. The rule is derived from analogy (qiyas) with other cases, such as a depositary swearing on certainty regarding receipt of a deposit. A dispute arises: some scholars, like al-Sarakhsi, argue that the oath is about the seller's own act (delivering sound), but the majority hold that it is about the act of another (the item's freedom from defect). Imam Zahir al-Din raises two problematic cases from Muhammad al-Shaybani: when a slave is sold by two partners and one dies or goes absent, the buyer's oath is split—certainty for his own share, knowledge for the other share—which seems to contradict the general rule. The author of Fath al-Qadir resolves this by explaining that the two cases can be interpreted as each partner having possession for a separate period, allowing the oath to be based on direct knowledge for one's own period. The passage then cites supporting evidence from al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah, establishing that the rule is consistent across various scenarios. Finally, it notes that Abu Yusuf's position is to swear on certainty in the two contested issues, which is the relied-upon view in the madhhab.

Key Terms
تحليف (tahlif): Administering an oath to a party in a legal dispute; here specifically the seller's oath in a defect claim.
بتات (batat): An oath of certainty, affirming or denying a fact without qualification (e.g., 'he never ran away').
إباق (ibaq): The act of a slave running away from his master; a defect that may justify return if it existed at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, what type of oath does the seller take when the buyer claims a defect like ibaq that existed before delivery?
A) An oath on knowledge (ilm) that he does not know of the defect.
B) An oath on certainty (batat) that the defect did not exist at delivery.
C) An oath that the slave did not run away during the seller's possession.
D) An oath that he delivered the item soundly without specific details.

Key Takeaway
When a seller claims knowledge of the item's soundness, Hanafi law requires him to swear on certainty (batat), not on knowledge (ilm).

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Four Categories of Defects in Sales and Their Evidentiary Rules
Categorization of defects and their evidentiary rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four categories of defects in sales according to Hanafi law and state the evidentiary rules for each.

Core Idea
Defects in sales are categorized by how they are known: apparent, internal, women's matters, and experiential — each with unique evidentiary rules.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir outlines a systematic classification of defects (uyub) in sales, building on Qadikhan's compilation. Understanding these categories is essential for applying the correct procedure for returning a defective item (khiyar al-ayb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
The passage lists three new categories alongside a previously discussed one (defects known by experience). List them and note the examples given.
The first category is apparent defects that do not occur after sale; the second is internal defects known only by doctors; the third is defects known only by women; the fourth (implied) is defects like running away known by experience/testing.
2
Determine the method of proof for each category
For each category, identify what evidence is required to prove the defect: no oath for apparent, physicians for internal, female witnesses for women's matters, and experience/testing for the fourth.
Pay attention to the number of witnesses required: for internal, one doctor suffices; for women's matters, one woman for oath and two for return of non-occurring defects.
3
Note the exceptions and conditions
For each category, note when the seller can prevent return by claiming the buyer's consent, knowledge, or acquittal. Understand how oaths are administered.
For apparent defects, the seller's claim of consent requires the buyer to deny under oath. For internal and women's defects, similar procedures apply.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the number of women needed for returning a non-occurring defect under the third category.
The text mentions 'عندهما' (according to both) but then specifies two women for qarn, indicating a difference.

النساء كدعوى الرتق والقرن والعفل والثيابة وقد اشترى بشرط البكارة فعلى هذا إلا أنه إذا أنكر قيامه للحال أريت النساء والمرأة العادلة كافية، فإذا قالت ثيباً أو قرناء ردت عليه بقولها عندهما كما تقدم أو And in Fath al-Qadir: It has become apparent from what we have mentioned the manner of arranging the adversary procedure regarding the defect of running away and the like, which is every defect known only by experience and testing, like theft, bedwetting, madness, and adultery. And there remain other categories that Qadikhan mentioned, and with what we have mentioned, they complete four types: The first: that it is an apparent defect that does not usually occur at all from the time of sale to the time of litigation, like an extra finger, blindness, a missing (tooth), and a protruding tooth (i.e., extra). So the judge rules for the return if the buyer requests without any oath, because of certainty that it was in the hand of the seller or the buyer, unless the seller claims the buyer's consent, or knowledge at the time of purchase, or acquittal. If he claims that, he asks the buyer; if he confesses, return is prevented; if he denies, the seller brings evidence; if unable, he is made to swear that he did not know of the defect at the time of the sale or that he consented, etc. If he swears, he returns it; if he refuses the oath, return is prevented. The second: that he claims an internal defect known only by doctors, like pain of the liver or spleen. If he admits it before both (the judges), he returns it. Likewise if he denies and the buyer brings evidence or the seller swears and then refuses, except if he claims consent — we act as mentioned. And if he denies it before the buyer, he shows him to two Muslim upright doctors; one suffices, two is preferable. If they say it is so, he litigates about whether it existed with him. The third: that it is a defect known only by women, like claims of closure (ratq), hardness (qarn), flatness ('afl), and defloration (thyaba). If he bought with the condition of virginity, then the rule applies: if he denies its existence at present, women are shown to him, and one upright woman suffices. If she says she is deflowered or hard, it is returned upon her statement according to both (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) as mentioned, or if it is accompanied by his refusal to take an oath. However, for hardness and the like, which does not usually occur, return is made upon the statement of two women that she is hard, without a claim of litigation about it being before two just witnesses as will come shortly. (The author's comment on the phrase 'the third: that it is a defect known only by women'): I say in al-Khulasa: If the defect can be known by women's statements, if one woman from among those qualified for testimony informs of the defect's existence before possession, the buyer does not have the right of annulment by her statement, but her statement is accepted to impose an oath on the seller, so he swears as we mentioned. If after possession and an upright woman informs of the defect's existence, the litigation is valid and the seller swears on certainty (probably 'batat' not 'banat') that he sold and delivered while there was no such defect in it. End. Similarly in al-Manh, al-Zayla'i, and Jami' al-Fusulayn. It remains: if he came to know of this defect by intercourse, does he have the right of return or not? And consider what we have presented regarding the author's statement 'And whoever bought a garment and cut it...' etc. And this, it may be said: what is mentioned here contradicts what is in the texts from the Book of Testimony regarding the required number of witnesses: that the required number for matters known only by women is one woman. Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from Fath al-Qadir (a commentary on al-Hidayah) provides a systematic categorization of defects (uyub) in sales, as compiled by Qadikhan. It first restates the type already discussed (defects like running away known only by experience), then adds three further categories to make four: (1) Apparent defects unlikely to have arisen since sale (e.g., extra finger, blindness); these require no oath from the seller if the buyer claims them, because the defect is evident. The seller may still avoid return by proving the buyer's consent, knowledge, or acquittal. (2) Internal defects known only by physicians (e.g., liver/spleen pain); these require expert medical testimony, with one or two Muslim upright doctors, and if they confirm, the seller may be litigated. (3) Defects known only by women (e.g., virginity issues like ratq, qarn, thyaba); a single upright woman's testimony suffices for imposition of oath on the seller, and in some cases for direct return if the defect is non-occurring. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq adds a note from al-Khulasa clarifying the rule before and after possession: before possession, a woman's report only imposes an oath; after possession, it enables return. The passage ends with a query about defects discovered through intercourse and a reference to apparent inconsistency with the Book of Testimony's rule that one woman suffices for such matters, but the answer is cut off.

Key Terms
عيب (ayb): A defect in the sold item that existed at the time of sale, giving the buyer the option of return (khiyar al-ayb).
رتق (ratq): A physical condition in a female where the vaginal opening is closed, a defect that prevents intercourse.
قرن (qarn): A hardness or obstruction in the vagina, another type of defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the categorization in Fath al-Qadir, what is the rule for an internal defect like liver disease known only by doctors?
A) The buyer must bring a female witness.
B) The buyer must show the item to two Muslim upright physicians.
C) The judge rules for return without any oath.
D) The seller swears on certainty immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies defects by their discoverability, and each category has precise evidentiary rules that the judge must follow to ensure justice.

Types of Defects and the Dispute over Women's Testimony in Khiyar al-'Ayb
Explanatory expansion (sharh) on the four defect types and internal Hanafi debate on evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of defects in Hanafi law and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the evidentiary weight of women’s testimony for returning a defective item.

Core Idea
For defects only women can detect, one woman initiates litigation, but two women are needed for return if the defect is static.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). It classifies defects into four types and details the procedures for the buyer's return right. A central issue is the proof of hidden defects that only women can verify, where Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differed on the number of female witnesses required. Understanding this classification and the resolution of the dispute is essential for correctly applying the rules of return in sales contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four defect categories
The passage divides defects into: (1) apparent defects that cannot arise after sale (e.g., extra finger), (2) hidden defects only known to doctors, (3) defects only detectable by women (e.g., virginity), and (4) defects requiring experience (e.g., running away). Understand the basis for each category.
This classification determines the evidence needed for the buyer to enforce the return.
2
Analyze the ruling for apparent, non-developing defects
For type 1, the judge orders return without an oath because the defect's existence is certain, unless the seller claims the buyer consented or knew. Understand the procedure when the seller raises such claims.
The certainty (yaqeen) eliminates the need for an oath.
3
Understand the ruling for doctor-known defects
For type 2, the buyer must present the item to two righteous Muslim physicians (one suffices) to establish the defect. If confirmed, litigation proceeds; the seller may be sworn.
The testimony of doctors is accepted because men can witness the examination.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on women's testimony
For type 3, the key dispute: Abu Yusuf (initial view) held that before delivery, one woman's testimony suffices for return without oath; Muhammad required the seller's oath. Later, Abu Yusuf agreed with Muhammad for defects that can arise. The settled view distinguishes: for static defects, two women are required for return; for defects that can develop (like thiyaba), one woman initiates the case, and return follows the seller's refusal to swear.
This is the core of the passage's legal reasoning.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position to a scenario
Given a buyer who claims a slave girl is non-virgin (a defect that can arise) after delivery, apply the rule: one woman's testimony initiates litigation, the seller is sworn, and if he refuses (nukūl), the girl is returned without two women. Contrast with a static defect like qarn where two women are needed for return.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is the number of witnesses needed for the actual return.

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب
غيره والتحليف في العلم والدليل على أنها قاعدة اعتبارها في مسائل أخرى منها ما في الخلاصة : لو قال إن لم يدخل فلان الدار اليوم فكذا ثم ادعى دخوله حلف على البتات بالله أنه دخلها. ومنها أن الوكيل إذا باع وادعى المشتري عيباً فإن الوكيل يحلف على نفي العلم والوصي لو باع وادعى المشتري عيباً يحلف على البتات لأنه في الأول لا يدعي علماً لكونه ليس في يده وهو في يد الوصي فيعلم عيبه كما في القنية. ثم اعلم أن مذهب أبي يوسف التحليف على البتات في المسالتين وهما من مسائل الجامع الكبير كما في المحيط من باب المخاصمة في الرد بالعيب. وفي فتح القدير وقد ظهر بما ذكرنا كيفية ترتيب الخصومة في عيب الإباق ونحوه وهو كل عيب لا يعرف إلا بالتجربة والاختبار كالسرقة والبول في الفراش والجنون والزنا، وبقي أصناف أخرى ذكرها قاضيخان وهي مع ما ذكرنا تتمة أربعة أنواع : الأول أن يكون ظاهراً لا يحدث مثله أصلاً من وقت البيع إلى وقت الخصومة كالأصبح الزائدة والعمى والناقصة والسن الشاغية أي الزائدة فالقاضي يقضي فيها بالرد إذا طلب المشتري من غير تحليف للتيقن به في يد البائع أو المشتري إلا أن يدعي البائع رضاه به أو العلم به عند الشراء والإبراء منه فإن ادعاه سأل المشتري فإن اعترف امتنع الرد، وإن أنكر أقام البينة عليه، فإن عجز يستحلف ما علم به وقت المبيع أو ما رضي به ونحوه، فإن حلف رده وإن نكل امتنع الرد الثاني أن يدعي عيباً باطناً لا يعرفه إلا الأطباء كوجع الكبد والطحال، فإن اعترف به عندهما رده، وكذا إذا أنكره فأقام المشتري البينة أو حلف البائع فنكل إلا إن ادعى الرضا فيعمل ما ذكرنا وإن أنكره عند المشتري يريه طبيبين مسلمين عدلين والواحد يكفي والاثنان أحوط فإذا قال به ذلك يخاصمه في أنه كان عنده. الثالث أن يكون عيباً لا يطلع عليه إلا النساء كدعوى الرتق والقرن والعفل والثيابة وقد اشترى بشرط البكارة فعلى هذا إلا أنه إذا أنكر قيامه للحال أريت النساء والمرأة العادلة كافية، فإذا قالت ثيباً أو قرناء ردت عليه بقولها عندهما كما تقدم أو إذا انضم إليه نكوله عند تحليفه غير أن القرن ونحوه إن كان مما لا يحدث مثله ترد عند قول المرأتين هي قرناء بلا خصومة في أن ذلك عند عدلين كما سيأتي قريباً قوله: الثالث أن يكون عيباً لا يطلع عليه إلا النساء الخ) أقول في الخلاصة : وإن كان العيب يتوصل إليه بقول النساء إن أخبرت امرأة واحدة من أهل الشهادة بوجود العيب إن كان قبل القبض ليس للمشتري حق الفسخ بقولها لكن يقبل قولها لإيجاب اليمين على البائع فيحلف كما ذكرنا، وإن كان بعد القبض وأخبرت امرأة عدلة بوجود العيب صحت الخصومة ويحلف البائع على البنات لقد باع وسلم وما بها هذا العيب .اهـ. ونحوه في المنح والزيلعي وجامع الفصولين. بقي لو علم بهذا العيب بالوطء هل له الرد أم لا وانظر ما قدمناه عند قول المصنف «ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه الخ. هذا وقد يقال : إن ما ذكر هنا يخالف ما في المتون من كتاب الشهادة من قولهم في نصاب الشهادة أن نصابها فيما لا يطلع عليه إلا النساء امرأة واحدة إلا أن يجاب بأن المراد

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيد
البائع للتيقن بذلك كما في الأصبح الزائدة إلا أن يدعي رضا فعلى ما ذكرنا. وفي شرح قاضيخان : العيب إذا كان مشاهداً وهو مما لا يحدث يؤمر بالرد، وإن كان مما يحدث واختلف في حدوثه فالبينة للمشتري لأنه يثبت الخيار والقول للبائع لأنه ينكر الخيار وهذا يعرف مما قدمناه. ولو اشترى جارية وادعى أنها خنثى يحلف البائع لأنه لا ينظر إليه الرجال ولا النساء إلى هنا ما في فتح القدير تبعاً لما في المعراج وفيه : ولو أراد المشتري الرد ولم يدع البائع عليه شيئاً يسقطه لم يحلف المشتري لأن التحليف لقطع الخصومة وفيه إنشاؤها. وعند أبي يوسف يحلف صيانة لقضائه عن النقض لو ظهر ذلك في ثاني الحال بالله ما علم بالعيب حين اشتراه ولا رضي به ولا عرضه على البيع، وأكثر القضاة يحلفون بالله ما سقط حقك في الرد بالعيب من الوجه الذي يدعيه نصاً ولا دلالة وهو الصحيح، وأحب إلي أن يستحلفه وإن لم يدع ، ولو ادعى سقوط حق الرد يحلف اتفاقاً .اهـ. وقدمنا أن خيار العيب على التراخي ولو خاصم ثم ترك ثم عاد . وخاصم فله الرد كما في المعراج أيضاً. وذكر في الخلاصة والبزازية أن القاضي لا يستحلف الخصم بدون طلب المدعي إلا في مسائل منها : خيار العيب وقد ذكرناه الثانية النفقة في مال الغائب لا يقضي بها حتى يستحلف المرأة الثالثة الشفعة لا يقضي بها حتى يستحلف الشفيع وكتبناها في الفوائد الفقهية مفصلة . ثم اعلم أن القاضي إنما يحتاج إلى قول الأطباء عند عدم علمه بالعيب، أما إذا كان من ذوي المعرفة نظر بنفسه كما في البزازية ونظر أمين القاضي كهو كما في البدائع واشتراط العدلين منهم إنما هو للرد وإن أخبر واحد عدل توجهت الخصومة فيحلف البائع كما فيها أيضاً ولكن في أدب القاضي
أن المرأة تكفي لا لأجل إثبات العيب والرد به بل لأجل توجه الخصومة على البائع، أو يحمل على ما قبل القبض كما يفيده ما في الخانية حيث قال : وفيما لا ينظره الرجال كالقرن والرتق ونحوه اختلفت فيه الروايات وآخر ما روي عن محمد إن كان ذلك قبل القبض وهو عيب لا يحدث يرد بشهادة النساء وهو قول أبي يوسف الأخير، والحبل يثبت بقول النساء في حق الخصومة ولا يرد بشهادتين اهـ. وكأنه احترز بقوله لا يحدث عن نحو الحبل، وبه علم أن ما مر عن الخلاصة وغيرها من عدم الفسخ قبل القبض قول أبي يوسف الأول والعمل على المتأخر. وعلى هذا فقول المؤلف ردت عليه بقولهما محمول أيضاً على ما قبل القبض بدليل ما في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان حيث قال : إن كان بعد القبض لا يرد بشهادة النساء بالاتفاق لكن يخلف البائع، فإن حلف لا يرد، وإن نكل ترد عليه بنكوله، وإن كان قبل القبض ذكر الخصاف أن على قول أبي يوسف ترد من غير يمين البائع . وقال محمد: لا ترد حتى يحلف البائع. وعن محمد في النوادر ؛ شهادة النساء فيما لا يطلع عليه الرجال حجة للرد وإن كان بعد القبض .اهـ. وفي مجموعة صمتي أفندي عن نقد الفتاوى : ما لا ينظر إليه الرجال كالقرن والرتق إذا أخبرت امرأة واحدة به يثبت العيب في حق الخصومة لا في الرد في ظاهر الرواية اهـ. ومثله في الخانية قوله : (ولكن في أدب القاضي ما يخالفه) قال في البزازية وفي
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. ... And know that the madhhab of Abu Yusuf is that the oath is on certainty (bil-batat) in the two cases, and they are from the issues of al-Jami' al-Kabir. In Fath al-Qadir: It has become clear from what we mentioned the manner of ordering the litigation concerning the defect of running away and the like, and it is every defect that is not known except through experience and testing, like theft, bedwetting, madness, and adultery. There remain other categories mentioned by Qadikhan, and with what we have mentioned they are four complete types: First: that it is apparent, and the like does not occur at all from the time of sale to the time of litigation, like an extra finger, blindness, missing [tooth?], and the soughing tooth (i.e., extra). So the judge rules for return if the buyer requests it without an oath due to certainty that it was in the hand of the seller or buyer, unless the seller claims his consent, or knowledge thereof at purchase, or discharge from it. If he claims it, he asks the buyer; if he admits, return is prevented; if he denies, he (the seller) brings evidence upon him; if he is unable, he (the buyer) is sworn: 'I did not know of it at the time of purchase, nor consented to it' and the like. If he swears, he returns it; if he refuses, return is prevented. Second: that he claims a hidden defect that only doctors know, like pain of the liver and spleen. If he admits it in their presence, he returns it. Likewise, if he denies it and the buyer brings evidence, or the seller swears and then refuses, except if he claims consent, then act according to what we have mentioned. If he denies it and the buyer shows it to two righteous Muslim doctors (and one suffices, but two is more precautionary), and if they give an opinion, then he litigates that it was with him. Third: that it is a defect only women can see, like the claim of being sealed (ratq), horn-like obstruction (qarn), hanging piece ('afl), and loss of virginity (thiyaba), and he purchased stipulating virginity. So according to this, if he denies its existence at present, women are shown and a righteous woman suffices. If she says 'non-virgin' or 'horned' it is returned upon her statement in his (Abu Yusuf's) view as previously, or if joined by his refusal to swear. However, the qarn and the like, if it is something whose like does not occur, she is returned upon the statement of two women that she is horned, without litigation that it existed with two righteous [witnesses] as will soon come. ... And know that the judge only needs the doctors' statement when he does not know the defect. But if he is among those with knowledge, he looks himself as in al-Bazzaziyya. And the opinion of the judge's trustee is like his own. ...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). It begins by establishing a general principle: when a buyer claims a defect, the seller may be required to swear an oath. The text then details the views of Abu Yusuf, who mandates a decisive oath (bil-batat) in cases where the seller does not have direct knowledge of the defect (e.g., a proxy seller). The passage then moves to the classification of defects into four types, as mentioned by Qadikhan, to determine the procedure for return. The first type is an obvious defect that cannot have arisen after the sale, such as an extra finger or blindness. In such cases, the judge rules for return without an oath, unless the seller claims the buyer was aware or consented. The second type is a hidden defect only known to doctors, requiring testimony from two righteous Muslim physicians (one suffices) to establish the defect. The third type is a defect only detectable by women, such as virginity issues. Here, the text expounds a dispute within the Hanafi school: according to Abu Yusuf (in one view), the testimony of a single righteous woman suffices for the return if the defect cannot have arisen later. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, holding that two women are required for return, but one woman can initiate the litigation and trigger an oath for the seller. The passage clarifies that the later view of Abu Yusuf aligns with Muhammad's position for defects that cannot develop. The text also discusses scenarios before and after delivery (qabd), and notes that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the latter view. Finally, the passage mentions that judges should not administer oaths without a request from the plaintiff, except in a few cases including the option of defect, maintenance for an absent husband, and pre-emption (shuf'a).

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyār al-‘ayb): The buyer's right to cancel the sale if a defect is found in the item at the time of delivery.
البتات (al-batāt): A decisive oath where the swearer affirms with certainty, e.g., 'by Allah, I did not know of the defect'.
الرتق (al-ratq): A defect in a woman where the vaginal opening is sealed, preventing intercourse.
التحليف (al-taḥlīf): Administering an oath to a party to establish or rebut a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a buyer claims a defect that only women can detect (e.g., non-virginity) after delivery, how many women's testimonies are required for a return?
A) One woman suffices for return
B) Two women are required for return
C) One woman suffices only to start the case; the seller is sworn; if he refuses, return occurs without two women
D) No women's testimony is accepted after delivery

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires two women for return on static defects like qarn, but for defects that can develop, one woman initiates the case, and return is triggered by the seller's refusal to swear.

Burden of Proof in Option of Defect: Apparent vs. Arising Defects
Establishing the rule for defects that are apparent vs. arising after purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects that are apparent and non-arising versus those that could arise, and state the rule for who bears the burden of proof in khiyar al-'ayb.

Core Idea
For apparent, non-arising defects, the seller must accept return; for arising defects, the buyer must prove.

This passage from Sharh Qadikhan presents a crucial distinction in the option of defect. The burden of proof is not absolute; it shifts based on the nature of the defect. Understanding this distinction prevents both buyer and seller from being unfairly treated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of defects
The text divides defects into: (1) Those that are apparent (mushahad) and cannot arise after the sale, e.g., a missing limb; (2) Those that could arise after the sale, e.g., a disease that could develop later.
The classification depends on the nature of the defect itself.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For category (1), the judge orders the seller to accept the return without requiring the buyer to provide proof. For category (2), if the buyer claims the defect existed at sale and the seller disputes, the burden of proof is on the buyer.
The ruling is procedural: who must prove?
3
Explain the reasoning
For category (1), the defect is obvious and cannot be attributed to the buyer, so the seller's refusal is unjustified. For category (2), the buyer is asserting a claim (establishing the option), so he must bring evidence (bayyinah). The seller's denial is taken as true (al-qawl li'l-ba'i').
This follows the general Hanafi principle: al-bayyinah 'ala man idda'a wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara.
4
Connect to the general Hanafi rule
The claimant must prove and the defendant's denial is accepted under oath. Here, the buyer is the claimant for the option; the seller is the defendant denying the option.
This rule is applied across many chapters of fiqh.

Source Text

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب
البائع للتيقن بذلك كما في الأصبح الزائدة إلا أن يدعي رضا فعلى ما ذكرنا. وفي شرح قاضيخان : العيب إذا كان مشاهداً وهو مما لا يحدث يؤمر بالرد، وإن كان مما يحدث واختلف في حدوثه فالبينة للمشتري لأنه يثبت الخيار والقول للبائع لأنه ينكر الخيار وهذا يعرف مما قدمناه.
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. The seller [is required to accept return] for certainty, as in al-Asbah al-Za'ida, unless he claims consent, then [the ruling] is as we mentioned. In Sharh Qadikhan: If the defect is apparent and it is something that does not arise [after purchase], he is ordered to return. And if it is something that arises and they differ about its occurrence, then the burden of proof is on the buyer because he is establishing the option, and the say is the seller's because he denies the option. And this is known from what we have presented.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is a ruling on khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) from the commentary of Qadikhan. The passage distinguishes two types of defects: those that are apparent and cannot arise after the sale (e.g., a missing limb), and those that could arise (e.g., a disease). For the first type, the judge orders the seller to accept the return without requiring proof from the buyer, because the defect is obvious and cannot be attributed to the buyer. For the second type, if the buyer claims the defect existed at the time of sale but the seller disputes it, the burden of proof (bayyinah) lies on the buyer because he is asserting the existence of the option. The seller's statement is taken (al-qawl li'l-ba'i') because he is denying the option. This follows the general Hanafi rule that the claimant must prove and the defendant's denial is accepted under oath.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return the purchased item due to a defect that existed at the time of sale.
بينة (bayyinah): Proof or evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females in fiqh contexts.
مشاهد (mushahad): An apparent defect that is visible and cannot be hidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In khiyar al-'ayb, if the defect is of a type that does not arise after the sale, and the buyer wants to return, what happens?
A) The buyer must bring two witnesses to prove the defect existed at sale.
B) The seller is ordered to accept the return without buyer proof.
C) The buyer takes an oath that the defect existed.
D) The seller may refuse and the buyer loses the option.

Key Takeaway
The burden of proof in khiyar al-'ayb depends on whether the defect could have arisen after purchase; for obvious, non-arising defects, the seller must accept return without buyer proof.
